Hadd and Rasm

8 min
Objections to the Definition of 'Ilm and Their Responses
Raises and answers two objections to a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two main objections to the definition of 'ilm (concerning particulars and divine knowledge) and explain the responses.

Core Idea
Definitions of 'ilm must account for particulars and divine knowledge.

After presenting a refined definition of 'ilm as 'the disclosure of reality as it is', the commentator anticipates two objections: (1) that this definition excludes particular conceptions, since particulars are apprehended by the particular faculties, not the intellect; (2) that it excludes God's knowledge, since God is not an intellect. The responses clarify that the intellect does apprehend particulars with mediation, and that the definition only applies to acquired knowledge, not divine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Read: 'فإن قيل إن هذا يستلزم خروج التصورات الجزئية عن المحدود' – the concern is that particular concepts (like Zayd's form) are not obtained in the intellect, so the definition would exclude them.
2
Analyze the response to the first objection
The response argues that 'the intellect does not apprehend particulars' means it does not do so without an intermediary; with mediation (through the senses or imagination), it does. So all things are depicted in the intellect, some directly, some indirectly.
This relies on the theory of internal senses.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection: 'خروج بعض أفراد المعرف عن التعريف وهو علم الله تعالى' – God's knowledge is not through an intellect, so it would be excluded.
4
Analyze the response to the second objection
The response limits the definition to 'al-ilm al-kasib' (acquired knowledge) or 'al-maktasab' (the known). God's knowledge is neither, so it is not intended to be included. The art's rules are general only relative to its own purposes.
This is a common move to restrict scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله هو الصورة الحاصلة وعرفه بعضهم: بقبول النفس تلك الصورة وبعضهم بحصول صورة الشيء عند العقل، فعلى الأول من مقولة الكيف وعلى الثاني من مقولة الانفعال وعلى الثالث من مقولة الإضافة وليس هنا موضع تحقيق ذلك فليطلب من مطولات كتب الحكمة. قال الأستاذ الشيخ محمد الكرمي في هذا المورد: إني لم أجد تعريفاً للعلم أطري وأحسن وألصق بالنفس وأمتن من قول العلامة الشيخ عبدالهادي شليلة: حقيقة العلم انكشاف الواقع له من المعلوم حكم التابع فإنه العلم الصادق ليس هو إلا انكشاف الأشياء على ما هي عليه. وكم تعتور النفس صور للأشياء تخال أنها صور واقعية وشعاع ذوات الأشياء بأنفسها وهي في الواقع خداع وكذب. وعلى كل حال فكل تعريف يتعدى حدود هذا التعريف تقصيراً أو زيادة فهو جزاف. قال الشيخ محمد علي (ره) في تحقيق المقام أيضاً ما هذا لفظه: فإن قيل إن هذا يستلزم خروج التصورات الجزئية عن المحدود لظهور أن صورها إنما تحصل في الآلات الجزئية دون العقل فإنه إنما يكون مدركاً للكليات لا الجزئيات كما تقرر في موضعه. قلت ليس معنى قولهم: إن العقل لا يدرك الجزئيات، أنه لا يدركها مطلقاً بل بنفسه وبدون واسطة شيء أما معها فلا ضرورة أن الأشياء كلها إنما ترسم في العقل. غاية ما في الباب أن بعضها يرتسم بنفسه وبدون واسطة وبعضها يرتسم بسبب القوى الجزئية الظاهرة أو الباطنة. ثم ربما يتوهم أيضاً أن هذا يستلزم خروج بعض أفراد المعرف عن التعريف وهو علم الله تعالى، لعدم إطلاق العقل عليه تعالى عما يقول الظالمون. والجواب أن المراد بالعلم هو العلم الكاسب أو المكتسب وعلمه تعالى ليس بواحد منهما ولا ينافي ذلك عموم قواعد هذا الفن فإن التعميم إنما هو بالنسبة إلى الأغراض المطلوبة من الفن لا مطلقاً فتأمل. قوله والمصنف لم يتعرض بتعريفه: لما ترك المصنف ما هو المناسب من تعريف العلم قبل التقسيم، ضرورة أن التقسيم حكم من أحكامه وهي لا يتحقق إلا بعد تحقق الشيء كما هو ظاهر، اعتذر المحشي (ره) عنه بثلاثة وجوه: الأول: أنه يكفي التصور بوجه ما في مقام التقسيم، يعني أنه يتبادر منه عند الإطلاق أنه ما يطلق عليه في اصطلاحهم العلم وهذا القدر من التصور يكفي في مقام التقسيم كما هو ظاهر. وذلك كما أن النحويين قسموا المستثنى إلى المتصل وإلى المنفصل من غير أن يعرفوه أولاً اعتماداً على ذلك. الثاني: أنه لما كان تعريف العلم مشهوراً مستفيضاً اكتفى به عن ذكر تعريفه روماً للاختصار. الثالث: أن العلم بديهي التصور كما نقل عن الإمام الرازي وذلك لأنه من الكيفيات الوجدانية التي يجدها كل من رجع إلى نفسه كالجوع والعطش والري والشبع. وربما يستدل أيضاً بأن كل أحد من العقلاء الذين لم يتمارسوا اكتساب التصورات من الحدود والرسوم أصلاً ولا عرفوا كيفيته، إذا استفهم عنهم هل تعلمون الشيء الفلاني أم لا؟ اختاروا في الجواب أحد الأمرين قطعاً وما ذلك إلا لعلمهم بمفهوم السؤال الذي من جملته العلم. ولا يحق أن واحداً منهما لا يثبت المدعى. أما الأول فظاهر فإن غاية ما يستلزمه أنه لا يحتاج إلى التعريف المعنوي وأما احتياجه إلى التعريف اللفظي وتعيين مسماه من بين المعاني المخزونة في الذهن فياق على حاله وكذلك حال جميع الوجدانيات لظهور أن البداهة إنما هي بالنسبة إلى إدراك مصداق العلم ومفهومه لا إلى إدراك أن هذا المعنى موضوع له للفظ العلم كما هو غير خفي على من له ذوق سليم.
His statement: 'It is the form that obtains' and some defined it as: the soul's acceptance of that form, and some as: the occurrence of the form of the thing in the intellect. So according to the first, it belongs to the category of quality; according to the second, to the category of passion; and according to the third, to the category of relation. This is not the place to investigate that, so let it be sought from the extended books of wisdom. The teacher Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi said in this regard: I have not found a definition for knowledge that is fresher, better, more attached to the soul, and firmer than the statement of the scholar Sheikh Abd al-Hadi Shalila: The reality of knowledge is the disclosure of the reality to the known, like the rule of the dependent; for true knowledge is nothing but the disclosure of things as they are. How many pictures of things assault the soul, imagining that they are real pictures and the very radiance of the things themselves, while in reality they are deception and falsehood. In any case, any definition that exceeds the limits of this definition, whether by deficiency or excess, is arbitrary. Sheikh Muhammad Ali (may Allah have mercy on him) said in the investigation of the topic also, verbatim: If it is said that this necessitates the exclusion of particular conceptions from the defined, because it is apparent that their pictures are obtained in the particular faculties, not in the intellect, for the intellect only apprehends universals, not particulars, as established in its place. I say: The meaning of their statement 'the intellect does not apprehend particulars' is not that it does not apprehend them absolutely, but that it does not apprehend them by itself and without any intermediary. As for with an intermediary, there is no necessity; for all things are only depicted in the intellect. The most that can be said is that some are depicted by themselves and without an intermediary, and some are depicted by means of the external or internal particular faculties. Then it might also be imagined that this necessitates the exclusion of some individuals of the defined from the definition, namely the knowledge of Allah the Exalted, because the intellect is not applied to Him—exalted is He above what the wrongdoers say. The answer is that what is meant by knowledge is the acquired knowledge or the known, and His knowledge is neither of these two. The generality of the rules of this art does not contradict that, for generality is only with respect to the purposes sought from the art, not absolutely. So reflect. His statement: 'And the author did not address its definition': Since the author omitted what is appropriate, namely the definition of knowledge before the division, the division being a ruling among its rulings, and it is not realized except after the realization of the thing, as is apparent. The commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) excused him with three aspects: First: A certain kind of conception suffices in the context of division, meaning that it is understood from it when unqualified that it is what in their terminology is called 'knowledge', and this amount of conception suffices in the context of division, as is apparent. This is similar to how the grammarians divided the exception into the connected and the disconnected without first defining it, relying on that. Second: Since the definition of knowledge was well-known and widespread, he sufficed with that instead of mentioning its definition, aiming for brevity. Third: Knowledge is self-evident in conception, as transmitted from Imam al-Razi, because it is among the subjective qualities that every person finds when returning to themselves, like hunger, thirst, satiety, and fullness. It might also be argued that every rational person who has not practiced acquiring conceptions from definitions and descriptions at all and does not know how to do so, when asked 'Do you know such-and-such or not?' would certainly choose one of the two answers, and that is only because of their knowledge of the concept of the question, which includes 'knowledge'. It is not valid that either of them does not establish the claim. As for the first, it is apparent, for the utmost it entails is that it does not need a conceptual definition; but its need for a verbal definition and specification of its referent among the meanings stored in the mind remains as it is. The same is the case for all subjective things, because it is clear that self-evidence is only with respect to the apprehension of the instance of knowledge and its concept, not to the apprehension that this meaning is the lexical sense of the word 'knowledge', as is not hidden from one with sound taste.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya deals with the definition of 'ilm (knowledge). It begins by quoting a definition that 'ilm is 'the form that obtains' and notes different definitions: some say it is the soul's acceptance of that form, others say it is the occurrence of the form in the intellect. The commentator observes that these definitions place 'ilm in different categories (quality, passion, relation). Then a lengthy definition from Sheikh Abd al-Hadi Shalila is presented: knowledge is the disclosure of reality as it is. The author warns against definitions that overreach or fall short. Next, an objection is raised: does this definition exclude particular conceptions, since they are obtained in the particular faculties and not the intellect? The response explains that the intellect does apprehend particulars with the help of instruments, so all things are depicted in the intellect. Another objection concerns God's knowledge: God is not subject to intellect, so God's knowledge would be excluded. The answer restricts the definition to acquired or known knowledge, not divine knowledge, noting that the art's general rules apply only to its own purposes. Finally, the passage addresses why the author (of the matn) did not define 'ilm before dividing it. The commentator offers three excuses: 1) A sufficient conception is available from common usage; 2) The definition is well-known and thus omitted for brevity; 3) 'Ilm is self-evident, like subjective experiences. The commentary emphasizes that self-evidence here means we know the concept and instance, not the lexical correspondence.

Key Terms
التصورات الجزئية
الآلات الجزئية
العقل
علم الله
العلم الكاسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the hashishi respond to the objection that the definition excludes particular conceptions?
A) He argues that particulars are not really known, so exclusion is fine.
B) He says the intellect apprehends particulars without any mediation.
C) He explains that the intellect apprehends particulars through mediation of the senses.
D) He changes the definition to include 'particular' explicitly.

Key Takeaway
A definition's scope can be clarified by responding to objections that challenge its coverage of certain cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the objection that the definition of 'ilm as 'conception of the thing as it is' excludes the knowledge of the Prophet (s) in his state of sleep. Use the method of the hashishi to formulate a response (e.g., sleep does not eliminate the intellect, it only suspends some faculties, so the definition still applies).

Why the Author Did Not Define 'Ilm: Three Excuses
Justifies omission of a definition with three reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the three excuses given for omitting a definition of 'ilm before dividing it.

Core Idea
A known or self-evident concept needs no definition for division.

The author of the matn did not define 'ilm before dividing it into its types. The hashishi provides three justifications for this omission: (1) a common understanding suffices for division, (2) the definition is well-known, (3) 'ilm is self-evident like hunger. Each excuse appeals to a different principle: pragmatic sufficiency, conventional knowledge, or psychological immediacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the three excuses
Find the three numbered reasons in the Arabic: 'الاول: أنه يكفي التصور بوجه ما...', 'الثاني: أنه لما كان تعريف العلم مشهوراً...', 'الثالث: أن العلم بديهي التصور...'
2
Understand the first excuse
A minimal conception (what the word means in common usage) is enough to proceed with division. The grammarian's treatment of 'mustathna' (exception) without defining it is given as an analogy.
This relies on the idea that division can be based on a known extension, not a precise intension.
3
Understand the second excuse
The definition of 'ilm was so widespread and famous that the author could omit it for brevity, expecting readers already know it.
This is a pedagogical-economy argument.
4
Understand the third excuse
'Ilm is self-evident (badihi al-tasawwur), like subjective sensations (hunger, thirst). Everyone who has experienced them knows what they are without definition.
The commentator adds that this self-evidence applies to the concept and its instances, not to the lexical correspondence.

Source Text

في الذهن فياق على حاله وكذلك حال جميع الوجدانيات لظهور أن البداهة إنما هي بالنسبة إلى إدراك مصداق العلم ومفهومه لا إلى إدراك أن هذا المعنى موضوع له للفظ العلم كما هو غير خفي على من له ذوق سليم.

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya deals with the definition of 'ilm (knowledge). It begins by quoting a definition that 'ilm is 'the form that obtains' and notes different definitions: some say it is the soul's acceptance of that form, others say it is the occurrence of the form in the intellect. The commentator observes that these definitions place 'ilm in different categories (quality, passion, relation). Next, an objection is raised: does this definition exclude particular conceptions, since they are obtained in the particular faculties and not the intellect? The response explains that the intellect does apprehend particulars with the help of instruments, so all things are depicted in the intellect. Another objection concerns God's knowledge: God is not subject to intellect, so God's knowledge would be excluded. The answer restricts the definition to acquired or known knowledge, not divine knowledge, noting that the art's general rules apply only to its own purposes. Finally, the passage addresses why the author (of the matn) did not define 'ilm before dividing it. The commentator offers three excuses: 1) A sufficient conception is available from common usage; 2) The definition is well-known and thus omitted for brevity; 3) 'Ilm is self-evident, like subjective experiences. The commentary emphasizes that self-evidence here means we know the concept and instance, not the lexical correspondence.

Definition of Knowledge as Disclosure of Reality and Two Objections
Author defines knowledge and answers two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain and defend the definition of knowledge as disclosure of things as they are, and analyze the two objections regarding particular concepts and Allah's knowledge using the author's responses.

Core Idea
True knowledge is disclosure of things as they are; objections about particulars and divine knowledge are resolved by clarifying the role of mediary faculties and the scope of definition.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational definition of knowledge from the Islamic logical tradition: knowledge is the disclosure (inkishāf) of things as they are in reality. The author not only presents this definition but also anticipates and refutes two critical objections that challenge its comprehensiveness. By working through these objections, you will deepen your understanding of how knowledge relates to both particular concepts and divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of knowledge
Locate the exact statement defining knowledge in the Arabic text. It is phrased as 'the reality of knowledge is the disclosure of the [thing as it is in] reality' (حقيقة العلم انكشاف الوا الواقع). Note that this definition emphasizes correspondence to actual reality.
The author warns that many mental images are deceptive, so true knowledge must match reality.
2
Identify the two objections
The text presents two objections to the definition. First, that particular concepts (التصورات الجزئية) are excluded because the intellect directly perceives only universals. Second, that Allah's knowledge is excluded because 'intellect' is not attributed to Him. Identify the Arabic phrases where these are introduced.
The objections are signaled by 'فان قيل' (if it is said) and 'ثم ربما يتوهم أيضاً' (then perhaps it is also imagined).
3
Analyze the response to the first objection
The author clarifies that the intellect does perceive particulars, but through the mediation of particular faculties (al-quwwā al-juz'iyya), such as the senses or imagination. The intellect is not deprived of knowledge of particulars absolutely; it acquires them via intermediaries. This preserves the definition's inclusiveness.
The key phrase: 'ليس معنى قولهم : ان العقل لا يدرك الجزئيات، أنه لا يدركها مطلقا بل بنفسه و بدون واسطة شيء'.
4
Analyze the response to the second objection
The author responds that the definition is meant for acquired human knowledge, not absolute divine knowledge. The rules of the craft (قواعد هذا الفن) apply only to its own purposes, so they do not need to cover the knowledge of Allah. Thus, the definition is not invalidated by this exclusion.
The response uses the principle of generalization relative to the craft's purposes: 'فان التعميم انما هو بالنسبة الى الاغراض المطلوبة من الفن لا مطلقا'.
5
Reflect on the completeness of the definition
Consider whether the definition, after these clarifications, includes all instances of knowledge and excludes all non-knowledge. The definition remains intact: knowledge is disclosure of reality, and anything that is not true correspondence is not knowledge. The objections are resolved without changing the core definition.
The author ends with 'فتأمل' (so reflect), inviting deeper contemplation.

From the statement of the scholar Sheikh Abdulhadi Shalila: The reality of knowledge is the disclosure of the [thing as it is in] reality; it has the ruling of a dependent, for true knowledge is nothing but the disclosure of things as they are. How often the soul forms images of things that it imagines are real images and the ray of the essences of things themselves, while in reality they are deception and falsehood. In any case, every definition that transgresses the limits of this definition, whether by deficiency or excess, is baseless. Then if it is said that this entails the exclusion of particular concepts from the defined, because it is apparent that their images are only obtained in the particular faculties, not in the intellect, for the intellect only perceives universals, not particulars, as established in its place. I say: The meaning of their statement 'the intellect does not perceive particulars' is not that it does not perceive them absolutely, but [that it does not perceive them] by itself and without any medium; as for with a medium, there is no necessity, for all things are only depicted in the intellect. The most that can be said is that some of them are depicted by themselves and without a medium, and some are depicted by means of the particular faculties, whether apparent or internal. Then perhaps it is also imagined that this entails the exclusion of some individuals of the defined from the definition, namely the knowledge of Allah Almighty, because intellect is not attributed to Him exalted above what the wrongdoers say. [The answer is:] Not one of them, and the generality of the rules of this craft does not prevent that, for generalization is only relative to the purposes sought from the craft, not absolutely. So reflect.

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge as the disclosure of things as they are, emphasizing that true knowledge corresponds to reality. It begins by quoting Sheikh Abdulhadi Shalila, who warns that many mental images are deceptive. The author then addresses two objections to this definition. First, it is objected that particular concepts would be excluded because the intellect only grasps universals. The response clarifies that the intellect does perceive particulars, but through the mediation of particular faculties (like senses or imagination). Second, it is objected that Allah's knowledge would be excluded since 'intellect' is not attributed to Him. The response states that the definition intends acquired or earned knowledge, not absolute knowledge, and that the rules of the art apply only to its own purposes. The passage ends with 'so reflect' (tā'ammal), indicating that this explanation should be pondered.

Refuting the Circularity Objection to Defining Knowledge
Refuting the circularity objection against defining knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the circularity objection against defining knowledge by distinguishing real from verbal definitions and universal from particular knowledge.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided because the definition of universal knowledge relies on particular knowledge, not itself.

Some scholars argued that defining knowledge is impossible because all things are known through knowledge, so any definition would require knowledge, leading to circularity. The author refutes this by showing the objection only applies to real definitions (which reveal essence), and even then circularity does not occur because universal knowledge depends on particular knowledge, not on itself. This lesson unpacks the refutation and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What exactly is the claim? It says defining knowledge is impossible because if you try to define it, you would need to know it already, and since all things are known through knowledge, defining knowledge would require knowledge of the definition, creating a circle.
This is an application of the logical fallacy of circularity (al-dawr).
2
Distinguish real vs. verbal definition
The author first notes that the objection only prevents a real definition (ta'rīf ḥaqīqī), which reveals the essence. A verbal definition (ta'rīf lafẓī) only clarifies linguistic usage and does not require knowing the essence, so no circularity arises.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.
3
Analyse the dependency structure
Even if we grant that the target is the concept of knowledge (a real definition), circularity is avoided. The definition of universal knowledge depends on particular knowledge of the things being defined, not on universal knowledge itself. Thus, there is no self-dependence.
Universal knowledge (al-ilm al-kullī) vs. particular knowledge (al-ilm al-juz'ī).
4
Evaluate the refutation
The refutation is logically sound: it identifies a false presupposition (that only real definitions matter) and provides a valid distinction to break the circle. This shows that defining knowledge is not impossible.
The argument is not rejecting the possibility of definition entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما قيل ان تعريف العلم غير ممكن فان الاشياء كلها انما تعلم به فلو علم بغيره لزم الدور لتوقف معلومية كل منهما على معلومية الآخر. و فيه ان هذا ايضاً انما يفيد عدم امكان تعريفه بالمعرف الحقيقى كما هو ظاهر من قوله: «فان الاشياء ... لظهور انا نعلم الاشياء من غير ملاحظة ان لفظ العلم موضوع للمعنى الفلاني بل من غير علمنا بذلك فتأمل. ولو سلم ان المدعى انما هو عدم امكان تعريف مفهوم العلم لا مدلوله اللفظى كما ذكر، فلزوم الدور ممنوع ، فان الموقوف هو العلم الكلى والموقوف عليه هو الجزئى لظهور أن الاشياء انما يحتاج في تصورها الى تصور علم جزئى متعلق بها لا مطلق العلم وذلك واضح.
And perhaps it is said that defining knowledge is impossible because all things are known only through it; so if it were known by another, circularity would follow, since the knowability of each would depend on the knowability of the other. And in it [the response], this also only indicates the impossibility of defining it by a real definition, as is apparent from his statement: 'for all things... because it is apparent that we know things without noticing that the word 'knowledge' is used for a certain meaning, rather without our knowledge of that. So reflect. And even if it is granted that the claim is only the impossibility of defining the concept of knowledge, not its verbal referent as mentioned, then the entailment of circularity is not granted, because what depends is universal knowledge and what is depended upon is particular knowledge, for it is apparent that things in their conception only need a particular knowledge related to them, not absolute knowledge. And that is clear.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the possibility of defining knowledge: if all things are known through knowledge, then any definition of knowledge would require knowledge of the definition, leading to circularity. The response distinguishes between a real definition (ta‘rīf ḥaqīqī) and a verbal definition (ta‘rīf lafẓī). The argument only prevents a real definition, because a verbal definition does not require knowing the true essence of knowledge; it only requires knowing the linguistic usage. Furthermore, even if we consider a real definition, circularity does not occur because what depends (the definition of universal knowledge) uses particular knowledge of the things being defined, not universal knowledge itself. This refutation shows that the impossibility claim is unfounded.

Refuting objections against defining 'ilm
Refutation of arguments against defining 'ilm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the two objections against defining the concept of 'ilm by distinguishing real and lexical definitions.

Core Idea
The concept of 'ilm cannot be defined really, but it can be defined lexically.

This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya responds to two arguments that the concept of knowledge ('ilm) is self-evident and cannot be defined. By mastering these refutations, you will learn how to distinguish real from lexical definition—a key tool in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims that if someone can answer a question about the meaning of 'ilm, then the concept is known and need not be defined. The second claims that defining 'ilm leads to circularity because all things are known through 'ilm.
Focus on the logical structure of each objection.
2
Recognize the author's key distinction
The author distinguishes real definition (ta'rīf haqīqī) from lexical definition (ta'rīf lafẓī). Real definition is by genus and differentia; lexical definition only explains the meaning of the word.
This distinction is central to the refutation.
3
Analyze the refutation of the first objection
The author grants that one knowledgeable of the convention can answer, but argues that this only shows lexical knowledge, not real definition. Moreover, not every term's real concept is known even to those who know the convention.
The objection confuses lexical with real definition.
4
Analyze the refutation of the second objection
The author denies the circularity. He argues that the dependence is between universal 'ilm and particular 'ilm, not a closed loop. Things require particular 'ilm to be known, not absolute 'ilm.
Circularity is avoided by distinguishing the type of 'ilm involved.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that 'ilm cannot be defined by a real definition, but it can be defined lexically. The objections only target real definition and do not affect lexical definition.
This resolution respects the self-evidence of the concept while allowing for explanatory definition.

As for the second: because what was mentioned regarding choosing one of two answers when questioned, if it is in relation to one who is knowledgeable of the convention, then it is granted; but what is considered in definitions is the situation of those not knowledgeable of the convention. Even if granted, all defined things in relation to one knowledgeable of the convention are known and do not need definition, so there is no justification for specifying that with 'ilm. And if it is in relation to one who is not knowledgeable of the convention, then we do not concede that he chooses one of the two answers, because the answer is a branch of understanding, and the one not knowledgeable of the convention does not understand anything from the question such that his answer could be valid. Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary addressing two objections raised against the idea that the concept of 'ilm (knowledge) can be defined. The first objection argues that if someone can answer a question about the meaning of 'ilm, it proves the concept is known and needs no definition. The author refutes this by distinguishing real definition (ta'rīf haqīqī) from lexical definition (ta'rīf lafzī), showing that the ability to answer only indicates lexical knowledge, not real definition. The second objection claims that defining 'ilm leads to circularity because all things are known through 'ilm. The author responds that circularity only arises if we demand a real definition of the universal concept, but the requirement for knowing things is particular 'ilm, not universal, so no circularity exists. The passage emphasizes that 'ilm can be defined lexically but not really, and the objections fail because they confuse these two types of definition.

The Judgmental Relation: Perfect Predicative vs. Restrictive
Author argues that the judgmental relation is perfect predicative, not restrictive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the perfect predicative and restrictive interpretations of the judgmental relation and explain why the perfect predicative view is preferred in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The judgmental relation is the object of assent, not a mere conceptual link.

In this passage, the commentator distinguishes between two interpretations of the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya) that underlies every proposition. Some logicians argue it must be affirmative and restrictive, but the author shows this leads to problems. Instead, the perfect predicative relation is the correct view, supported by the consensus of philosophers and our own intuition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Find where the author presents the position that the judgmental relation is perfect predicative (tamma khabariyya) and the opposing view that it is affirmative restrictive (thubutiyya taqyidiyya).
Both views are discussed in the first few sentences.
2
Examine the objection to the restrictive view
The author notes that if the relation were restrictive, then a negative proposition like 'Zayd is not a writer' would become affirmative when the subject exists. Understand why this is a problem: the negative would collapse into an affirmation.
This objection is crucial to the argument.
3
Analyze the argument for the perfect predicative view
The author argues that the philosophers' agreement that conceptualizing the judgmental relation is a condition for judgment only makes sense if it is perfect predicative. Without that mental representation, assent cannot occur.
Consider why the restrictive view fails to satisfy the condition of judgment.
4
Test with inner experience
The author invites you to consult your own intuition. Reflect on how you mentally process a negative proposition and see if a restrictive relation is necessary.
This step reinforces the argument through introspection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسر النسبة الحكمية بالنسبة التامة الخبرية الثبوتية في الموجبة والسالبة في السالبة، ومنهم من ذهب الى انها يجب ان يكون ثبوتية تقييدية فيهما والا يعبر السالبة موجبة عند وجود الموضوع وفيه نظر لانه انما كانت الحكمية النسبة لو كانت النسبة الحكمية تقييدية كما اعترف به و ملحوظة تفصيلاً على وجه تكون محكوماً يصبح عليها كما اذا قلنا النسبة بين الطرفين باللا ثبوت ليست بواقعة و اما اذا كانت قامة خبرية وغير ملحوظة تفصيلاً كما يفهم من قولنا زيد ليس بكاتب و ادركتها ثم اذ عنتها وقبلتها فلا . والحق انها تامة خبرية، لان الحكماء اتفقوا على ان تصور النسبة الحكمية شرط الحصول الحكم وهذا اما الاتفاق منهم اذا كانت النسبة الحكمية هى النسبة التامة الخبرية. لأنه ما لم يحصل تلك النسبة يصح فى الذهن لم يكن له الاذعان الذى هو من ضروريات الحكم و اما اذا التقييدية الثبوتية فلا، اذ يمكن بعد تصور الطرفين تصور النسبة التامة الخبرية بينهما بلا اذعان ثم مع الاذعان من غير ملاحظة نسبة تقييدية بينهما اصلاً وذلك ظاهر لمن راجع وجدانه . (عبد الرحيم)
He interpreted the judgmental relation as the perfect predicative affirmative relation in the affirmative and the negative in the negative. Some held that it must be affirmative restrictive in both, otherwise the negative would become affirmative when the subject exists. There is a consideration: if the judgmental relation were restrictive, as acknowledged, and considered in detail so that it is judged, as when we say 'the relation between the two extremes by non-existence is not occurring', but if it were predicative and not considered in detail as understood from our saying 'Zayd is not a writer' and you perceive it, then assent to it and accept it, then no. The truth is that it is perfect predicative, because the philosophers agreed that conceptualizing the judgmental relation is a condition for the occurrence of judgment. This agreement only obtains if the judgmental relation is the perfect predicative relation. For if that relation is not obtained in the mind, he would not have the assent which is a necessary concomitant of judgment. But if it is affirmative restrictive, then no, because after conceptualizing the two extremes, it is possible to conceptualize the perfect predicative relation between them without assent, then with assent without observing a restrictive relation between them at all. That is clear to one who consults his own intuition. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya) that is involved in propositions and assent. The author begins by stating that some scholars interpret the judgmental relation as a perfect predicative relation (tamma khabariyya) in both affirmative and negative propositions. Others hold that it must be an affirmative restrictive relation (taqyidiyya thubutiyya) to avoid making a negative proposition become affirmative when the subject exists. The author then examines the restrictive view: if it were restrictive, it would have to be considered in detail as a subject of judgment, as in 'the relation between the two extremes is not existent' – this would require a second-order judgment. But in ordinary usage like 'Zayd is not a writer', we perceptually grasp the relation without such detail. The correct view, argues the author, is the perfect predicative relation. The philosophers agreed that conceptualizing the judgmental relation is a condition for judgment; this agreement only makes sense if the relation is perfect predicative. Without that mental representation, assent (idh'an) – a necessary concomitant of judgment – cannot occur. On the restrictive view, one could after conceiving the two extremes conceive the perfect predicative relation without assent and then later assent without any restrictive relation. This is evident to anyone who reflects on their own inner experience. The passage concludes by noting that the author (al-Muhashi?) supports the perfect predicative view.

Key Terms
النسبة الحكمية
النسبة التامة الخبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it makes all negative propositions impossible.
B) Because it would require a second-order judgment to avoid turning negatives into affirmatives.
C) Because philosophers never agreed on it.
D) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The judgmental relation must be perfect predicative because assent requires the mental presence of the relation itself, not a secondary restriction.

Definitions of Angel, Jinn, and Spirit
Author defines angel, jinn, and spirit with their properties.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Three spiritual entities: luminous celestial, dark fiery, and immaterial substance.

In this passage, the author concisely defines three key spiritual entities in Islamic cosmology: the angel (malak), the jinn, and the spirit (rūḥ). Each has distinct properties that determine its nature and capabilities. Understanding these definitions is foundational for theological discussions about the unseen world.

Guided Path

Angels can take any form except the dog and pig; jinn can take any form including those. Spirit does not take forms but attaches to the body.

The spirit's attachment to the body is compared to rose water's scent in a rose. This indicates an immaterial essence that permeates the body without being material.

Intentional Reasoning vs. Intuition in Logic
Author distinguishes deliberate reasoning from intuitive grasp.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish deliberate reasoning (nazar) from intuition (hads) and explain why intuition does not undermine the intentional character of nazar.

Core Idea
Reasoning is deliberate; intuition is involuntary. They are distinct modes of knowledge.

This gloss from Abd al-Rahim clarifies a key feature of nazar (reasoning): it is a deliberate act. Some might think that sudden intuitive insights (hads) contradict this, but the author explains that intuition is not voluntary, so it does not challenge the definition. Let's examine the distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key contrast
Notice the passage contrasts 'by way of intention' (biṭarīq al-qaṣd) with intuition (hads). The core distinction is deliberate choice vs. involuntary occurrence.
2
Understand why intuition is not a counterexample
The passage states that intuition involves 'comprehension of ordered principles in a single stroke', but it is 'not by the soul's intention and choice'. Therefore, the definition of nazar as deliberate stands.
3
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider a theologian constructing a proof for God's existence step by step (deliberate nazar) versus someone who suddenly feels certainty about God's existence without reasoning (intuition). The latter does not replace the former in systematic theology.
This shows why kalam values methodical argument over mere intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اى بطريق القصد كما هو المتبادر سيما وقد قيل بالغاية فلا ينتقض بتعقل المبادى المرتبة دفعة في الحدس لأنه ليس بقصد النفس واختياره بل يسنح لها بغير اختيارها. (عبد الرحيم)
That is, by way of intention, as is apparent, especially since the goal has been mentioned, so it is not contradicted by the comprehension of ordered principles in a single stroke in intuition, because that is not by the soul's intention and choice, but rather occurs to it involuntarily. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This gloss clarifies that the reasoning process (nazar) is by deliberate intention (biṭarīq al-qaṣd). This is not contradicted by cases of intuition (hads) where principles are grasped all at once, because intuition is involuntary and not by the soul's choice. Thus the definition stands.

Deliberate intention is essential to reasoning; intuition is a distinct involuntary mode that does not negate the definition of nazar.

Avoiding Ambiguous Terms in Definitions
Author explains why ambiguous words like 'ilm must be avoided in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguous terms (mushtarak) in definitions and explain why they must be avoided.

Core Idea
A clear definition cannot use a word that means two different things.

In logic, a definition must be precise and unambiguous. The author warns against using al-lafz al-mushtarak (ambiguous words) because they can mislead. He illustrates with the word 'ilm, which has multiple technical meanings. Learning to spot such terms is crucial for constructing valid definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term in the passage
Find 'ilm and note that the author explicitly labels it as mushtarak (ambiguous). Recognize that the passage is about avoiding such terms in definitions.
2
List the distinct meanings of 'ilm mentioned
The passage enumerates: mental form (tasawwur/tasdiq), certainty (yaqin), opinion (zann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), taqlid, and broader belief. Write them down to see the range.
Each meaning belongs to a different category in Islamic epistemology.
3
Explain why each meaning causes confusion in a definition
If a definer intends one meaning but the reader assumes another, the definition fails to convey the intended concept precisely. For example, defining 'ilm as 'firm belief' might be confused with 'certainty' or 'taqlid'.
This defeats the purpose of definition (hadd or rasm).
4
Apply the principle to another Islamic term
Think of a term like 'iman' (faith) which can mean 'verbal profession', 'inner conviction', or 'actions'. Explain why using 'iman' in a definition without clarification would be problematic.
This exercise connects the logical tool to actual Islamic discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله منها التحرز عن استعمال اللفظ المشترك : فان العلم كما يطلق على الصورة الحاصلة عند العقل التي قسموها الى التصور والتصديق باقسامه من: الظن والجزم الثابت المطابق للواقع الذي يسمونه يقيناً في الاصطلاح و الغير المطابق الذى هو عبارة عن الجهل المركب والجزم المطابق الغير الثابت الذي يسمونه تقليداً في بعض الاصطلاحات و اعتقاداً في بعض آخر فكذلك يطلق على الاعتقاد الجازم الثابت المطابق للواقع الذى لا يقبل التشكيك و على الاعتقاد بالمعنى الاعم الشامل له ولغيره من الظن و الجهل و التقليد، فلا يجوز استعماله في التعريف كساير الالفاظ المشتركة ، لأنه ربما يراد منها معنى و يفهم المخاطب معنى آخر فافهم.
His statement: 'among them is avoiding the use of an ambiguous word (lafz mushtarak).' For 'ilm (knowledge) is used for the mental form acquired in the intellect, which they divide into conception and assent, with its types: opinion (zann), firm and stable conformity to reality which they call certainty (yaqin) in terminology, and non-conformity which is compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and firm but unstable conformity which they call taqlid in some terminologies and i'tiqad in others. Similarly, it is used for firm, stable, reality-conforming belief that admits no doubt, and also for belief in the broader sense that includes it and other things like opinion, ignorance, and taqlid. Hence it is not permissible to use it in definitions, like all other ambiguous words, because sometimes the speaker intends one meaning and the listener understands another. So understand.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an annotation on why the author of the matn used 'ma'lum' instead of the ambiguous term 'ilm. The commentator lists the many meanings of 'ilm: it can refer to the mental form (which itself divides), or to specific types of belief (certainty, opinion, compound ignorance, taqlid). Because it has multiple meanings, it is a mushtarak (homonym) and thus unsuitable for precise definition. The listener might misunderstand which sense is intended, defeating the purpose of definition. This is a standard principle in logic: definitions must use unambiguous terms.

Key Terms

Qanun: From Straightedge to Logical Rule
Author gives the etymological and technical definition of qanun in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the linguistic and technical definitions of qanun and explain the analogy between them.

Core Idea
Qanun in logic is a universal premise that, like a ruler, straightens many particular cases.

The term 'qanun' is common in modern political discourse, but in classical Islamic logic it has a precise technical meaning. This lesson traces the word from its origin as a straightedge for writing to its role as a universal proposition that governs many particular rulings in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic origin
The text mentions that 'qanun' is originally a Roman word for a straightedge used to align lines of writing. Note the physical instrument: it is a single tool that ensures straightness across many lines.
The straightedge is called 'misṭar' or 'misṭarah'.
2
Identify the technical definition
In logic, qanun is defined as a universal proposition (qaḍiyya kulliyya) from which many particular rulings are known. The example given is from Arabic grammar: 'Every faʿil is marfuʿ' – a universal rule that applies to every subject of a verb.
The universal subject 'every faʿil' allows deriving individual rulings like 'Zayd is marfuʿ' when Zayd is a faʿil.
3
Analyze the analogy between the two meanings
The commentator explicitly notes the correlation: both the straightedge and the universal proposition are single instruments that produce many results. The straightedge straightens multiple lines; the universal premise yields multiple particular conclusions.
This analogy is not accidental – it reveals how technical terms in logic often build on concrete physical metaphors.
4
Consider the author's note on usage
The author states that the term can be considered either a customary reality (ḥaqīqa ʿurfiyya) or an explicit metaphor (istiʿāra muṣarraḥa), and that the first is preferable. This shows the sensitivity to how terms transition from ordinary to technical language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قيل رومى موضوع فى الاصل اى فى لغتهم لمسطر الكتابة، وقيل موضوع المسطر اما مسطر الكتابه او الجدول وفى القاموس : القانون مقياس كل شيء جمعه قوانين. قوله لمسطر الكتابة المسطر أو المسطرة كما هو اصطلاح اليوم، آلة هندسية معدة لتعديل سطور الكتابة. و في الاصطلاح قضية كلية تعرف منها احكام جمة على عدد جزئيات موضوعها كقول النحاة كل فاعل مرفوع ، فان الرفع حكم كلى لعمومية موضوعه يعلم منه احكام جزئيات الفاعل من قام زيد و قعد عمرو ومشى خالد و سعى بكر الى الوف غير ذلك. قوله و في الاصطلاح قضية كلية والمناسبة بين المعنى الاصلى والاصطلاحي ظاهر فان كل واحد منها شيء واحد يتوصل به الى اشياء متعددة وامور متفرقة. ثم لا يخفى انه يجوز فيه الأمران المتقدمان في المقدمة من جواز كونه حقيقة عرفية او استعارة مصرحة و كان الاول اولى فافهم.
It is said that 'Romi' is originally, in their language, for the straightedge of writing. And it is said that the straightedge is either for writing or for the table. In al-Qamus: 'al-qanun' is the measure of everything, plural qawanin. His statement: 'for the straightedge of writing' - the straightedge (mishtar or mistara) is, as is the current term, an engineering tool prepared for straightening lines of writing. In terminology: it is a universal proposition from which many rulings are known according to the number of the particulars of its subject, like the statement of grammarians: 'Every fa'il is marfu'.' For the nominative case (raf') is a universal ruling due to the universality of its subject, from which one knows the rulings of the particulars of the subject, such as 'Zayd stood', 'Amr sat', 'Khalid walked', 'Bakr strived', and so on. His statement: 'and in terminology it is a universal proposition' - the correlation between the original meaning and the terminological one is apparent, for each one is a single thing by which multiple things and diverse matters are reached. Then it is not hidden that both possibilities mentioned earlier in the introduction are permissible: that it be a customary reality (haqiqa 'urfiyya) or an explicit metaphor (isti'ara musarrah), and the first is more appropriate. So understand.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qanun' in both its linguistic and technical senses. Linguistically, it traces the origin to a Roman word for a straightedge used in writing or aligning lines. It is also defined as a 'measure' (miqyas) in the dictionary. Technically, in logic, qanun is a universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) from which one can derive many particular rulings. The author gives the grammatical example: the rule 'Every fa'il is marfu' is a qanun because from it we can derive that 'Zayd is marfu' when we know 'Zayd is fa'il'. The commentator then notes the relationship between the literal (a tool for straightening) and technical meanings: both are single instruments that lead to many results. He also notes that the term may be considered a customary reality or a clear metaphor, but the first is preferable. This shows the precision required in defining technical terms in logic.

Key Terms
قانون (qanun): A universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) used as a major premise to derive many particular rulings, analogous to a ruler for straightening lines. [Note: This use is distinct from political 'qanun'; in logic it is a technical term.]

Key Takeaway
Qanun in logic is a universal premise that functions analogously to a straightedge, enabling derivation of many specific rulings from a single rule.

Metonymy in the Definition of Qanun: Pars Pro Toto
Author notes a metonymical use where part stands for whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the part-for-whole metonymical relationship in Islamic definitions and explain why such metonymy is permissible.

Core Idea
A definition may use metonymy where the part stands for the whole.

In this brief commentary note, the author clarifies that describing qanun as a 'universal proposition' is not strictly literal. Just as 'neck' stands for the whole person and 'eye' for a spy, the term 'universal proposition' (a part) is used to refer to the entire qanun (the whole), which includes the rule and its application. This lesson unpacks this linguistic subtlety and its relevance to logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
The term 'qanun' is being defined. The definiens given is 'a universal proposition'. Note that a qanun is typically a comprehensive rule including its application method, not just a single proposition.
The source says: 'a universal proposition, by way of applying the name of the part...'
2
Recognize the metonymical relation
Observe that 'universal proposition' is only one part of what constitutes a qanun. The full qanun includes the universal statement plus the method of deriving particular cases. Using the part to refer to the whole is a figure of speech called majaz mursal (metonymy).
The author explicitly says it is an application of the name of the part to the whole.
3
Analyze the linguistic mechanism
The part is chosen because the whole depends on it in existence and non-existence. For example, without the universal proposition, the qanun would not be what it is. Similarly, a person is identified by the neck, and a spy by the eye. This dependency justifies the metonymy.
The Arabic says: 'الجزء الذي يدور عليه الكل وجوداً وعدماً'
4
See parallels in Islamic terminology
Reflect on other Islamic terms using the same pattern: 'raqaba' (neck) for a slave, 'ayn' (eye) for a spy. These are established usages in fiqh and adab.
These examples are directly from the source.
5
Evaluate validity in definitions
In logical definitions, metonymy is acceptable when it is commonly understood and does not cause confusion. It allows concise expression. However, one must be aware when a definition is literal versus figurative.
This helps avoid misinterpretations of crictical terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اى قضية كلية اطلاقاً لاسم الجزء الذي يدور عليه الكل وجوداً و عدماً على الكل كالرقبة على الانسان والعين على الربيئة.
That is, a universal proposition, by way of applying the name of the part upon which the whole depends in existence and non-existence to the whole, like 'neck' for 'man' and 'eye' for 'spy'.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This very brief note clarifies that when the phrase 'a universal proposition' is used to define qanun, it might be a form of metonymy (majaz mursal) where the name of a part is used for the whole. The examples given: 'neck' stands for the whole person (since a man is identified by his neck), and 'eye' for 'spy' (since the spy's function is seeing). Similarly, the term 'universal proposition' might be used to refer to the entire qanun, even though a qanun is not just a proposition but also includes the method of application. This is a subtle linguistic point about the usage of terms in definitions.

Key Terms

العين

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logical definitions, a part may stand for the whole via metonymy, as seen when 'universal proposition' is used for the entire qanun.

The Definition of Qanun in Logic
Author defines qanun, explains its appropriateness, and illustrates its use in deriving particular judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the terminological definition of 'qanun' and explain its appropriateness to the literal meaning.

Core Idea
A qanun is a universal rule from which many particulars are derived.

In this lesson, we explore the term 'qanun' as it is used in logic. The author begins by noting its possible linguistic origins and then gives its precise technical meaning: a universal proposition from which numerous rulings about its subject's particulars are known. We will see how this definition relates to the literal meaning of a ruler or measure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical origin
Look at the remarks about 'qanun' being Rumic for 'line of writing' and its definition in al-Qamus. What is the literal meaning?
2
Extract the terminological definition
Find where the author says 'And terminologically, it is a universal proposition...' Write down the exact phrasing.
3
Understand the appropriateness
The author says the connection between literal and technical is that both are a single thing used to reach many. Explain why this is fitting.
4
Note the author's preference
The author mentions it could be a customary reality or explicit metaphor, preferring the first. Why does this matter?

(۵۵) و قيل رومى موضوع فى الاصل اى فى لغتهم لمسطر الكتابة، وقيل موضوع المسطر اما مسطر الكتابه او الجدول وفى القاموس : القانون مقياس كل شيء جمعه قوانين (عبد الرحيم) (۵۶) قوله لمسطر الكتابة المسطر أو المسطرة كما هو اصطلاح اليوم، آلة هندسية معدة لتعديل سطور الكتابة و في الاصطلاح قضية كلية تعرف منها احكام جمة على عدد جزئيات موضوعها كقول النحاة كل فاعل مرفوع ، فان الرفع حكم كلى لعمومية موضوعه يعلم منه احكام جزئيات الفاعل من قام زيد و قعد عمرو ومشى خالد و سعى بكر الى الوف غير ذلك (التقريب ص ۱۷-۱۸) (۵۷) قوله و في الاصطلاح قضية كلية والمناسبة بين المعنى الاصلى والاصطلاحي ظاهر فان كل واحد منها شيء واحد يتوصل به الى اشياء متعددة وامور متفرقة. ثم لا يخفى انه يجوز فيه الأمران المتقدمان في المقدمة من جواز كونه حقيقة عرفية او استعارة مصرحة و كان الاول اولى فافهم. (میرزا محمد علی (ره) (۵۸) قوله تعرف منها احكام جزئيات موضوعها وفى بعض النسخ يتعرف بها » و المأل واحد ثم ان ذلك بان تجعل تلك القضية كبرى لصغرى سهلة الحصول فى الصغرى الحاصلة من حل موضوعها على كل واحد من الجزئيات المندرجة تحت ذلك الموضوع حتى يخرج بذلك الاحكام الجزئية المشتملة عليها تلك القضية بالقوة القريبة من الفعل الى الفعل مثلاً في المثال الذي ذكره المحشى، اردنا ان تعرف حال زيد فى ضرب زيد مثلاً الذى هو جزئى من جزئيات الموضوع اعنى: الفاعل فحملنا ذلك الموضوع عليه فحصل قولنا زید فاعل و جعلناه صغرى لتلك القضية الكلية وقلنا: زيد فاعل و كل فاعل مرفوع فحصل زيد مرفوع و هكذا جميع قوانين العلوم فقس ولا تقصر. ثم لا يخفى ان ليس يجب ان يكون الفروع المندرجة تحت القانون نظرية كما هو الظاهر من قوله تعرف منها احكام جزئيات موضوعها والا لخرج عنه نحو قولهم: الشكل الاول منتج، لظهور ان الاحكام المندرجة تحت ذلك بديهية كما سبق اليه الاشارة قبيل هذا فى دفع المعارضة المشهورة، مع انهم اتفقوا على انه من مسائل المنطق، فالمراد من تعرف الاحكام مجرد صلوحها لذلك بمعنى انه لو لم تكن تلك الاحكام ضرورية حصلت و تعرفت بها فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی) (۵۹) اى قضية كلية اطلاقاً لاسم الجزء الذي يدور عليه الكل وجوداً و عدماً على الكل كالرقبة على الانسان والعين على الربيئة (محمد على)
(55) And it is said: Rumic, originally subject, i.e., in their language, for the line of writing. And it is said: subject of the line, either the line of writing or the column. And in al-Qamus: al-qanun is the measure of everything, its plural is qawanin. (Abd al-Rahim) (56) His saying: 'for the line of writing': al-misṭar or al-misṭara, as is the current term, is a geometric tool prepared for straightening lines of writing. And terminologically, it is a universal proposition from which are known numerous rulings concerning the particulars of its subject, such as the grammarian's statement: 'Every subject (fa'il) is nominative (marfu'),' for the nominative is a universal ruling due to the generality of its subject; from it are known the rulings of the particular subjects such as 'Zaid stood', 'Amr sat', 'Khalid walked', 'Bakr hastened', and thousands of others. (al-Taqrib pp. 17-18) (57) His saying: 'And terminologically, it is a universal proposition.' The appropriateness between the original meaning and the terminological meaning is apparent, for each one of them is a single thing by which one reaches multiple things and scattered matters. Then it is not hidden that both preceding matters regarding the introduction are permissible for it: the possibility of its being a customary reality or an explicit metaphor. And the first is more appropriate, so understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali (ra)) (58) His saying: 'from which are known the rulings of the particulars of its subject.' And in some copies: 'by which are known,' and the meaning is one. Then that is by making that proposition a major premise for a minor premise that is easy to obtain, from the minor premise resulting from applying its subject to each one of the particulars subsumed under that subject, so that by that the particular rulings contained in that proposition emerge from potentiality near to actuality into actuality. For example, in the example mentioned by the commentator: we want to know the status of Zaid in, say, 'Zaid struck,' which is one of the particulars of the subject, namely fa'il. So we apply that subject to him, resulting in our saying: 'Zaid is a fa'il,' and we make it a minor premise for that universal proposition, and we say: 'Zaid is a fa'il and every fa'il is marfu', resulting in 'Zaid is marfu'. And likewise for all canons of the sciences, so analogize and do not fall short. Then it is not hidden that the branches subsumed under the canon need not be theoretical, as is apparent from his saying 'from which are known the rulings of the particulars of its subject,' otherwise there would be excluded from it sayings such as 'The first figure is productive,' for it is evident that the rulings subsumed under it are intuitive, as was previously indicated near this in refuting the famous objection, and they agreed that it is among the problems of logic. So the meaning of 'from which are known the rulings' is merely its suitability for that, i.e., even if those rulings were not necessary, they would be obtained and known by it. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (59) That is, a universal proposition, by applying the name of the part on which the whole depends in existence and non-existence to the whole, like 'neck' for 'human' and 'eye' for 'spy'. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the term 'qanun' (canon/rule). It begins with a lexical note: 'qanun' may be of Rumic origin meaning 'line of writing' or 'column', and in al-Qamus it is defined as 'the measure of everything'. The author then gives the terminological definition: a universal proposition from which many rulings about the particulars of its subject are known. The example from Arabic grammar is given: 'Every fa'il is marfu''—a universal rule from which one derives specific judgments like 'Zaid is marfu''. The author explains the appropriateness of the term: both the literal tool (a ruler) and the logical tool (a universal premise) are single means to multiple ends. He notes that this usage could be a customary reality or an explicit metaphor, preferring the former. Then he elaborates on how the universal rule functions: it serves as a major premise in a syllogism; we take a particular instance, apply the subject term to it to form a minor premise, and deduce the particular ruling. The example with Zaid and 'fa'il' is worked out. He also addresses an important nuance: the branches (the particular rulings) derived from the canon need not be theoretical (nazari); they can be intuitive (badihi), as in the case of 'the first figure is productive', which is itself a universal rule but its instances are self-evident. The purpose is that the universal rule is suitable for deriving rulings even if they are not necessary. The final note clarifies that calling it a 'universal proposition' is a metonymy: the name of the part (the proposition) is applied to the whole (the rule).

القانون
قضية كلية
المسطر

Key Takeaway
Qanun is a universal proposition from which many particular rulings about its subject's instances can be derived.

How a Qanun Functions: Deriving Particular Rulings
Author defines qanun, explains its appropriateness, and illustrates its use in deriving particular judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply a universal rule (qanun) as a major premise in a categorical syllogism to derive a particular judgment.

Core Idea
A qanun works as a major premise; add a minor premise to produce a particular conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine how a qanun is actually used to derive particular rulings. The author explains that the universal proposition is made the major premise, and we apply its subject to a particular instance to form a minor premise, yielding a specific conclusion. The example from Arabic grammar is worked out, and the author notes that the derived rulings need not be theoretical—they can be intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal rule (qanun)
In the example, 'كل فاعل مرفوع' is the qanun. Note its subject and predicate.
2
Identify a particular instance
Choose a specific fa'il like 'زيد' in 'ضرب زيد'. This is one particular of the subject.
3
Form the minor premise
Apply the subject of the qanun to the instance: 'زيد فاعل'. This becomes the minor premise.
4
Construct the syllogism
Combine the minor and major: 'زيد فاعل' and 'كل فاعل مرفوع' to deduce 'زيد مرفوع'. Recognize this as a first figure Barbara syllogism.
5
Note the author's nuance
The author says even if the particular rulings are intuitive, the qanun still serves as a basis. Reflect on why this matters for logical methodology.

Definition of Logic as a Description (Rasm): Purpose and Co-Extensivity
Author explains that the definition of logic is by rasm and resolves an objection about purpose being broader.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a definition by essential nature (hadd) and a definition by description (rasm) in the context of defining a science, and identify the condition of co-extensivity for a descriptive property.

Core Idea
A definition by description must use a property exclusive to the defined thing.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the definition of logic given earlier is not an essential definition (hadd) but a descriptive one (rasm). He explains that by identifying the purpose of logic—the need people have for it in acquiring theoretical sciences—we obtain a conception of logic through its property. Then he addresses a subtle point: a purpose might be too broad, so he insists that the purpose intended must be co-extensive (ghaya musawiya). This teaches us a critical condition for valid descriptive definitions in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of definition
Notice that the commentator says the definition is 'bi al-rasm' (by description). This implies that the definition uses a property or purpose, not genus and differentia.
Rasm contrasts with hadd, which captures the essence.
2
Extract the property used in the rasm
The property is the purpose (ghaya) of logic: people need logic to acquire theoretical sciences. Check if this property is exclusive to logic.
If the purpose is shared with other sciences, the definition fails.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection says that a purpose might be broader than the thing defined (one purpose for many sciences). Understand why this would invalidate a rasm: the property must be co-extensive.
Co-extensive means the property applies to all instances of the defined thing and only to them.
4
Examine the commentator's response
He restricts the purpose to the co-extensive purpose (ghaya musawiya). This means the purpose considered must belong only to that science.
This saves the definition from being too broad.
5
Apply the condition to another science
Consider the science of grammar (nahw). Its purpose might be 'to avoid error in speech'. Is this exclusive to grammar? Possibly also to other sciences like rhetoric? Test the condition.
This exercise reinforces the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و علم من هذا تعريف المنطق أي بالرسم لأن إثبات الاحتياج إليه هو أن يبين أن الناس في أي شيء يحتاجون إليه فذلك الشيء يكون غايته وعرضه ويحصل بذلك معرفة العلم بغايته وهي تصوره برسمه. لا يقال أن تعريف الشيء بخاصته البيّنة الشاملة وتلك الخاصة لا تكون إلا مساوية وغاية الشيء يجوز أن تكون أعم منه لجواز أن يكون الأمر الواحد غاية لأمور متعددة. لنا نقول: المراد بالغاية الغاية المساوية، فلا محذور.
His statement: 'And it is known from this the definition of logic' i.e. by description (rasm), because proving the need for it is to show that people need it for something, so that something becomes its purpose and property, and thereby knowledge of the science is obtained through its purpose, which is its conception by description. It should not be said that defining a thing by its evident and comprehensive property, and that property cannot be but co-extensive, while the purpose of a thing may be broader than it, because it is possible that one thing is the purpose of multiple things. We say: What is meant by purpose is the co-extensive purpose, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement that the definition of logic has been learned 'from this'—meaning from the previous discussion about the need for logic. The commentator clarifies that this definition is a rasm (definition by description), not a hadd (essential definition). The reason is that by demonstrating people's need for logic in acquiring theoretical sciences, one identifies the purpose (ghaya) of logic and its property ('arad). This gives a conception of logic through its purpose, which is a form of descriptive definition. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: a property used in a rasm must be necessarily co-extensive (musawiya) with the defined thing, but a purpose (ghaya) might be broader, since one purpose could serve many sciences. The answer is that here the purpose intended is the exclusive, co-extensive purpose, not a general one. Thus the definition is valid.

Key Terms
رسم
غاية مساوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator insist that the purpose in the definition of logic is 'ghaya musawiya' (co-extensive purpose)?
A) Because any purpose can define a science.
B) Because a property used in a rasm must be exclusive to the defined thing.
C) Because logic has no essential definition.
D) Because the purpose of logic is unknown.

Key Takeaway
A descriptive definition (rasm) is valid only if its property is co-extensive with the defined thing, as shown in the definition of logic by its exclusive purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'Fiqh' as 'knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidence'. Is this a hadd or a rasm? Identify the genus and differentia or the property used. Check if the property is co-extensive.
?
In the definition of 'Nahw' (grammar) as 'the science by which one avoids error in i'rab', test whether this purpose is exclusive to grammar or also belongs to other sciences like rhetoric or lexicography.

Proving an Essential Accident: The Role of Subject and Type
Author explains how to prove an essential accident for the subject by making the subject or its type the subject of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two methods for proving an essential accident for the subject of a science and apply them to an Islamic science example.

Core Idea
To prove a property of a science's subject, use the subject itself or one of its types.

This lesson explains how to prove that a subject has an essential accident ('araḍ dhātī). The author distinguishes two approaches: taking the subject of the science directly, or taking a species (type) of that subject. This is a key methodological tool in Islamic sciences, especially physics and biology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the science
What is the overall subject of the science under discussion? For physics, it is 'natural body' (jism ṭabī'ī).
2
Recognize the essential accident
What property does the author want to prove belongs essentially to that subject? E.g., 'having a natural place' (lahu ḥayz ṭabī'ī).
3
Choose the direct method
Set the subject itself as the subject of the proposition. E.g., 'Every natural body has a natural place.'
4
Choose the indirect method (type)
Use a type (naw') of that subject as the proposition's subject. E.g., 'Every animal is a body' where animal is a type of natural body.
The passage is cut off, but the intended example likely continues: 'like animal in their statement: every animal is a body.'
5
Confirm the property extends
Ensure that the property proven for the type also holds for the broader subject (if the type truly shares the essential nature).

The example 'natural body' and 'every body has a natural place' is from physics (ilm al-tabi'i). The cut-off phrase 'like animal in' likely continues with a similar example from biology or metaphysics. The point is that the subject of a science can be proven to have certain essential accidents either directly or through its subtypes.

Al-Mishkini's Correction: The Essential Predication of Genus and Species
Author reports and endorses al-Mishkini's correction on predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental predication in definitions, using genus-species examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Genus and species predications are essential accidents, not merely accidental.

In the science of definitions (ḥadd), it is crucial to understand the nature of predication between genus and species. A common mistake is to think that when we predicate the species upon the genus (e.g., 'rational' upon 'animal'), the predication is accidental because the species is not a part of the genus. Al-Mishkini corrects this: both directions of predication are essential accidents. This lesson will help you grasp this subtle but important point.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'urūḍ (predication)
In logic, 'urūḍ refers to the occurrence of a universal upon a subject. Here, we are concerned with the predication of genus on species and species on genus.
The term is often used in discussions of essential vs. accidental attributes.
2
Examine the two examples given
The first example: 'animal' (genus) predicated of 'rational' (species). The second: 'rational' predicated of 'animal'. Recognize that these are two different predication relations.
'Animal' is the genus of the species 'rational' (in the definition of human), and 'rational' is the differentia of the genus 'animal'.
3
Identify the potential mistake
One might think the second predication (species on genus) is accidental because the species is not a part of the genus's definition. But al-Mishkini corrects that both are essential.
Essential accidents are those that derive from the essence, even if they are not part of the definition.
4
Understand why both are essential
The predication of genus on species is essential because the genus is a constitutive part of the species. The predication of species on genus is essential because the species is a determination of the genus; both reveal something about the essence.
Al-Mishkini's insight is that these predications are not merely accidental (like whiteness on a wall) but grounded in the essential nature.
5
Recognize the author's endorsement
The author affirms the correction, calling it 'in the utmost correctness'. This shows that the point is accepted and important for definition theory.
This refinement prevents errors in constructing complete definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصحح المشكيني: عروض الجنس على الفصل كالحيوان على الناطق مثلاً. وعروض الفصل على الجنس كالناطق على الحيوان مثلاً. وهذان ذاتيان وما صححه هذا الاستاذ المرحوم في غاية الوجاهة. التقريب ص ١٩.
And al-Mishkini corrected: the occurrence of the genus upon the species, like 'animal' upon 'rational' for example. And the occurrence of the species upon the genus, like 'rational' upon 'animal' for example. And these two are essential, and what this noble professor corrected is in the utmost correctness. Al-Taqrib p. 19.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement. The author cites al-Mishkini's correction regarding the predication (عروض) of genus (جنس) and species (فصل) upon each other. Some might think that when the genus is predicated of the species (e.g., 'animal' of 'rational'), it is an essential accident because the genus is part of the species. Similarly, when the species is predicated of the genus (e.g., 'rational' of 'animal'), it might be considered accidental because the species is not a part of the genus. Al-Mishkini corrects this: both are essential accidents. The author affirms the correctness of this view.

Key Terms
عروض
الجنس
الفصل

Checkpoint

A) Essential accident
B) Accidental accident
C) Disputed accident
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The predication between genus and species, in both directions, is an essential accident—a key point in definition theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
