The Seven Types of Accidents in Islamic Logic
Shaykh Muhammad Ali enumerates seven types of accidents and classifies them into essential, accidental, and disputed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the seven types of accidents and classify them as essential, accidental, or disputed.

Core Idea
Accidents are classified by attachment modes: three essential, three accidental, one disputed.

In Islamic logic, understanding how accidents relate to their subjects is crucial for defining the scope of a science. Shaykh Muhammad Ali enumerates seven modes of attachment and groups them into essential accidents (ذاتية), accidental accidents (غريبة), and one disputed category. This lesson will help you navigate this classification.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two main divisions
Note that accidents are broadly divided into essential (ذاتي) and accidental (غريبي). The passage then details seven specific ways an accident can attach to its subject.
2
List the three agreed-upon essential types
From the passage, essential accidents attach: (1) by itself (e.g., amazement to man as such), (2) by an equal part (e.g., speech to man because he is rational), (3) by an external equal (e.g., laughter to man because he is amazed).
3
List the three agreed-upon accidental types
Accidental accidents attach via an external: (4) more general (e.g., occupying space to white because of body), (5) more specific (e.g., laughter to animal because of human), (6) separate (e.g., heat to water from fire).
4
Identify the disputed type
The seventh type attaches via a more general part (like genus), e.g., occupying space to man because he is a body. Later scholars consider it essential, ancients consider it accidental.
5
Test your classification on new examples
Given an accident and its subject, determine which of the seven modes applies and whether it is essential or accidental by agreement or disputed.
Use examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن العوارض قسمان: أعراض ذاتية وأعراض عرضية. وتفصيل ذلك: أن ما يعرض للشيء إما أن يكون عروضه له لذاته أو لجزئه الأعم أو المساوي أو لأمر خارج عنه مساو له أو أعم منه أو أخص منه أو مباين له، فذلك سبعة أقسام. ثلاثة منها أعراض ذاتية بالاتفاق: ما كان عروضه له لذاته كالتعجب اللاحق للإنسان من حيث هو هو، أو لجزئه المساوي كالتكلم له لكونه ناطقاً، أو لأمر خارج مساو له كالضحك له لكونه متعجباً. وثلاثة منها أعراض غريبة بالاتفاق على ما قيل: ما يعرض للشيء بواسطة أمر خارج أعم منه كالتحيز اللاحق للأبيض لكونه جسماً، أو أخص منه كالضحك العارض للحيوان لكونه إنساناً، أو مباين له كالحرارة العارضة للماء بالنار أو شعاع الشمس. وواحد منها مختلف فيه: وهو العارض له لجزئه الأعم كالتحيز اللاحق للإنسان لكونه جسماً، والحركة الإرادية العارضة له لكونه حيواناً. فذهب المتأخرون إلى كونه من الأعراض الذاتية، والقدماء إلى أنه من الأعراض الغريبة، وتبعهم جماعة من محققي المتأخرين. وتفسير المحشي للعرض الذاتي مبني على قولهم. وههنا كلام لا يسع المقام. ثم إنما لم يبحث في العلم إلا عن العوارض الذاتية، لأن المقصود فيه بيان أحوال موضوعه، والعوارض الذاتية للشيء أحوال في الحقيقة. وأما العوارض الغريبة فهي بالحقيقة أحوال للأشياء الأخرى التي هي أعراض ذاتية لها، فينبغي أن يبحث عنها في العلوم التي موضوعها تلك الأشياء. (ميرزا محمد علي)
Know that accidents are two divisions: essential accidents and accidental accidents. The detail of that: what occurs to a thing either occurs to it by itself, or by its more general part, or by its equal part, or by an external thing equal to it, or more general than it, or more specific than it, or separate from it. So that is seven divisions. Three of them are essential accidents by agreement: what occurs to it by itself, like amazement attaching to man insofar as he is man; or by its equal part, like speech for him because he is rational; or by an external equal thing, like laughter for him because he is amazed. And three of them are accidental accidents by agreement, according to what is said: what occurs to a thing via an external thing more general than it, like occupying space attaching to white because it is a body; or more specific than it, like laughter occurring to animal because it is human; or separate from it, like heat occurring to water by fire or sunlight. And one of them is disputed: what occurs to it by its more general part, like occupying space attaching to man because he is a body, and voluntary motion occurring to him because he is an animal. The later scholars held it to be among essential accidents, and the ancients that it is among accidental accidents, and a group of later researchers followed them. And the commentator's interpretation of essential accident is based on their view. And here there is a discussion that cannot be accommodated in this context. Then, a science only investigates essential accidents, because the purpose in it is to explain the states of its subject, and essential accidents of a thing are its states in reality. As for accidental accidents, they are in reality states of other things that are essential accidents for them, so they should be investigated in the sciences whose subjects are those things. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive classification of accidents (عوارض). It begins by stating that accidents are divided into essential (ذاتية) and accidental (عرضية). The detail follows: the manner of attachment (عروض) of an accident to its subject can be in seven ways. Three are agreed upon as essential: (1) attached by itself (e.g., amazement to man as such), (2) by its equal part (e.g., speech to man because he is rational, speech being an equal part? Actually here 'equal part' means a part that is coextensive, like the differentia), (3) by an external equal (e.g., laughter to man because he is amazed, amazement being an external equal). Three are agreed upon as accidental (غريبة): (4) via a more general external (e.g., occupying space to white because white is a body), (5) via a more specific external (e.g., laughter to animal because it is human), (6) via a separate external (e.g., heat to water from fire). One is disputed: (7) via a more general part (e.g., occupying space to man because he is a body, or voluntary motion because he is an animal). Later scholars say this is essential; ancients say it is accidental. The passage then explains why sciences only study essential accidents: essential accidents are the true states of the subject, while accidental accidents belong to other subjects.

Key Terms
عرض ذاتي
عرض غريب
جزء أعم
جزء مساوي
أمر خارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Laughter attaching to animal because it is human

D) Voluntary motion attaching to man because he is animal

Key Takeaway
The seven modes of attachment of accidents are divided into three essential, three accidental, and one disputed, with the disputed mode being via a more general part.

Why Sciences Only Investigate Essential Accidents
Shaykh Muhammad Ali explains why sciences only investigate essential accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasoning that a science only investigates essential accidents of its subject, and apply this to determine the proper location of an accident in the encyclopedic structure of Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A science studies only the essential accidents of its subject; accidental accidents belong elsewhere.

The passage concludes with a brief but profound principle: a science only investigates essential accidents, because they are the true states of its subject. Accidental accidents are actually states of other things, so they belong in other sciences. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows its application in the organization of Islamic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of essential accident
Essential accidents attach to the thing by itself, by an equal part, or by an external equal. They are inherent to the thing's quiddity.
Review the previous lesson if needed.
2
Understand the aim of a science (ilm)
The passage states: 'the purpose in it (the science) is to explain the states of its subject.' So each science aims to describe the properties (ahwal) that necessarily belong to its subject matter.
3
Connect essential accidents to the subject's states
Essential accidents are described as 'in reality the states of the thing'. Therefore, if a science wants to study the states of its subject, it must study its essential accidents.
4
Handle accidental accidents
Accidental accidents are in reality states of the external thing through which they attach. For example, heat (accident) attaches to water via fire; heat is an essential accident of fire (or something hot), not of water. Therefore, heat should be studied in the science whose subject is fire, not the science of water.
5
Apply the principle to organize Islamic sciences
Consider the science of physics (ilm al-tabi'i): its subject is body. Essential accidents of body include motion, rest, shape, etc. Accidental accidents like whiteness are studied in optics or something else. This principle ensures each science has a coherent domain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Understanding 'al-amr' in Accident Classification
Muhammad Ali clarifies that 'al-amr' in the classification includes both internal and external things, leaving only two types of essential accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of essential accidents according to Muhammad Ali's refinement and explain why 'al-amr' must include both internal and external causes.

Core Idea
Essential accidents are those caused by an equal thing, whether internal or external.

In this hashiya, Muhammad Ali corrects a common misunderstanding about the classification of accidents. He shows that the term 'al-amr' (the thing) must include both internal and external causes, which narrows down the essential accidents to only two types. This clarification is crucial for correctly identifying essential vs. accidental properties in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original classification context
The passage refers to the six accidents (al-āriḍ al-dhātī) and the condition of equality (musāwāt). Four of them are excluded by this condition.
The original classification is likely from the Shamsiyya or similar text.
2
Understand what 'al-amr' means
Muhammad Ali states that 'al-amr' encompasses both internal (dākhil) and external (khārij) things. Internal means part of the essence (e.g., the differentia), external means something outside the essence.
This is a key refinement: earlier interpretations restricted 'al-amr' to external causes only.
3
See which four are excluded
The four excluded are those via: a more general internal part, a more general external thing, a more specific external thing, and a separate external thing. These fail the equality condition.
Equality means the cause and the subject have the same extension.
4
Recognize the two remaining types
The two essential types are: (1) via an internal equal, e.g., speech attaching to man because he is rational; (2) via an external equal, e.g., laughter via amazement.
Both are essential because the cause is equal (musāwin) to the subject.
5
Note the criticism
Muhammad Ali criticizes those who interpret 'al-amr' as only external, stating it is incorrect.
This shows the importance of precise terminology in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أراد بالأمر ما يعم الداخل والخارج فيشمل الأعراض الستة، وخرج بقيد المساواة أربعة منها وهي ما يكون بواسطة جزء أعم أو شيء خارج أعم أو أخص أو مباين، فيبقى اثنان منها: الأول ما يكون بواسطة أمر داخل مساو كالتكلم اللاحق للإنسان من حيث إنه ناطق، والثاني ما يكون بواسطة أمر خارج مساو كالمثال الذي ذكره المحشي. ومن هنا يعلم أن تفسير الأمر المساوي بالخارج فقط ليس كما ينبغي. (محمد علي)
He intended by 'al-amr' that which encompasses the internal and the external, so it includes the six accidents. And by the condition of equality, four of them are excluded: those that are by way of a more general part, or an external thing that is more general, more specific, or separate. So two remain: the first is that which is by way of an internal equal thing, like speech attaching to man insofar as he is rational; the second is that which is by way of an external equal thing, like the example that the commentator mentioned. From here it is known that the interpretation of 'the equal thing' as only external is not as it should be. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement. The author (Muhammad Ali) comments on the classification of accidents. He specifies that the term 'al-amr' (the thing) in the original classification means both internal (inside the essence) and external. Thus, when we consider the six accidental types (excluding the one by itself), the condition of 'equality' (musawat) eliminates four types (those via a more general part, external more general, external more specific, external separate). This leaves only two types of essential accidents: (1) via an internal equal (e.g., speech via rationality), and (2) via an external equal (e.g., laughter via amazement). He criticizes the interpretation that restricts 'al-amr' to only external, stating it is incorrect.

الأمر (al-amr)
مساو (musāwin)

Key Takeaway
Essential accidents are those that attach via an equal cause, which can be either internal (part of the essence) or external (a separate property).

Potential vs Actual in Essential Accidents: The Laughter Example
Muhammad Ali refines that 'laughter' in the example is potential, not actual, to avoid objections about its attachment via amazement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between potential (bil-quwwa) and actual (bil-fiʿl) in a definition and explain how this distinction resolves an objection about essential accidents.

Core Idea
Laughter is an essential accident of man but only in potentiality, not actuality.

In this passage, Muhammad Ali refines the classic example of laughter as an essential accident of man. He addresses an objection by clarifying that the laughter in the definition is potential, not actual. This distinction is crucial for understanding how accidents can be essential even if not always manifest. We will walk through his reasoning and see how the potential/actual distinction protects definitions from certain criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being refined
Find the phrase 'laughter that occurs' (الضحك الذي يعرض) in the Arabic. The author is refining the example of laughter as an essential accident (ʿaraḍ dhātī) of man.
The example is from earlier logical works; the author is responding to an objection.
2
Recognize the objection against actual laughter
The objection states: if amazement is actual, it is not an equal cause but more specific (since not all men are actually amazed). If amazement is potential, laughter cannot attach via potential amazement. The author says this objection only applies if laughter is meant as actual.
The key is that the objection assumes actual laughter; the author denies this.
3
See the author's solution: potential laughter
The author states that what is meant by laughter is potential (بالقوة). This means the capacity or disposition to laugh, not the act of laughing itself. Thus, the objection collapses.
Potential laughter is always present in man as a species, so it fits the definition of an essential accident.
4
Analyze the discussion on 'the amazed one' (al-mutaʿajjib)
The author then examines whether 'the amazed one' refers to a concept distinct from its referent or to the referent itself. He rejects both options and leaves the matter open.
This shows the depth of logical analysis; the exact nature of the medium is debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: كالضحك الذي يعرض: اعلم أن المراد من الضحك ما هو بالقوة، فلا يرد ما قيل: إن كان المراد من التعجب التعجب بالفعل لا يكون ذلك بواسطة أمر مساو بل أخص، لظهور أن الإنسان قد لا يكون متعجباً بالفعل. أو التعجب بالقوة لا يصح القول بأن الضحك عارض له بواسطة التعجب، ضرورة أنه إنما يعرض له بواسطة التعجب الفعلي لا القوي. وذلك لأن هذا إنما يأتي لو كان المراد من الضحك الفعلي لا القوي، وليس. فليس بقى ههنا شيء وهو أن المراد من المتعجب إما أن يكون مفهومه المغاير للمصداق كما صرح به المحقق الشريف، أو نفس المصداق. لا سبيل إلى الأول، وإلا لما صح القول بأن عروض الضحك حقيقة له، لظهور أنه عارض للإنسان أولاً وللمتعجب بواسطة كونه محمولاً، وإلا والمتعجب من حيث هو هو لا يتصف بالضحك. ولا إلى الثاني، وإلا لامتنع أن يكون واسطة في العروض بل هو نفس المعروض له. كذا قيل، وللنظر فيه مجال. (محمد علي)
His statement: 'like laughter that occurs': Know that what is meant by laughter is that which is in potentiality, so it is not open to what is said: if what is meant by amazement is actual amazement, then it is not by way of an equal thing but rather a more specific, because it is obvious that man may not be actually amazed. Or if it is potential amazement, it is not correct to say that laughter attaches to him by way of amazement, since necessarily it attaches to him only by way of actual amazement, not potential. That is because this objection only comes if what is meant by laughter is actual, not potential, and it is not. So nothing remains here. And what is meant by 'the amazed one' is either its concept that differs from the referent, as al-Sharif stated, or the actual referent. The first is not possible, for then it would not be correct to say that the occurrence of laughter is truly to him, because it is apparent that it attaches to man first, and to the amazed one by way of it being a predicate; otherwise the amazed one insofar as he is amazed is not described with laughter. Nor the second, for then it would be impossible for it to be a medium in the occurrence; rather it would be the very subject. Thus it is said, and there is room for consideration. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing an objection about the example of laughter. The author explains that 'laughter' in the example (as an essential accident via amazement) is meant as potential laughter (ما بالقوة), not actual laughter. If actual laughter were meant, the objection would arise: if amazement is actual, then it is not an equal cause but a more specific one (since not all man are actually amazed). If amazement is potential, then laughter cannot attach via potential amazement. The author resolves this by stating that the intended laughter is potential. Then he delves into the nature of 'al-muta'ajjib' (the amazed one), whether it is a concept distinct from its referent or the referent itself. He argues against both possibilities, leaving room for further reflection.

Key Terms
بالقوة (bil-quwwa)
بالفعل (bil-fiʿl)
المتعجب (al-mutaʿajjib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the objection that laughter attaches via a more specific cause invalid?
A) Because laughter is actual, not potential
B) Because amazement is potential, not actual
C) Because the intended laughter is potential, not actual
D) Because the intended amazement is actual, not potential

Key Takeaway
The potential/actual distinction protects essential accident definitions from objections of non-universality by allowing the accident to be a capacity rather than a constant manifestation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Majaz in Predication from Figurative Word
Muhammad Ali refines the meaning of 'majaz' as figurative predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish metaphorical predication (majaz fi al-isnad) from figurative word (majaz fi al-kalima) and figurative declension (majaz fi al-i'rab) in logical and usul contexts.

Core Idea
In logic, majaz often means figurative predication, not just a figurative word.

This short but precise note from Muhammad Ali's super-commentary clarifies a key terminological point. When a logic text mentions 'majaz', it does not always mean a figurative word or declension; it may specifically refer to figurative predication (majaz fi al-isnad). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when reading later passages that discuss metaphorical usage in arguments and definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being refined
Find the term 'majaz' in the passage. Muhammad Ali is specifying that here it refers to a particular type.
The term 'majaz' can be ambiguous; this step is about locating it in context.
2
Recognize the two alternatives he rejects
He explicitly excludes 'majaz fi al-kalima' (figurative word) and 'majaz fi al-i'rab' (figurative declension). Make sure you know what each means.
These are common categories in balagha (rhetoric) and Arabic grammar.
3
Extract the accepted meaning
He affirms that the intended meaning is 'majaz fi al-isnad' (figurative predication). Understand that this deals with the attribution of a predicate to a subject, not with individual words or inflection.
This is crucial for interpreting logical propositions where the relation between subject and predicate is metaphorical.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take a sentence like 'Zaid is a lion' (زيد أسد). Is the majaz in the word 'lion' (which is true figuratively) or in the predication (attributing 'lion' to Zaid)? Here it is in the word. Now consider a logical example like 'The world is writing' – if it means the world manifests signs, the majaz may be in the predication.
Practice distinguishing by asking: is the metaphor in the term itself or in how it is attributed?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحتمل أن يكون إشارة إلى كل من الأبحاث المذكورة في الحواشي المسطورة أو إلى جميعها، وأن يكون إشارة إلى أن المراد من المجاز ليس ما يكون في الكلمة أو الإعراب بل ما يكون في الإسناد. فافهم. (محمد علي)
It is possible that it is a reference to each of the discussions mentioned in the recorded glosses, or to all of them, and that it is a reference to the fact that what is meant by 'majaz' is not that which is in the word or declension, but rather that which is in the predication. So understand. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a brief critical refinement. The author, Muhammad Ali, suggests that the phrase 'al-abhath' (the discussions) in the original text might refer either to each individual discussion or to all of them collectively. More importantly, he clarifies that 'majaz' (metaphorical/figurative) in this context should be understood as metaphorical predication (majaz fi al-isnad), not as figurative word (majaz fi al-kalima) or figurative declension (majaz fi al-i'rab). This is a common distinction in the study of balagha and usul.

Key Terms

الإسناد

In this context, 'majaz' specifically denotes figurative predication, not figurative word or declension.

Proving Essential Accidents via a Broader Term
Commentator clarifies proof of essential accidents via broader term with condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for using a broader term (amr a'am) to prove an essential accident and apply it to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Essential accidents are proven via broader terms that must not exceed the science's subject.

In this passage, the commentator refines how essential accidents (al-a'rad al-dhatiyya) are established for a subject. He introduces a crucial condition: the broader term used as a medium must not be more general than the subject of the science. This condition ensures that what is proven is truly essential to the subject under investigation. By examining examples from fiqh and natural philosophy, you will learn to recognize this condition and apply it in your own analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The commentator explains that essential accidents can be proven for a subject either directly or through a broader term (amr a'am). What is the broader term supposed to do?
2
Extract the condition
The condition is explicitly stated: the broader term must not exceed in generality the subject of the science (bishart an la yatajawaza fi al-umum an mawdu' al-ilm). Why is this condition necessary?
If the broader term were more general, it would introduce properties not specific to the subject, thus not essential.
3
Analyze the fiqh example
The jurists say: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Here, 'intoxicant' is the broader term, and the subject of the science (fiqh) includes acts and rulings. Does 'intoxicant' exceed the subject of fiqh? Why is this proof valid?
Intoxicant is a type of substance, and its ruling (forbidden) is an essential accident for it within the scope of fiqh.
4
Analyze the natural philosophy example
The second example: 'Every moving thing with two straight movements must come to rest between them.' Identify the broader term and the subject of natural philosophy. Does the condition hold?
The broader term is 'moving thing with two straight movements', which is within the subject of natural philosophy (bodies and motion).
5
Apply the condition to a new example
Test a statement: 'Every animal is mortal.' If the subject of the science is 'animal', does 'mortal' as a broader term satisfy the condition? What if the science is 'medicine'?
Consider whether the broader term introduces properties that are essential or accidental to the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم كل حيوان فله قوة اللمس و ذلك قد يثبت له ما هو عرض ذاتى وقد يثبت ما يعرضه لامراعم بشرط ان لا يتجاوز فى العموم عن موضوع العلم كقول الفقهاء كل مسكر حرام ويجعل عرضه الذاتي و نوعه موضوع المسألة ليثبت له العرض الذاتى اما يلحقه لامر اعم بالشرط المذكور كقولهم: كل متحرك بحركتين مستقيمتين لابد ان يسكن بينهما، والحاصل أن البحث عن الاعراض ذاتية للموضوع له او يثبت ما هو اعراض ذاتية للاشياء المختصة بالموضوع لها فيندفع ما يقال من ان الاحوال التي تعرض بواسطة الامر الاخص يبحث عنها في العلوم مع انها ليست اعراضاً ذاتية و يجىء مع جواب آخر. (عبدالرحيم)
Their statement: 'Every animal has the power of touch.' And that may establish for it what is an essential accident, or may establish what is accidental to it due to a broader term, on condition that it does not exceed in generality the subject of the science. Like the statement of the jurists: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and they make its essential accident and its species the subject of the issue to establish for it the essential accident. Or what attaches to it due to a broader term under the mentioned condition, like their statement: 'Every moving thing with two straight movements must come to rest between them.' The conclusion is that the investigation is about the essential accidents of the subject for it, or it establishes what are essential accidents for things specific to its subject. Thus it repels what is said: 'The states that occur through a more specific term are investigated in the sciences even though they are not essential accidents.' And there comes another answer. (Abdurrahim)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous commentary (sharh). The commentator explains how essential accidents (al-a'rad al-dhatiyya) are proven for a subject. He begins by discussing an example: 'Every animal has the power of touch.' He notes that what is proven for the animal can be an essential accident or something accidental due to a broader term. The crucial condition is that the broader term must not exceed the subject of the science. He gives two examples from fiqh and natural philosophy. In the first example, the jurists say 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and treat the intoxicant's essential accident (forbiddance) as part of the subject of the legal issue. In the second, they say 'Every moving thing with two straight movements must rest between them' – here the broader term 'moving thing' is used but under the condition. The conclusion is that sciences investigate essential accidents of their subjects or what is essential for things specific to those subjects. This repels the objection that states occurring through a more specific term are investigated even though they are not essential. The commentator ends by noting there is another answer to this objection.

أمر أعم

Key Takeaway
The condition for proving an essential accident via a broader term is that the broader term must not be more general than the subject of the science.

Defining 'Al-Amr' and the Condition of Equality (Musawa)
Commentator refines the definition of 'al-amr' and distinguishes four types excluded by equality condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of accidents excluded by the condition of equality (musawa) and distinguish between internal and external equal causes.

Core Idea
Equality (musawa) is the filter that separates two valid accident types from four invalid ones.

In this passage, the commentator Muhammad Ali refines the definition of 'al-amr' (the cause) by introducing the condition of equality (musawa). This condition is crucial because it determines which of the six types of accidents are valid in a given context. By understanding which types are excluded, we sharpen our ability to assess definitions in kalam and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'al-amr' in the Arabic. The commentator intends it to encompass both internal (dakhil) and external (kharij) causes, thus covering all six accident types.
The initial scope is broad, including all six.
2
Extract the condition (qayd) of equality
The phrase 'خرج بقيد المساواة' indicates that equality is a restrictive condition. It filters out four of the six types.
Equality means the cause and the effect are coextensive in generality.
3
List the four excluded types
The text specifies: by means of a broader part (juz' a'am), broader external (shay' khariji a'am), more specific (akhass), or opposite (mubayin). These are the four that lack equality.
Recite these types in Arabic and English.
4
Identify the two remaining types
Only internal equal and external equal survive. The example given for internal equal is speech (takallum) attaching to man because he is rational (natiq).
Note that 'internal' refers to an essential attribute, 'external' to an accidental cause.
5
Check the commentator's correction
The passage ends with a refutation of a wrong interpretation: that 'equal cause' refers only to external causes. The commentator insists internal equal causes are also valid.
This shows the importance of precision in definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا البحث (۷۶) اراد بالامر ما يعم الداخل والخارج فيشمل الاعراض الستة و خرج بقيد المساواة اربعة منها وهي ما يكون بواسطة جزء اعم اوشيء خارج اعم او اخص او مباين فيبقى اثنان منها: الاول ما يكون بواسطة امر داخل مساو كالتكلم اللاحق للانسان من حيث انه ناطق و الثاني ما يكون بواسطة امر خارج مساو كالمثال الذي ذكره المحشي، ومن هنا يعلم أن تفسير الأمر المساوى بالخارج فقط ليس كما ينبغي. (محمد على)
This research (76). He intended by 'al-amr' what encompasses the internal and the external, so it includes the six accidents. And through the condition of equality, four of them are excluded: those which are by means of a part that is broader, or an external thing that is broader, or more specific, or opposite. So two remain: the first is what is by means of an internal equal — like speech attaching to man in that he is rational; and the second is what is by means of an external equal — like the example mentioned by the commentator (al-muhashi). From here it is known that interpreting 'al-amr al-musawi' as only the external is not as it should be. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous commentary. The commentator (Muhammad Ali) discusses the term 'al-amr' (the thing/cause) in the context of accidents. He says that the preceding commentator (al-muhashi) intended 'al-amr' to include both internal and external causes, thus covering the six types of accidents. However, by adding the condition of equality (musawa), four of those six types are excluded: those occurring through a broader part (juz' a'am), an external broader thing (shay' khariji a'am), a more specific thing (akhass), or an opposite (mubayin). This leaves only two types: (1) that which occurs through an internal equal, such as speech (al-takallum) attaching to man because he is rational; (2) that which occurs through an external equal, as exemplified by the commentator (al-muhashi). The commentator then refutes a possible interpretation: that 'equal cause' (al-amr al-musawi) refers only to external causes. He asserts that this is incorrect because it excludes the internal equal type.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr)
مساواة (musawa)
داخل (dakhil)
خارج (kharij)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Four
C) Six
D) One

Key Takeaway
The condition of equality (musawa) refines the definition of 'al-amr' by excluding four types of accidents, leaving only internal and external equal causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What is Signification (Dalalah)?
Author defines signification and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dalalah in terms of its essential property and explain why conventional assignment (wad') is necessary for signification.

Core Idea
Signification is not in the word itself but in the convention that links it to a meaning.

Logic aims at conceptual and assentive knowledge, not at words. Yet logicians begin by studying words and their significations because they need to clarify how words convey meanings through convention. This lesson unpacks the precise definition of dalalah (signification) and its necessary condition: conventional assignment (wad'). Without wad', words are mere sounds, not signifiers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined. The passage defines 'dalalah' (signification) as a property of something such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of another thing. Locate this definition in the Arabic.
The definition is: 'كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر'.
2
Extract genus and differentia
The genus is 'being of something' (كون الشيء) – a property. The differentia is 'such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of another thing' (بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر). This distinguishes dalalah from other properties.
This is a real definition (hadd) covering all types of signification.
3
Note the condition of wad'
The author states that words only signify if they have been conventionally assigned (wad') to a meaning. Words without wad' have no signification. This is a necessary condition for verbal signification.
Compare with natural signs (e.g., smoke signifies fire) – those are not conventional.
4
Contextualize within logic
The passage explains why logicians discuss words despite logic's goal being knowledge. They do so to assist in understanding technical terms (alfaz mustalaha) that are customary in the discipline.
This is a reminder that the study of words is instrumental, not the core of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله دلالة اللفظ - قد عرفت قريباً ان هدف المنطق هو المعلومات التصوري و التصديق من حيث الايصال الى معلوم تصوری و معلوم تصدیقی. و هذا النوع من الاهداف لا تماس له بعالم الالفاظ و دلالاتها و لكن القوم تعارف عندهم في صدر هذا المقصد البحث عن المفرد والمركب و المتواطى و المشكك و سائر اقران هذه الامور ولذلك التجأوا الى البحث عن بعض خصوصيات الالفاظ ليستعينوا بذلك على ما تعارفوا قصده ودلالة اللفظ هو كونه بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بمعناه فحتماً الالفاظ لا تكون دالة الا اذا كانت موضوعة لمعان والتي لم يطرء عليها الوضع منتفية عنها الدلالة باعتبار أنه ليس هناك معان بازائها حتى تدل او لا تدل و الدلالة من حيث هي دلالة هى كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر. والدلالة التي ترتبط بهذا المقصد هى الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية كما هو اشارة عنوان البحث و هي ثلاثة اقسام : - دلالة المطابقة وهى دلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له وهى الاصل في الدلالات الثلاث. -۲- دلالة التضمن وهى دلالة اللفظ على جزء ما وضع له وهى ادخل الدلالتين التضمنية والالتزامية بالدلالة اللفظية الوضعية. - دلالة الالتزام وهي دلالة اللفظ على ما هو خارج عنه لازم له. المراد من الحد هو التعريف الجامع المانع. اى المباحث المذكورة فى كتب الفن لا جميعها والى هذا يشير قوله : بان يبين معانى الالفاظ المصطلحة.
His statement: 'Signification of the word' - You have recently learned that the goal of logic is the conceptual and assentive information in terms of leading to a conceptual known and an assentive known. And this type of goal has no connection to the world of words and their significations; but the people (the logicians) have a custom at the beginning of this section to research the simple and compound, the univocal and equivocal, and the rest of the peers of these matters, and therefore they resorted to researching some particularities of words in order to seek assistance by that on what they customarily intend. And the signification of the word is its being such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of its meaning; so necessarily words are signifying only if they are conventionally assigned to meanings, and those upon which convention has not occurred are devoid of signification because there are no meanings corresponding to them so that they would signify or not. And signification in itself is the being of something such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else. And the signification that relates to this section is conventional verbal signification, as is indicated by the title of the discussion, and it is three divisions: (1) Signification of mutabaqa (conformity): it is the word's signification of the whole of what it was conventionally assigned to, and it is the original among the three significations. (2) Signification of tadammun (inclusion): it is the word's signification of a part of what it was conventionally assigned to, and it is the stronger of the two significations of inclusion and implication in terms of conventional verbal signification. (3) Signification of iltizam (implication): it is the word's signification of what is external to it and necessary for it. The intended meaning of 'hadd' is the comprehensive and restrictive definition. That is, the discussions mentioned in the books of the art, not all of them, and to this points his statement: 'that he explains the meanings of technical terms.'
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the concept of signification (dalalat), particularly as it pertains to logic. The commentator begins by reminding the reader that the ultimate goal of logic is knowledge (both conceptual and assentive), and that this goal is not directly concerned with words and their significations. However, logicians customarily begin this section by discussing topics like simple and compound terms, univocal and equivocal terms, etc., and thus they need to research some aspects of words as an aid. The core definition is then given: signification of a word is its property such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of its meaning. A word only signifies if it has been conventionally assigned (wad') to a meaning; otherwise it has no signification. Signification in general is defined as the property of something whereby knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else. The relevant type for this discussion is conventional verbal signification (al-dalalah al-lafdhiyyah al-wad'iyyah), and it is divided into three types: مطابقة (mutabaqa) — signification of the whole of the assigned meaning; تضمن (tadammun) — signification of a part of the assigned meaning; and التزام (iltizam) — signification of something external to the assigned meaning but necessarily concomitant with it. The commentator notes that the first is the original (asl) and the second is stronger (adkhal) among the inclusion and implication types. The passage ends with two brief notes: first, the definition of 'hadd' (definition) is the comprehensive and restrictive definition (al-ta'rif al-jami' al-mani'); second, that the intended discussions in the books of the art (mabaahith) are not exhaustive but refer to explaining the meanings of technical terms.

Key Takeaway
Signification requires a conventional relationship between a word and its meaning.

The Three Types of Conventional Verbal Signification
Author divides conventional verbal signification into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dalalat al-mutabaqa, al-tadammun, and al-iltizam, and identify which one is primary and which is stronger in conventional signification.

Core Idea
A word signifies three things: the whole meaning, a part, or a necessary concomitant.

Having defined signification and the necessity of convention, the author now classifies conventional verbal signification into three types: conformity (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). These categories are essential for analyzing how words convey meanings in Islamic texts, from the Qur'an to legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
List the three types
From the passage, identify the three named categories: مطابقة, تضمن, التزام. Write them down in order.
The Arabic terms are given in sequence with descriptions.
2
Define each type
For each type, extract the definition: mutabaqa is signification of the whole assigned meaning; tadammun is signification of a part; iltizam is signification of something external to the meaning but necessary for it.
The passage explicitly defines each.
3
Analyze the hierarchy
Note the author's remarks: mutabaqa is 'the original' (الأصل) among the three. Tadammun is 'the stronger' (ادخل) in terms of conventional verbal signification compared to iltizam. This implies a ranking based on how directly the word relates to the assigned meaning.
'ادخل' here means more closely connected or more properly included in the domain of wad'.
4
Apply with Islamic examples
Test each type with a Qur'anic example: the word 'house' (بيت) signifies the entire building (mutabaqa), the roof (tadammun), and the inhabitants (iltizam, since a house implies inhabitants). Discuss why iltizam is weaker.
Use examples familiar from usul al-fiqh or tafsir.

Conditions of Conventional Verbal Indication
Author presents and explains Qutb al-Din al-Razi's definition of verbal conventional indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of conventional verbal indication according to Qutb al-Din al-Razi and explain why each condition excludes other types of indication.

Core Idea
Conventional verbal indication requires universal understanding based on convention, not context or nature.

In the study of logic, a precise definition of conventional verbal indication is foundational. Al-Qutb al-Razi's definition sharpens the concept by imposing strict conditions: the word must indicate its meaning universally whenever uttered, and understanding must stem from knowledge of the convention. This lesson unpacks those conditions and contrasts them with natural and rational indication, showing why logicians demand rigor over mere customary usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'conventional verbal indication' (al-dalala al-lafziyya al-wad'iyya). Note that the author relies on the general definition of indication.
The definition is not given in full in the matn; it is supplied by al-Qutb.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'indication' (dalala). The differentia includes two conditions: (1) the word is such that whenever uttered, its meaning is understood, and (2) this understanding is due to knowledge of its convention.
The phrase 'whenever uttered' implies universality (al-ijab al-kulli).
3
Analyze what each condition excludes
The condition 'by knowledge of its convention' excludes natural verbal indication (no convention) and rational verbal indication (understanding occurs even without knowledge of convention). The condition 'whenever uttered' excludes cases where understanding depends on a contextual clue.
Logicians require universal indication, unlike grammarians who accept customary understanding.
4
Examine the wording choice
Al-Qutb says 'knowledge of its convention' (bi-ilm wad'ihi) rather than 'knowledge of its convention for its meaning' to avoid restricting the definition to corresponsive (mutabaqa) indication; it should also cover implicative (tadammun) and associative (iltizam) indication.
This subtlety shows the definition's comprehensiveness.
5
Contrast with other disciplines
Logicians require universal indication without contextual clues. Grammarians and usul scholars accept customary understanding even if occasional. This distinction highlights the rigor of logical analysis.
The commentator notes that in iltizam, they also use customary understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وهى تنقسم : لم يعرف الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية في مقام التقسيم كما عرفها القطب و غيره من المحققين اكتفاء على ما علم من تعريف مطلق الدلالة وتقسيمها . قال القطب هي يعنى الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية، كون اللفظ بحيث متى اطلق فهم معناه للعلم بوضعه واحترز بالقيد الاخير عن الدلالة اللفظية الطبعية اذلا وضع فيها اصلاً فلا يكون فهم المعنى من اللفظ ح لاجل العلم به بل لتأدى الطبع اليه عند التلفظ به وعن الدلالة اللفظية العقلية لتحققها حيث لا وضع ولعدم توقفها بالعلم بالوضع لان دلالة اللفظ الموضوع المسموع من وراء الجدار لا يتوقف على العلم بالوضع لاستواء العالم و الجاهل فيه و انما قال: متى اطلق بالايجاب الكلى، لان الدلالة المعتبرة فى هذا الفن ما كانت كلية و اما اذا فهم من اللفظ معنى في بعض الاوقات بواسطة قرينة، فاصحاب هذا الفن لا يحكمون بان ذلك اللفظ دال على ذلك المعنى بخلاف ارباب العربية والاصول فان نظرهم الى مجرد تفاهم العرف و اهل اللسان ولذا عبروا فى الالتزام باللزوم العرفي ايضاً كما سيجيء انشاء الله تعالى. و انما :قال للعلم، بوضعه اى بوضع ذلك اللفظ ولم يقل بوضعه له ای لمعناه، لئلا يختص بالدلالة المطابقية فافهم. (عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'And it is divided': He did not define verbal conventional indication in the context of division as al-Qutb and other scholars defined it, relying on what is known from the definition of absolute indication and its division. Al-Qutb said: 'It—meaning conventional verbal indication—is that the word is such that whenever it is uttered, its meaning is understood due to knowledge of its convention.' He guarded by the last restriction against natural verbal indication, for there is no convention in it at all, so understanding the meaning from the word is not due to knowledge of it but rather due to the nature leading to it upon utterance; and against rational verbal indication, because it is realized where there is no convention and because it does not depend on knowledge of convention, for the indication of a conventional word heard from behind a wall does not depend on knowledge of convention, as the scholar and the ignorant are equal in it. And he only said: 'whenever it is uttered' with universal affirmation, because the indication considered in this art is general. But if a meaning is understood from a word in some instances by means of a contextual indicator, the practitioners of this art do not judge that the word indicates that meaning, unlike the practitioners of Arabic grammar and principles (usul), for their view is mere customary understanding of the people of the tongue, and therefore they expressed in commitment (iltizam) customarily as well, as will come, Allah willing. And he only said 'for knowledge of its convention' (bi-ilm wad'ihi), i.e., the convention of that word, and he did not say 'for knowledge of its convention for it' (bi-wad'ihi lahu), i.e., for its meaning, so that it would not be restricted to corresponsive indication; understand. ('Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage provides a precise definition of verbal conventional indication (al-dalala al-lafziyya al-wad'iyya) as articulated by al-Qutb al-Razi. The definition states that it is the property of a word such that whenever it is uttered, its meaning is understood by virtue of knowledge of its convention. The author explains that the condition 'whenever it is uttered universally' excludes cases where understanding occurs only occasionally due to a contextual clue; in logic, indication must be general. The condition 'by knowledge of its convention' excludes natural verbal indication (where understanding is due to nature, not convention) and rational verbal indication (where understanding occurs without knowledge of convention, e.g., a word heard from behind a wall implying a speaker). The author also notes that al-Qutb said 'knowledge of its convention' (bi-ilm wad'ihi) and not 'knowledge of its convention for its meaning' to avoid restricting the definition to mutabaqa indication; it should include tadammun and iltizam as well. The commentator contrasts the logicians' rigorous approach with that of grammarians and usul scholars, who accept customary understanding even if not universal. This definition is foundational for subsequent discussions on signification.

Key Terms
دلالة لفظية وضعية (dalala lafziyya wad'iyya): Conventional verbal indication: a word that whenever uttered, its meaning is understood due to knowledge of the convention between word and meaning. [Note: This definition from Qutb al-Din al-Razi is standard; later commentators expand on the conditions.]
الايجاب الكلى (al-ijab al-kulli): Universal affirmation: the word must indicate its meaning universally, not just sometimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When it can be understood with the help of a contextual clue
C) When it is a natural sound like 'akh'
D) When it is heard from behind a wall

Key Takeaway
Conventional verbal indication in logic requires universal understanding based solely on knowledge of the word's convention, excluding natural, rational, and context-dependent indication.

The Three Types of Verbal Indication in Islamic Logic
Author provides examples of mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of verbal indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) given an example and identify which type is being used.

Core Idea
One word can indicate its full meaning, a part, or a necessary external correlate.

In Islamic logic, words indicate meanings in three distinct ways. This lesson uses the word 'insan' (human) to illustrate each type, with a crucial caution from the commentator to avoid confusing the full meaning with a part. Understanding these types is essential for precise interpretation of texts in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and the meaning indicated
Start with the example: the word 'insan' indicates 'rational animal'. Note that this is the complete essence of what 'insan' means.
The commentator adds that the word 'tamam' (complete) is used to emphasize that this is the full denotation.
2
Check if the meaning is the whole or a part
Now consider the same word 'insan' indicating only 'animal' alone. This is a part of the full meaning. Recognize that this is tadammun (implicative indication), not mutabaqa.
The caution prevents mistaking a part for the whole.
3
Identify external necessary correlates
Finally, consider 'insan' indicating 'laughter'. Laughter is not part of the definition but is necessarily connected to human nature. This is iltizam (necessary concomitant indication).
The connection must be necessary, not accidental.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take another word from Islamic sciences, such as 'salat' (prayer). Determine: what is its mutabaqa indication (full meaning), tadammun indication (e.g., ruku'), and iltizam indication (e.g., obedience)?
This practice reinforces the conceptual boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كدلالة لفظ الانسان على الحيوان الناطق، وانها اتى بقوله: «تمام»، لئلا يتوهم ان هذا شامل على الدلالة التضمنية فان الجزء لكونه داخلاً في مدلول اللفظ ربما يتوهم صدق الموضوع له عليه. (محمد علی) (۲۰) كدلالة الانسان على الحيوان وحده او على الناطق وحده. (۲۱) قوله او على ما هو خارج عنه لازم له كدلالة الانسان على الضحك مثلاً، وانما سمى
Like the indication of the word 'insan' upon 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal); and he brought his statement 'tamam' (complete) so that it would not be understood that this includes implicative indication, for the part, being inside the denotation of the word, might possibly be understood that it is true of what the word is placed for. (Muhammad 'Ali) (20) Like the indication of 'insan' upon animal alone or upon rational alone. (21) His statement: 'or upon what is outside it but necessary for it, like the indication of 'insan' upon laughter, for example, and he only named...'
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of the three subdivisions of verbal conventional indication: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (implication), and iltizam (necessary correlate). First, the word 'insan' (human) indicates the entire essence 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal) — this is mutabaqa, where the word indicates its full meaning. The commentator notes that the word 'tamam' (complete) was added to avoid confusing this with tadammun, because a part of the meaning (like 'animal') is within the whole and might be mistakenly thought to be what the word is placed for. Second, the word 'insan' indicating only 'animal' or only 'rational' is an example of tadammun, where the word indicates a part of its meaning. Third, the word 'insan' indicating 'laughter' (a necessary property of humans) is iltizam, where the word indicates something external but necessarily connected to the meaning. The text cuts off, but the intention is clear. These examples are standard in Islamic logic for teaching the categorization of conventional indication.

Three Types of Indication and the Perspective Qualifier
Author defines the three types and discusses the need for the 'min haythu' qualifier to avoid overlap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of verbal conventional indication (mutabaqah, tadammun, iltizam) and explain why the 'min haythu' qualifier is needed to avoid overlap.

Core Idea
A single word can indicate its whole meaning, a part, or a necessary consequence—but only if you specify the perspective.

This passage defines the three subtypes of verbal conventional indication: correspondence (mutabaqah), implication by inclusion (tadammun), and commitment to a necessary consequence (iltizam). The commentator then raises a subtle point: without a qualifying phrase that specifies the perspective ('min haythu huwa kathalik'), these definitions would overlap. Understanding this qualifier is essential for precise logical analysis of definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types and their definitions
Find in the passage the definitions of mutabaqah (indication of entire meaning), tadammun (indication of part), and iltizam (indication of external necessary). Note the examples: 'human' for each type.
2
Understand the naming rationale
Notice why the third is called 'iltizam' instead of 'luzum'—it implies effort and is the strongest type of necessary connection. This shows attention to terminological precision.
3
Recognize the overlap problem
If a word can indicate both the whole and a part (e.g., 'human' indicates rational animal and animal), the same utterance can be considered mutabaqah from one perspective and tadammun from another. This creates confusion without a qualifier.
4
Apply the 'min haythu' qualifier
Rewrite each definition by adding 'from the perspective that it is such'. For example: mutabaqah is indication of the entire subject matter from the perspective that it is the entire subject matter. This prevents misclassification.
5
Explain why the matn author omitted the qualifier
The author was giving a division, not strict definitions, and relied on the common practice of using the qualifier in definitions. The commentator points out this omission without criticizing it.

(محمد علی)
Like the indication of the word 'human' on 'rational animal'. And he only said 'complete' to prevent misunderstanding that this includes implicative indication, for the part, since it is included in the meaning of the word, might be mistakenly thought to be the subject matter. (Muhammad Ali) (20) Like the indication of 'human' on animal alone or on rational alone. (21) His saying: 'or on what is external to it and necessary to it, like the indication of human on laughing, for example.' And the first is called mutabaqah (correspondence) because of the correspondence of word and meaning; the second is called tadammun (implication) because the part indicated is within the meaning posited for it; and the third is called iltizam (commitment) because the external thing indicated by the word is necessary for the subject matter. So the effect is named after the cause in all of them. And the word 'iltizam' was chosen for the third over 'luzum' because there is effort (i'mal) involved, and the necessary connection considered in it is the strongest degree of necessary connection, as will be alluded to later, so it was more appropriate. It is not hidden that it was necessary for him to qualify each of these definitions by our saying: 'from the perspective that it is such', so that he would say: mutabaqah is the indication of the word of the entire subject matter from the perspective that it is the entire subject matter; tadammun is its indication of a part of the subject matter from the perspective that it is a part; and iltizam is its indication of the external necessary consequence from the perspective that it is an external necessary consequence. This is to prevent the definitions of the indications from conflicting with each other. For if the word is shared between the part and the whole and it is uttered for the whole and its indication of the part is considered as tadammun, then upon this consideration the same indication would also be called 'indication of the entire subject matter' even though it is tadammun, not mutabaqah; or if it is uttered for the part because it is a subject matter, then the same indication would be called 'indication of a part of the subject matter' even though it is mutabaqah, not tadammun. Similarly, if it is shared between the necessary and the necessary-from and it is uttered for the necessary-from and its indication of the necessary is considered as iltizam, then upon this consideration the same indication would be called 'indication of the entire subject matter' even though it is not mutabaqah but iltizam; or if it is uttered for the necessary because it is a subject matter, then it would be called 'indication of the external necessary' even though it is not iltizam but mutabaqah. And so on if it is shared between each one of the necessary and the necessary-from and the combination together, and it is uttered for the combination and its indication of the necessary is considered as a part of the subject matter, then it would be called 'indication of the external necessary' even though it is tadammun, not iltizam; or if it is uttered for the necessary-from and its indication of the necessary is considered as an external necessary, then it would be called 'indication of a part of the subject matter' even though it is iltizam, not tadammun. But he was not intending to give definitions; rather he intended a division that suggests the definitions, so he omitted some conditions, especially since its fame suffices for the trouble of mentioning it, and their custom is to be satisfied with the qualifier of perspective in definitions. And in this context there are investigations that are not suitable for this concise work; whoever wishes, let him seek from the extended works of the people. And some of the investigators among the commentators and glossators have spared us the trouble of mentioning some of them; may Allah reward their efforts. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the three subtypes of verbal conventional indication: mutabaqah (correspondence), tadammun (implication/containment), and iltizam (commitment/necessary connection). The first is when the word indicates its entire intended meaning, e.g., 'human' indicating the rational animal. The second is when it indicates a part of that meaning, e.g., 'human' indicating animal alone or rational alone. The third is when it indicates something external to the meaning but necessarily connected to it, e.g., 'human' indicating the capacity to laugh (as an essential property). The commentator explains why the third is called iltizam rather than luzum: because iltizam implies a mental effort (i'mal) and the necessary connection here is the strongest type. Then a crucial point is raised: to avoid overlap, each definition should include the qualifier 'from the perspective that it is such' (min haythu huwa kathalik). For example, if a word is ambiguous (mushtarak) between multiple meanings, or if the intended meaning has parts, the same utterance could be considered mutabaqah in one perspective and tadammun in another. The qualifier defines the perspective. However, the author of the matn omitted this because he was not giving strict definitions but a division that hints at definitions, and the custom is to be satisfied with the perspective qualifier in definitions. This shows the precision required in logical terminology.

The Extra Condition for Iltizam Indication
Author argues that iltizam requires an extra condition beyond knowledge of convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish iltizam from mutabaqah and tadammun by explaining why it requires an additional condition to prevent infinite indication.

Core Idea
Without an extra condition, iltizam would indicate infinite consequences—an absurdity.

The commentator draws a sharp contrast among the three types of indication. While mutabaqah and tadammun rely solely on convention, iltizam—which indicates a necessary consequence external to the literal meaning—would lead to an infinite regress without a further restriction. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the logical rationale for the extra condition.

Guided Path
1
Review the three types of indication
Recall that mutabaqah indicates the literal meaning, tadammun indicates a part of that meaning, and iltizam indicates something external but necessarily connected to it. Focus on the fact that iltizam points outside the posited meaning.
2
Identify the infinite regress problem
The author argues: if every external necessary consequence were indicated by iltizam without restriction, a single word would indicate infinite things (e.g., 'fire' would indicate smoke, heat, ashes, etc.). This is impossible, so a condition is needed to limit which external consequence is signified.
3
Contrast with mutabaqah and tadammun
For these two, knowledge of the convention alone suffices. Even if a word has multiple meanings or a composite object with parts, all are understood upon utterance regardless of speaker intention. No extra condition is needed because the indication is confined to the posited meaning or its parts.
4
Determine the nature of the extra condition
The passage indicates that the extra condition must restrict iltizam to specific external consequences—typically those with a strong essential or customary connection. While speaker intention (iradah) is not required for mutabaqah/tadammun, it may play a role in iltizam to select which consequence is intended.
Later commentators often specify the condition as 'i'mal (mental effort) or a recognized necessary connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢٢) قوله في الدلالة الالتزامية لما كان الالتزام عبارة عن دلالة اللفظ على الخارج ولا ريب في ان اللفظ لا يدل على كل امر خارج و الا لزم ان يدل لفظ واحد على معان غير متناهية لعدم التفاوت بينها فلا بد لها من شرط آخر بخلاف المطابقة والتضمن لظهور انه يكفي فيها العلم بالوضع من غير اشتراط بشيء آخر اما الاولى فظاهر و أما الثانية فكذلك ايضاً فان فهم الجزء لازم لفهم الكل وهكذا اذا كان اللفظ موضوعاً لمعان متعددة بوضع على حدة او اذا كان للموضوع له الواحد اجزاء متعددة فانه يفهم منه العلم بالوضع عند اطلاقه جميع هذه المعانى او تلك الاجزاء فيكون دالاً على كل واحد من هذه المعاني مطابقة وعلى كل واحد من تلك الاجزاء تضمناً و ان لم يعلم ان مراد المتكلم ماذا من بين هذه المعانى او تلك الاجزاء ، فان الدلالة ليست بموقوفة على الارادة لظهور ان المعانى انما تفهم من الالفاظ عند الاطلاق و ان لم تكن مرادة للمتكلم وليس لنا لفظ واحد موضوع لكل واحد من معان غير متناهية بوضع غير متناه ولا لفظ واحد موضوع لمعنى مركب من اجزاء غير متناهية حتى يلزم دلالة اللفظ على الامور الغير المتناهية دلالة مطابقية او تضمنية فيلزم اشتراط أمر آخر لتحقق الدلالة فيهما كما في الالتزام. (محمد على)
His saying concerning iltizam indication: Since iltizam is an expression for the indication of the word on something external, and there is no doubt that the word does not indicate every external thing; otherwise a single word would necessarily indicate infinite meanings because there is no distinction between them. So it must have another condition. Contrary to mutabaqah and tadammun, for it is apparent that knowledge of the convention suffices in them without stipulating anything else. As for the first, it is obvious; and as for the second, it is likewise, because understanding the part is necessary for understanding the whole. And if the word is posited for multiple meanings by separate conventions, or if the single subject matter has multiple parts, then from the word upon utterance, all these meanings or those parts are understood by knowledge of the convention, so it indicates each of these meanings by mutabaqah and each of those parts by tadammun, even if one does not know what the speaker intends among these meanings or those parts. For indication is not dependent on intention, because it is apparent that meanings are understood from words upon utterance even if they are not intended by the speaker. And we do not have a single word posited for each of infinite meanings by an infinite convention, nor a single word posited for a meaning composed of infinite parts such that the word would indicate infinite things by mutabaqah or tadammun, thereby necessitating the stipulation of another condition for their realization as in iltizam. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage addresses a key distinction between iltizam indication and the other two types. The commentator argues that for mutabaqah and tadammun, simply knowing the convention suffices for the word to indicate its meaning. For example, if a word is conventionally assigned to a single meaning (or multiple distinct meanings), upon hearing the word, all those meanings are understood regardless of the speaker's intention. However, for iltizam, which indicates something external to the posited meaning (a necessary consequence), an additional condition is needed. Why? Because if any word could indicate any external necessary consequence without restriction, then a single word would indicate an infinite number of them, since any word has many external necessities (e.g., the word 'fire' might indicate smoke, heat, burning, etc.). This would lead to an absurdity. Therefore, iltizam requires that the necessary connection be specifically recognized (e.g., a strong essential or customary connection) and that the word is used with the intention of indicating that specific necessary consequence. In other words, mere knowledge of convention does not suffice for iltizam; there must be a condition that limits the indication to a specific external meaning. This shows the precision of logical analysis: not all types of indication are alike; the conventional and implicative types rely solely on wad', while the necessary type requires an additional cognitive or contextual constraint.

إرادة

The Condition of Entailment in Iltizam: Rational vs Conventional
Definition of iltizam's entailment condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational (ʿaqli) and conventional (ʿurfi) entailment as conditions for signification by entailment (iltizam) and identify which the author accepts.

Core Idea
Iltizam requires that the mind cannot separate the malzum from the lazim, whether by reason or custom.

The author explains that for a word to signify something by entailment (al-dalāla al-iltizāmiyya), there must be a necessary mental connection. This connection can be either rational, as in blindness and sight, or conventional, as accepted by some scholars. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly analyzing how words imply meanings beyond their literal scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for iltizam
Locate the author's statement: 'لابد من اللزوم عقلاً' (entailment must be rational). This is the core condition. Note that he adds 'أو عرفاً' (or conventional), indicating two possibilities.
The condition is that conceiving the malzum necessarily entails conceiving the lazim.
2
Distinguish rational from conventional entailment
Rational entailment (luzum ʿaqli) is a necessary mental connection, e.g., blindness entails sight. Conventional entailment (luzum ʿurfi) is based on custom or common usage, not strict reason.
Some scholars restrict iltizam to rational; others allow conventional. The author seems to accept both.
3
Apply to the blindness example
Blindness is defined as the absence of sight. To conceive of blindness, one must conceive of sight, because blindness is precisely the lack of sight. This is rational entailment.
The author cites Quranic verses where blindness is attributed to eyes to show common usage, but the literal definition remains rational.

قوله ولابد من اللزوم عقلاً بان يمتنع عقلاً تصور الملزوم بدون تصور اللازم كما بين العمى والبصر فان العمى موضوع للعدم المقيد بالبصر والبصر خارج عنه فان اسناده إلى البصر شايع بدون قرينة مجازية. قال الله تعالى: «فانها لا تعمى الابصار ولكن تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور»، ( سورة الحج الايه (۴۶) و قال تعالى: «عميت ابصارهم» الى غير ذلك من النظائر الشائعة والاصل الحقيقة على أن المناقشة فى المثال غیر رضی . جلال الدین) (۲۴) قوله سواء كان هذا اللزوم الذهنى عقلا هذا هو الظاهر من كلام المصنف هنا وصريحه في سائر ،مصنفاته، فيكون إشارة الى ان المعتبر فى الالتزام هو اللزوم بالمعنى الاعم الشامل للعقلى والعرفى و احتمل بعض المحققين من شراح المتن ان يكون اشعاراً الى ان اللزوم المعتبر في الالتزام مما اختلف فيه، فقيل : : ان المراد اللزوم العقلى فقط وقيل : اللزوم الذهنى فقط فقوله: «لابد من اللزوم عقلاً» اشارة الى المذهب الاول وقوله : أو عرفاً » اشارة الى المذهب الثانى فكانه قال: «لابد من اللزوم عقلاً كما ذهب اليه جماعة او عرفاً كما ذهب اليه آخرون قال وعلى هذا لايكون العبارة دالة على ماهو المختار عنده (محمد علی) (۲۵) قوله كالبصر بالنسبة الى العمى : لايقال : البصر جزء مفهوم العمى فلا تكون دلالته عليه بالالتزام بل بالتضمن دالة . لا نا نقول : العمي عدم البصر اعنى: العدم المضاف الى البصر ، لا العدم والبصر، والمضاف اذا اخذ من حيث انه مضاف كانت الاضافة داخلة فيه والمضاف اليه خارجاً عنه وان كان مفهوم العمى هو العدم المضاف الى البصر، كانت الاضافة الى البصر داخلة في مفهومه والمضاف اليه خارجاً عنه فتعقل العدم من حيث أنه مضاف الى البصر، لا يكون بدون تعقل البصر و ان كان البصر خارجاً عن مفهومه. ثم انه اذا كان مفهومه عدم البصر، أى : العدم المقيد بالبصر، فلا يصح اسناده اليه وقد قال الله تعالى: «فانها لا تعمى الابصار و لكن تعمى القلوب التى فى الصدور و قال ايضاً عميت ابصارهم» بدون قرينة على ان المراد بالعمى هو العدم المطلق، هكذا قيل. وفيه انه يدل على ان التقييد بالبصر ايضاً خارج عن مفهوم العمى لانه لوكان داخلاً فيه لم يصح اسناده إلى البصر بدون قرينة فلزم ان يكون العمى عبارة عن مطلق العدم و هو باطل على ان هيهنا قرينة و هى نفس اسناده إلى البصر. (عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'And it is necessary that the entailment be rational, such that it is rationally impossible to conceive of the entailed without conceiving of the necessary concomitant, as between blindness and sight.' For blindness is defined as the absence restricted by sight, and sight is external to it; for its attribution to sight is common without a figurative context. Allah Almighty said: 'For indeed it is not the eyes that become blind, but it is the hearts within chests that become blind' (Surah al-Hajj, verse 46), and He said: 'Their eyes became blind' and other similar common examples. The original principle is the literal meaning, although the discussion about the example is unsatisfactory. (Jalal al-Din) (24) His statement: 'Whether this mental entailment is rational' – this is apparent from the author's words here and explicit in his other works, so it indicates that what is considered in entailment is entailment in the broader sense that includes both rational and conventional. Some esteemed commentators of the text have suggested that it is an indication that the entailment considered in iltizam is a matter of disagreement: it is said that what is meant is only rational entailment, and it is said that only mental entailment is meant. So his statement: 'It is necessary that the entailment be rational' indicates the first view, and his statement: 'or conventional' indicates the second view, as if he said: 'Entailment must be rational, as a group held, or conventional, as others held.' He said: 'On this, the expression does not indicate what is preferred by him.' (Muhammad Ali) (25) His statement: 'Like sight in relation to blindness' – it cannot be said that sight is part of the concept of blindness, so its indication of it is not by entailment but by containment. We say: Blindness is the absence of sight, meaning the absence attributed to sight, not absence and sight. When the attributed is taken as being attributed, the attribution is included in it and the object of attribution is external to it. And if the concept of blindness is the absence attributed to sight, then the attribution to sight is included in its concept and the object of attribution is external to it. Therefore, conceiving of the absence as being attributed to sight is not without conceiving of sight, even though sight is external to its concept. Then, if its concept is the absence of sight, i.e., the absence restricted by sight, then its attribution to it would not be correct, and Allah Almighty said: 'For indeed it is not the eyes that become blind...' and also said: 'Their eyes became blind' without a contextual indicator that what is meant by blindness is absolute absence, as was said. And in this, it indicates that the restriction by sight is also external to the concept of blindness, because if it were included in it, its attribution to sight would not be correct without a contextual indicator, which would necessarily mean that blindness is absolute absence, and that is false, given that there is a contextual indicator here, namely the very attribution to sight. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the nature of signification by entailment (al-dalala al-iltizamiyya). The author begins by stating the condition for iltizam: there must be a rational or conventional entailment such that the mind cannot conceive of the signified (malzum) without also conceiving of the necessary concomitant (lazim). He then illustrates this with the example of blindness and sight. Blindness is defined as the absence of sight, i.e., a privation that is inherently related to sight. Sight is not part of the concept of blindness; rather, it is external to it. However, whenever one conceives of blindness, one necessarily conceives of sight because blindness is the absence specifically of sight. This demonstrates rational entailment. The author cites Quranic verses where 'blindness' is attributed to eyes, but he argues that this is a common figurative usage; the literal meaning involves relation to sight. Next, a commentator (Muhammad Ali) notes a disagreement among scholars: some require only rational entailment, others allow conventional (urf) entailment as well. The original author's phrasing 'either rational or conventional' indicates he includes both. Then another commentator (Abd al-Rahim) defends the example against an objection that sight might be part of the concept of blindness, which would make the signification by containment (tadammun) rather than entailment. He responds by analyzing the concept of blindness as 'the absence attributed to sight', where the attribution is internal but the object of attribution (sight) is external. Hence, conceiving of blindness requires conceiving of sight, but sight is not part of the definition; thus it is a true entailment. The objection that 'blind eyes' would be impossible if sight were part of the concept is rebutted by noting that the phrase relies on a contextual indicator, meaning the literal definition remains intact. This lesson thus clarifies the precise condition for iltizam and defends it against confusion with tadammun.

اللزوم العرفي

C) The concomitant must be a part of the definition of the signified.

Key Takeaway
Iltizam requires a necessary mental connection between malzum and lazim, which can be rational or conventional.
