Distinguishing Iltizam from Tadammun: The Blindness Example
Defense of the blindness example against tadammun confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the signification of 'blindness' to 'sight' is entailment (iltizam) and not containment (tadammun), using the logical analysis of the definition of blindness.

Core Idea
Sight is external to the definition of blindness, yet necessarily conceived of — that is entailment, not containment.

A subtle but important confusion arises between signification by entailment (iltizam) and signification by containment (tadammun). The commentator Abd al-Rahim defends the classic example against the objection that sight might be part of the definition of blindness. This lesson will show you how to analyze such definitions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if blindness is defined as absence of sight, then sight is part of the concept, so 'blindness' signifies 'sight' by containment (tadammun), not entailment. We must examine the definition carefully.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding of the relationship between the term and its presumed parts.
2
Analyze the definition of blindness
Blindness is defined as 'the absence (al-ʿadam) restricted by sight (al-muqayyad bi'l-baṣar).' This means the concept includes the relation to sight, but sight itself (the object of attribution) is external to the concept. The attribution (idafa) is internal, but the object of attribution (mudaf ilayh) is external.
Think of 'absence of sight' as a single unitary concept that inherently points to sight without including sight as a component.
3
See why sight is external
When we conceive of darkness, we conceive of absence of light, but light is not part of the concept of darkness; it is externally required for the conception. Similarly, blindness is the absence specifically of sight, so sight is necessarily conceived of but not included in the definition.
This is the key: necessary conception does not imply inclusion in the definition.
4
Respond to the Quranic evidence
The critic says that if blindness means absence of sight, then 'blind eyes' would be impossible unless blindness is absolute absence. The commentator rebuts: the Quranic attributions are figurative, with a contextual indicator (qarina) that allows it. Thus, the literal definition remains intact.
The presence of a contextual indicator shows the usage is not literal.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the nature of signification by entailment (al-dalala al-iltizamiyya). The author begins by stating the condition for iltizam: there must be a rational or conventional entailment such that the mind cannot conceive of the signified (malzum) without also conceiving of the necessary concomitant (lazim). He then illustrates this with the example of blindness and sight. Blindness is defined as the absence of sight, i.e., a privation that is inherently related to sight. Sight is not part of the concept of blindness; rather, it is external to it. However, whenever one conceives of blindness, one necessarily conceives of sight because blindness is the absence specifically of sight. This demonstrates rational entailment. The author cites Quranic verses where 'blindness' is attributed to eyes, but he argues that this is a common figurative usage; the literal meaning involves relation to sight. Next, a commentator (Muhammad Ali) notes a disagreement among scholars: some require only rational entailment, others allow conventional (urf) entailment as well. The original author's phrasing 'either rational or conventional' indicates he includes both. Then another commentator (Abd al-Rahim) defends the example against an objection that sight might be part of the concept of blindness, which would make the signification by containment (tadammun) rather than entailment. He responds by analyzing the concept of blindness as 'the absence attributed to sight', where the attribution is internal but the object of attribution (sight) is external. Hence, conceiving of blindness requires conceiving of sight, but sight is not part of the definition; thus it is a true entailment. The objection that 'blind eyes' would be impossible if sight were part of the concept is rebutted by noting that the phrase relies on a contextual indicator, meaning the literal definition remains intact. This lesson thus clarifies the precise condition for iltizam and defends it against confusion with tadammun.

The Mutual Entailment of the Three Significations
Author argues that tadammun and iltizam presuppose mutabaqa, addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how tadammun and iltizam depend on mutabaqa and defend this against objections from contextual indicators and grammatical analysis.

Core Idea
Tadammun and iltizam logically presuppose mutabaqa, which remains present even when not intended.

In this lesson, we explore the logical relationship among the three types of conventional signification: mutabaqa (whole), tadammun (part), and iltizam (necessary concomitant). The author asserts that tadammun and iltizam are subsidiary to mutabaqa, and he addresses key objections. By understanding this dependency, you will gain precision in how words convey meaning in Islamic sciences, especially in legal and theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall the definitions: mutabaqa is signification of the whole meaning for which the word is placed; tadammun is signification of a part of that meaning; iltizam is signification of something external but necessarily connected. These are all conventional (wad'iyya).
Example: The word 'house' has mutabaqa for the whole building, tadammun for the roof, and iltizam for the dweller.
2
Understand the claim of dependency
The author states that tadammun and iltizam are 'far' (subsidiary) to mutabaqa. To signify a part or a concomitant, one must first signify the whole. For instance, to understand 'roof' as a part of house via tadammun, you must first have the concept of 'house'.
This is intuitive: a part is defined relative to the whole.
3
Analyze the two scholarly justifications
Some logicians argue that tadammun and iltizam entail wad' (conventional placement), which in turn entails mutabaqa. Others say they are simply subordinate (tabi') to mutabaqa, so they cannot exist without it. The author critiques the second view: if X is subordinate to Y, Y is also dependent on X in a sense. He reinterprets 'subordination' as intentional (bi-hasab al-qasd): the primary intention in word usage is the whole meaning; parts and concomitants are intended secondarily.
The intentional subordination avoids the mutual dependency problem.
4
Address the first objection: contextual indicator
Objection: A word can be used for a part or concomitant with a contextual indicator that diverts from the original meaning, thus tadammun/iltizam exist without mutabaqa. Response: The contextual indicator only prevents intending mutabaqa, not its existence. Knowledge of the wad' ensures mutabaqa is present in the mind even if unintended.
Existence and intention are distinct.
5
Address the second objection: the grammatical verb example
Objection from grammar: A verb's parts are event, relation to agent, and time. Uttering a verb without mentioning the agent yields understanding of the event (tadammun of the event) but not the relation, so tadammun exists without mutabaqa of the whole verb? Response: (1) There is an estimative mutabaqa (if agent were mentioned). (2) The event is not truly understood without proper linguistic context. (3) The relation is not part of the verb's meaning; the verb is only for the event restricted by time.
This preserves the primacy of mutabaqa.

قوله اذ لاشك ان الدلالة الوضعية على جزء المسمى ولازمه فرع الدلالة على المسمى : وذلك لان الدلالة التضمنية هى الدلالة على جزء المسمى و الدلالة الالتزامية هى الدلالة على خارج المسمى ولا ارتياب فى ان الدلالة على جزء المسمى من حيث انه جزء لا يتحقق بدون الدلالة على المسمى وكذلك الدلالة على الخارج عن المسمى من حيث انه خارج ، لا يتحقق بدون الدلالة عليه. قال بعض اهل الصنعة فى بيان استلزام التضمن العدم المأخوذ من حيث هو مضاف اليها لكن الظاهر ان مرادهم من التابعية هي التابعية بحسب القصد ضرورة ان المقصد الاصلى من وضع اللفظ للمعنى دلالته عليه واما على جزئه او لازمه القصودة بالتبعية لا بالتابعية بمعنى التأخر و المسبوقية الا أنه يتجه عليه ح ان التابع فى القصدر بما يوجد بدون المتبوع كما نشاهد فيمن قصد شيئاً فوصل الى بعض المواضع في الطريق ثم رجع قبل الوصول الى المقصود. فان قلت التضمن والالتزام لا يستلزمان المطابقة لانها قد يوجدان كما اذا استعمل اللفظ في جزء الموضوع له او لازمه و نصب قرينة صارفة عن ارادة الموضوع له . قلت القرينة انما تمنع عن ارادة الدلالة المطابقة لا عن وجودها فالدلالة المطابقة متحققة لوجود العلم بالوضع لكنها ليست بمرادة فافهم. فان قلت المشهور بين الجمهور من النحاة ان اجزاء الفعل ثلاث الحدث والنسبة الى فاعلها والزمان، فلواطلق الفعل بدون ذكر الفاعل فلا شبهة في ان الحدث يفهم منه للعلم بالوضع و هل يفهم النسبة إلى الفاعل اولا؟ لاسبيل الى الاول لان فهم النسبة لايكون الا بعد فهم المنتسبين فاذالم يكن الفاعل مذكوراً لم يكن مفهوماً و اذا لم يكن مفهوماً لم تكن النسبة مفهومة فتعين الثاني، فيلزم وجود الدلالة التضمنية بدون المطابقية. قلت اولاً ان هنا مطابقة تقديرية بمعنى انه لو ذكر الفاعل كانت المطابقة متحققة. وثانياً: انا لاتسلم أن الحدث في الصورة المذكورة يكون مفهوماً لان الفهم موقوف على الاطلاق المعتبر فى محاورات البلغاء و ما فرضتم ليس من هذا الباب. وثالثاً: ان الفعل موضوع للحدث المقيد بالزمان والنسبة ليست داخلة في معناه فتامل صحيح جداً. (عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'Since there is no doubt that the conventional signification of a part of the named thing and its necessary concomitant is subsidiary to the signification of the named thing.' That is because signification by containment is the signification of a part of the named thing, and signification by entailment is the signification of what is external to the named thing. There is no doubt that the signification of a part of the named thing, insofar as it is a part, is not realized without the signification of the named thing, and likewise the signification of what is external to the named thing, insofar as it is external, is not realized without the signification of it. Some of the practitioners of the craft said in explaining the entailment of tadammun and iltizam for mutabaqa: Tadammun and iltizam entail the conventional signification, and conventional signification entails mutabaqa, so tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa. Some others said: Tadammun and iltizam are subordinate to mutabaqa, and the subordinate, insofar as it is subordinate, does not exist without the superordinate, so they do not exist without it. But there is a shortcoming: If it were subordinate, then mutabaqa would also not exist without it, because just as the subordinate does not exist without the superordinate, the superordinate, insofar as it is such, does not exist without the subordinate. Yet if what is meant by subordination is posteriority in existence, then the matter would be the opposite, because mutabaqa is subordinate to tadammun, since understanding the part precedes understanding the whole, and understanding some necessary concomitants like properties and dispositions precedes understanding the things to which they belong, for understanding the disposition precedes understanding the privation taken as being attributed to it. But the apparent is that what they mean by subordination is subordination in terms of intention, of necessity because the primary purpose of placing the word for the meaning is its signification of it; as for its part or its necessary concomitant, they are intended by way of following, not by subordination in the sense of posteriority and precedence. However, it is faced that the subordinate in intention can exist without the superordinate, as we see in someone who intends something and reaches some intermediate point on the path and then returns before reaching the intended goal. If you say: Tadammun and iltizam do not entail mutabaqa, because they can exist when the word is used for a part of what it is placed for or its necessary concomitant, and a contextual indicator is set up to divert from intending what it is placed for. I say: The contextual indicator only prevents intending the signification of mutabaqa, not its existence; for the signification of mutabaqa is actualized due to the knowledge of the conventional placement, but it is not intended. So understand. If you say: The well-known view among most grammarians is that the parts of the verb are three: the event, the relation to its agent, and the time. So if the verb is uttered without mentioning the agent, there is no doubt that the event is understood from it due to knowledge of the conventional placement. But is the relation to the agent understood or not? The first is impossible because the understanding of the relation is only after understanding the two relata; so when the agent is not mentioned, it is not understood, and when it is not understood, the relation is not understood. Thus the second is established, and it follows that signification by containment exists without signification by mutabaqa. I say: First, here there is an estimative mutabaqa, meaning that if the agent were mentioned, mutabaqa would be actualized. Second, we do not concede that the event in the mentioned form is understood, because understanding depends on the appropriate unrestrictedness in the discourse of eloquent speakers, and what you have assumed is not of this kind. Third, the verb is placed for the event restricted by time, and the relation is not included in its meaning. So reflect. This is very correct. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the relationship among the three types of signification (dalala): mutabaqa (whole), tadammun (part), and iltizam (necessary concomitant). The author begins by asserting that tadammun and iltizam are subsidiary (far') to mutabaqa, meaning they depend on the prior signification of the whole. For example, to signify a part of 'house' (like roof) by tadammun, one must first signify 'house' itself. Similarly, to signify 'creator' by iltizam from 'world', one must first signify 'world'. The author then presents two scholarly positions on how this dependency works. The first view: tadammun and iltizam entail the conventional placement (wad') and thus entail mutabaqa, since wad' entails mutabaqa. The second view: tadammun and iltizam are subordinate beings (tabi') to mutabaqa, so they cannot exist without it. The author points out a shortcoming in the second view: if X is subordinate to Y, then Y is also dependent on X in some sense (as a superordinate depends on a subordinate). He reinterprets 'subordination' as intentional (bi-hasab al-qasd), meaning that when using a word, the primary intention is the whole meaning; parts or concomitants are intended secondarily. However, the intentional subordinate can exist without the superordinate, as when someone intends to reach a destination but stops at an intermediate point. The author then addresses two objections. First objection: tadammun and iltizam can exist without mutabaqa when the word is used for a part or concomitant with a contextual indicator that diverts from the original meaning. Response: the contextual indicator prevents intending the mutabaqa, not its existence; the mutabaqa signification still exists in the mind due to knowledge of the wad', even if not intended. Second objection: from grammar, a verb's parts include event, relation to agent, and time. When a verb is used without mentioning the agent, the event is understood (by mutabaqa?) but the relation is not, so there is tadammun without mutabaqa. The response gives three points: (1) there is an estimative mutabaqa; (2) the event is not truly understood in such a case because it lacks the proper context of eloquence; (3) the relation is not part of the verb's meaning at all. This lesson thus clarifies the subtle logical dependencies among the three types of signification and defends the primacy of mutabaqa.

Two Views on the Three Significations
Author contrasts common and alternative definitions of the three significations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the majority view from the alternative view on mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam, and identify how intention reclassifies signification.

Core Idea
Intention shifts a signification from tadammun to mutabaqa.

The three types of signification are foundational in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh. However, scholars disagree on their precise definitions. This lesson presents two interpretations: the majority's absolute view and a conditional view that requires primary intention. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyze how words convey meaning.

Guided Path
1
Extract both definitions of mutabaqa
From the passage, find the first definition: 'signification of the entire literal meaning absolutely.' Then the second: 'signification of the entire thing placed for, literally or contextually, on condition of primary intention.' Note the addition of 'hukman' and the condition of intent.
The second view allows for contextual extension (hukman).
2
Compare the definitions of tadammun and iltizam across the two views
In the majority view, tadammun is signification of a part absolutely, and iltizam of a necessary concomitant absolutely. In the alternative view, both require secondary intention (bi'l-taba'). If intended primarily, they become mutabaqa.
3
Apply the alternative view to a concrete case
Consider the word 'bayt' (house) signifying 'room' (part). According to the alternative view, if you say 'bayt' and primarily mean 'room', that is mutabaqa by contextual extension, not tadammun. Under the majority view, it would still be tadammun.
This shows the practical consequence of adding the intention condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم انه اختلف كلماتهم في تفسير الدلالات فالمشهور عند الجمهور ما اشار اليه المحشى (ره) من ان المطابقة دلالة اللفظ على تمام معناه الحقيقى مطلقا سواء كان مراداً منه ام لا والتضمنية دلالته على جزئه مطلقاً ايضاً والالتزامية دلالته على لازمه مطلقا ايضاً و ذهب بعضهم الى ان المطابقة دلالته على تمام ما وضع له حقيقة أو حكماً و التضمنية دلالته على جزئه والالتزامية دلالته على لازمه بشرط ان يكون استعماله فيما وضع له فى الثلاثة ويكون هو مراداً منه بالاصالة و ان قصد الجزء أو اللازم بالتبع وعلى هذا اذا استعمل اللفظ فى الجزء او الخارج اللازم و يراد هو من اللفظ بالاصالة فلا تسمى هذه بالتضمن او الالتزام بل بالمطابقة بخلافه على ما ذكره المحشى من مذهب الجمهور فان الاولى داخلة تحت التضمن والثانية تحت الالتزام كما هو ظاهر. اذا تمهد هذا فنقول : كون المطابقة لازمة للتضمن والالتزام على ما ذكره البعض من تفسير هما ظاهر، فانه لابد ان
Know that their statements differ in the interpretation of the significations. The well-known view among the majority is what the commentator (may God have mercy on him) indicated: that mutabaqa is the signification of the word for its entire literal meaning absolutely, whether it is intended from it or not; tadammuniyya is its signification for its part also absolutely; and iltizamiyya is its signification for its necessary concomitant also absolutely. Some others held that mutabaqa is its signification for the entire thing for which it was placed, literally or contextually; tadammuniyya is its signification for its part; and iltizamiyya is its signification for its necessary concomitant, on condition that in all three the word is used in what it was placed for and is intended from it primarily, and that the part or necessary concomitant is intended by way of following. On this view, if the word is used for the part or the external necessary concomitant and it is intended from the word primarily, then this is not called tadammun or iltizam but rather mutabaqa, contrary to what the commentator mentioned from the view of the majority, for the first (i.e., use for part) falls under tadammun and the second under iltizam, as is apparent. Once this is established, we say: that mutabaqa is necessary for tadammun and iltizam, according to what some mentioned as their interpretation, is apparent; for it must be that...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two distinct interpretations of the three types of signification. The author begins by stating that there is a disagreement. The first view, attributed to the majority (jumhur) and referenced by the commentator (al-Muhashshi), defines mutabaqa as the word's signification of its entire literal meaning (tamam ma'nahu al-haqiqi) absolutely, regardless of whether that meaning is intended or not. Similarly, tadammun is defined as signification of a part of that meaning absolutely, and iltizam as signification of a necessary concomitant absolutely. The key point is that these definitions do not require the word to be used in its original meaning intentionally; they simply describe the semantic relation established by the original placement (wad'). The second view, attributed to 'some' (ba'd), defines mutabaqa as signification of the entire thing for which the word was placed, either literally or contextually (haqiqatan aw hukman). Here, 'hukman' likely refers to a contextual or legal extension. Tadammun and iltizam are defined similarly, but with an important condition: in all three cases, the word must be used in what it was placed for (musta'mal fima wudi'a lahu) and must be intended primarily (muraadan minhu bil-asala). If one uses a word to refer to a part or a necessary concomitant primarily, then that signification is not considered tadammun or iltizam but rather mutabaqa, because the meaning intended matches the word's placement either literally or contextually. This is opposite to the first view, where such usage would still be called tadammun or iltizam. The author then concludes by noting that on the second view, it becomes clear that mutabaqa is necessary for tadammun and iltizam, because they depend on the primary intention of the whole. The passage ends abruptly, but it likely continued to elaborate on this necessity. This lesson highlights how the definition of the three signifiers can shift based on whether the actual usage (isti'mal) and intention (qasd) are taken into account.

Defining Compound and the Verb Objection
Definition of compound and response to objection from verbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the logicians' definition of compound (المركب) to determine whether a given verbal form is simple or compound, and identify the dialectical move of denying that the morphological form is a part of the verbal expression.

Core Idea
A compound word's part must be audible; a verb's morphological pattern is not an audible part.

The logicians define a compound term as one where a part of its expression indicates a part of its intended meaning. This definition, however, faces an objection: verbs like ضرب seem to have two parts—the material root indicating the event and the morphological form indicating time—so they would be compound, a conclusion logicians reject. In this passage, the author examines and refines the definition through dialectical reasoning, ultimately showing that the morphological form is not an 'audible part' and thus verbs remain simple.

Guided Path
1
Restate the definition of compound
Recall the logicians' definition: a word is compound (مركب) if a part of its expression indicates a part of its intended meaning. Write down this definition in your own words.
The definition uses 'part of the expression' (جزء من اللفظ) and 'part of the meaning' (جزء من المعنى).
2
Identify the objection from verbs
The objection: verbs like ضرب have a material part (مادة) that indicates the event, and a morphological pattern (هيئة) that indicates time. Both seem to be parts of the expression that indicate parts of the meaning, so verbs would fall under the definition of compound, which is problematic since verbs are intuitively simple.
This challenges the completeness of the definition.
3
Analyse the attempted defences
The author considers two responses: (i) 'audible' includes estimated or potential parts, and (ii) 'audible' means potentially audible. He critiques these because if the morphological pattern is conceded as a part, it must be actually or potentially audible; otherwise the verb wouldn't be a word. Thus these defences are inconsistent.
The inconsistency is that if the form is a part, it must be audible; if it is not audible, the verb is not a word.
4
Follow the author's resolution
The best answer, the author argues, is to deny that the morphological pattern is a part of the verb at all. Once that is denied, the material part alone does not indicate any part of the meaning (since it is not a complete part), so the verb has no part and is simple. Thus the definition holds.
This resolution preserves the simplicity of verbs and the definition's integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا انهم ارادوا بالمسموع اعم من ان يكون تحقيقاً كما فى رامى الحجارة او تقديراً كما في نحو اضرب فان الفاعل فيه وان لم يكن مسموعاً مرتباً في السمع حقيقة لكنه كذلك تقديراً. او نقول: ان المراد من المسموع، المسموع الشأنى اى ما يصلح ان يكون مسموعاً و مرتباً في السمع و ان لم يكن كذلك بالفعل ولاشك ان كلمة «انت» فى اضرب وكلمة «هو» فى ضرب في نحو زيد ضرب و نظائر هما جزء مسموع باحد هذين المعنيين، هذا. ولا يخفى ما فى هذا الجواب من التهافت فانه اذا سلم كون الهيئة جزء من الفعل الذي هو قسم من اقسام اللفظ لا يمكن ان يقال انها ليست بمسموعة والا لوجب أن لا يعد الفعل من قبيل الالفاظ والاصوات ضرورة انها لا تكون لفظا و ما لا يكون جزئه لفظاً لا يكون كله لفظاً لاستلزام انتفاء الجزء انتفاء الكل. فَالْأَوْلى فِى الجواب ان يمنع كون الهيئة جزء من الفعل و السند ما اشير اليه فحينئذ لا يرد ايضاً ما يتوهم من انا لو سلمنا ان المراد من الجزء الجزء المسموع المرتب فى السمع وان الهيئة الصورية ليست بذلك الصفة نقول :ايضاً ان الجزء المادى من الفعل يدل على جزء المعنى يقيناً فيصدق تعريف المركب على الفعل بهذه الحيثية كما هو ظاهر و ذلك لانه بعد ما ثبت ان الهيئة ليست بجزء من الفعل فلا يكون للفظ الفعل جزء حتى يدل على جزء معناه أو لا يدل فتأمل فان هذا المقام يستصعبه اقوام.
We said: They intended by 'audible' to be comprehensive, whether it is actual, as in the case of a stone-thrower, or estimated, as in the case of 'ضرب' (strike), for the agent in it, even if it is not actually audibly arranged in hearing, it is so estimated. Or we say: What is meant by 'audible' is the potential audible, i.e., what is fit to be audible and arranged in hearing even if not actually so. And there is no doubt that the word 'أنت' in 'اضرب' and the word 'هو' in 'ضرب' in the example 'زيد ضرب' and their like are an audible part in one of these two senses. This. And it is not hidden what is in this answer of inconsistency, for if the form (الهيئة) is conceded to be a part of the verb which is a division of speech, it cannot be said that it is not audible, otherwise it would be necessary that the verb not be considered among words and sounds, because it is not a word, and whatever does not have a part that is a word is not entirely a word, due to the implication of the negation of the part negating the whole. So the best in the answer is to deny that the form is a part of the verb, and the evidence is what was indicated. Then there would not occur also what is imagined that if we concede that what is meant by 'part' is the audible part arranged in hearing, and that the formal form is not of that description, we say: Moreover, the material part of the verb indicates a part of the meaning certainly, so the definition of the compound applies to the verb in this respect, as is apparent. And that is because after it is established that the form is not a part of the verb, then the expression of the verb has no part at all to indicate a part of its meaning or not, so ponder, for this station is difficult for some people.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical objection to the definition of a compound term (المركب). The definition states that a compound is a word where a part of its expression indicates a part of its intended meaning. The objection is that verbs like 'ضرب' would then be compound, because their material root (مادة) indicates the event (حدث) and their morphological pattern (هيئة) indicates the time (زمان). The author first examines two attempts to defend the definition. The first attempt claims that the 'part' of a verb like 'اضرب' includes an estimated agent (أنت) which is heard in a potential sense. The second attempt similarly says that the part is potential. The author critiques these, arguing that if the morphological pattern (هيئة) is conceded as a part, it must be audible, otherwise the verb would not be a word. The best answer, says the author, is to deny that the morphological pattern is a part of the verb at all. Once that is denied, the material part alone does not make the verb compound because the material part does not indicate a part of the meaning on its own; the verb's meaning is simple. The author concludes by asking the reader to ponder this, as it is a difficult point.

Verb Meanings as Simple Forms
The author presents the correct view that verb meanings are simple forms abstracted from three elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the simple form theory of verb meanings from the composite theory and explain how it avoids the earlier objection.

Core Idea
Verb meanings are simple forms abstracted from event, time, and relation, not composites of them.

In the previous discussion, verb meanings were assumed to be composites of event, time, and relation, leading to certain objections. Here, the author presents the correct view: verb meanings are simple forms (hay'a basīṭa) that are abstracted from those three elements. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the logical analysis of verbs in Islamic grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing theories of verb meanings
The passage presents two views: the composite view (that verb meanings are composed of event, time, and relation) and the simple form view (that they are simple forms abstracted from these three). The author states the latter is correct.
2
Extract the three elements abstracted
The simple form is abstracted from three things: the event (al-ḥadath), the time (al-zamān), and the relation to an agent (al-nisba ilā fā`il). Note that these are not components but sources of abstraction.
These three are the same elements that the composite view treats as parts.
3
Understand how this view escapes the objection
The objection was that the verb's meaning would be composite, but under the simple form view, the verb's meaning is a single simple concept indicated by the whole expression. The morphological pattern (ṣūra) does not separately indicate time, nor does the root (mādda) separately indicate the event.
The composite of root and pattern indicates the simple form as a whole.
4
Note the author's promise of further investigation
The author says 'further investigation will come for this, God willing,' indicating that this topic will be explored more deeply later.
This is a standard placeholder in Arabic scholarly works.

ثم لا يخفى ان جميع ما ذكر الى هنا مبنى على القول بان معاني الافعال مركبة من الحدث والزمان والنسبة الى فاعل معين او فاعل ما على خلاف فى ذلك واما على القول بانها هي الهيئات البسيطة المنتزعة عن هذه الثلاثة لا المجموع المركب منها كما هو الحق، فنقول : انا لا نسلم ان الهيئة تدل على الزمان والمادة على الحدث حتى يرد ما ذكر ويحتاج في التفصى عنه الى ما سطر بل نقول : المجموع المركب من المادة و الصورة يدل على و الصورة يدل على الهيئة البسيطة المنتزعة عن الامور المذكورة الثلاثة، فلا محذور حتى يتفصى عنه وليكن هذا على ذكر منك وسيأتى لهذا زيادة تحقيق انشاء الله تعالى.
Then it is not hidden that all that has been mentioned up to here is based on the statement that the meanings of verbs are composed of the event, time, and relation to a specific agent or some agent, with a difference of opinion on that. But as for the statement that they are simple forms abstracted from these three, not the composite whole from them, as is the correct view, we say: We do not concede that the form indicates time and the material indicates the event, such that what was mentioned would occur and one would need to escape from it by what was written. Rather, we say: The composite whole of the material and the form indicates the simple form abstracted from the mentioned three things, so there is no problematic matter to escape from. Commentary
This passage presents an alternative theory of verb meanings. The previous discussion assumed that verb meanings are composite (consisting of event, time, and relation). But the author states that the correct view is that verb meanings are simple forms (هيئات بسيطة) that are abstracted from these three elements, not composed of them. Under this view, the morphological pattern (صورة) does not separately indicate time, nor does the material root (مادة) separately indicate the event. Instead, the entire composite expression indicates the simple meaning as a whole. Therefore, the earlier objections about verbs being compound are irrelevant, because the verb's meaning is a single simple concept, not a composite. The author promises further investigation on this topic.

The Audible Part in the Definition of Compound
A hashya clarifying that the part must be audible, either actually or potentially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of audibility and arrangement to determine whether a candidate part qualifies as a genuine part of a compound word.

Core Idea
A part must be audible and arranged in hearing, actually or potentially.

This hashya refines the definition of 'part' in the definition of a compound. Without this refinement, one might mistakenly consider morphological patterns or implied meanings as parts of a word. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly analyzing compound vs simple words in Arabic grammar and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being refined
The hashya discusses 'part' (الجزء) in the context of compound definition. Determine what counts as a part.
2
Extract the two conditions
The part must be مسموع (audible) and مرتّب (arranged) in hearing.
3
Understand the modalities
The part can be actually audible (e.g., letters) or potentially audible (e.g., implied agent in imperative).
4
Apply to an example
Test the agent part in imperative verbs: In 'اضرب', the agent 'أنت' is considered a potential part, but it does not make the word compound because it is not actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۸) المراد من الجزء، هو الجزء المسموع المرتب فى السمع سواء كان تحقيقاً او تقديراً فيندفع ما قيل من ان ما ذكرتموه فى تعريف المركب يقتضى ان يكون الافعال مركبة فان الجزء المادى فيها بدل على الحدث و الجزء الصورى اعنى الهيئة الحاصلة من الحركات و السكنات وتقديم بعض الحروف و تأخيرها يدل على الزمان فيصدق عليها ان جزء لفظها يدل على جزء معناها دلالة مقصودة فيلزم ان يكون مركبة مع انها ليست بالاتفاق. و وجه الدفع: ان الهيئة ليست مسموعة ولامرتبة ويندفع ايضاً ما يتوهم من ان صيغ الأمر لا تكون مركبة فان الجزء فى كلمة اضرب مسموع تقديراً فانها في قوة اضرب انت. ولقائل ان يقول : ان الهيئة ليست جزء من الفعل، لأن الهيئة بالمعنى المشهور لا تكون لفظاً فلو فرض انها جزء من الفعل لزم أن لا يكون الفعل لفظاً لان المركب من اللفظ وغيره لا يكون لفظاً و اذا لم يكن لفظاً يلزم امتناع البحث من الافعال فى علمى النحو و الصرف و امتناع تقسيم الكلمة الى الفعل و غيره كما لا يخفى لذي الانصاف فتأمل جداً.
What is intended by 'part' is the audible part arranged in hearing, whether actually or potentially. So what was said is repelled: that what you mentioned in the definition of the compound would necessitate that verbs are compound, because the material part in them indicates the event and the formal part, namely the form arising from movements and pauses and the advancement of some letters and their delay, indicates time, so it would be true that a part of their expression indicates a part of their meaning intentionally, so it would follow that they are compound, while they are not by agreement. And the repulsion: that the form is not audible nor arranged. And also what is imagined is repelled: that imperative forms are not compound, because the part in the word 'ضرب' is audible potentially, for it is equivalent to 'ضرب أنت'. And someone may say: The form is not a part of the verb, because the form in the well-known sense is not a word, so if it were assumed to be a part of the verb, it would necessitate that the verb is not a word, because a composite of a word and other than a word is not a word. And if it is not a word, it would entail the impossibility of investigating verbs in the sciences of grammar and morphology, and the impossibility of dividing the word into verb and other, as is not hidden from the fair-minded. So ponder deeply.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This marginal note (hashiya) clarifies the definition of 'part' in the definition of compound. It says part must be audible, either actually or potentially. The objection that verbs are compound is repelled because the morphological form (هيئة) is not audible or arranged. The note also addresses a potential misunderstanding about imperative forms like 'اضرب', saying that its agent is potentially audible. Finally, it presents an alternative argument: the morphological form is not a word, so if it were a part of the verb, the verb would not be a word, which is absurd given that grammar treats verbs as words. The note emphasizes the need for deep reflection.

Key Terms
الجزء المسموع المرتب (al-juz' al-masmū' al-murattab): A part that is audible and arranged in hearing, either actually or potentially.

Key Takeaway
A part in a compound must be audible and arranged in hearing, whether actually or potentially.

Refuting the Claim that Verbs are Compound
A hashya clarifying that the part must be audible, either actually or potentially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that verbs are compound words using the condition that the formal part is neither audible nor a word.

Core Idea
The morphological form is not a word, so a verb remains a simple word.

A common objection in logic is that verbs appear compound because their root and pattern each contribute to the meaning. This hashya shows why that objection fails: the pattern is not audible and not a word. Understanding this refutation is crucial for maintaining the classification of verbs as simple words in Arabic morphology and logic.

Guided Path
1
Restate the objection
The objection claims verbs are compound because the material part indicates the event and the formal part (pattern) indicates time, so a part of the expression indicates a part of the meaning intentionally.
2
Identify the first repulsion
The hashya argues that the morphological pattern (hay'a) is not audible (masmū') nor arranged (murattab) in hearing.
3
Identify the second repulsion
Even if it were arranged, the form is not a word in the well-known sense. Including it as a part would make the verb not a word, contradicting grammar.
4
Apply to imperative verbs
In imperative forms like 'اضرب', the implied agent is potentially audible, but that does not make the verb compound because it is not actual.

The Form (Hay'a) Is Not a Part of a Compound Word
Author defends that compound words are composite despite the form not being heard or ordered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the morphological form (hay'a) is not considered a linguistic part of a compound word and how this defends the definition of 'compound'.

Core Idea
The form (hay'a) is not a word, so it cannot be a part of a compound word.

This passage addresses a subtle challenge to the definition of a compound word. Some argue that words like imperative forms appear simple, but they are actually compound in meaning. Here, the author clarifies that the morphological pattern (hay'a) is not a separate audible part, so compound words remain composite even if the form is not explicitly uttered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'murakkab' (compound) in the passage. Notice that the discussion is about whether a word like 'idrib' is compound even though its form (hay'a) is not a separate audible part.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that the form (hay'a) is not heard and not ordered sequentially, so it cannot be a part of the compound. This challenges the definition.
The objector uses 'hay'a' to refer to the morphological pattern.
3
Follow the author's defense
The author argues that the form is not a linguistic part because it is not a word (lafz). If it were a part, the verb would not be a word (since a word composed of a word and non-word is not a word), leading to absurdities in grammar and morphology.
4
Recognize the implications
The defense preserves the ability to study verbs in nahw and sarf and to divide words into verb, noun, and particle. Without this, the entire science of Arabic grammar would be undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حواشى الحاشية
مركبة مع انها ليست بالاتفاق. و وجه الدفع: ان الهيئة ليست مسموعة ولامرتبة ويندفع ايضاً ما يتوهم من ان صيغ الأمر لا تكون مركبة فان الجزء فى كلمة اضرب مسموع تقديراً فانها في قوة اضرب انت. ولقائل ان يقول : ان الهيئة ليست جزء من الفعل، لأن الهيئة بالمعنى المشهور لا تكون لفظاً فلو فرض انها جزء من الفعل لزم أن لا يكون الفعل لفظاً لان المركب من اللفظ وغيره لا يكون لفظاً و اذا لم يكن نفظاً يلزم امتناع البحث من الافعال فى علمى النحو و الصرف و امتناع تقسيم الكلمة الى الفعل و غيره كما لا يخفى لذي الانصاف فتأمل جداً.
الفعل
Margin of the Hashiya. [They are] compound even though it is not by agreement. And the point of refutation: that the form is not heard and not ordered. And it also refutes what is imagined: that the imperative forms are not compound, for the part in the word 'idrib' is heard in estimation, for it is in force of 'idrib anta'. And one may say: that the form is not a part of the verb, because the form in the well-known sense is not a word. So if it were supposed that it is a part of the verb, it would necessitate that the verb is not a word, because what is composed of a word and something else is not a word. And if it is not a word, then it becomes impossible to investigate verbs in the sciences of grammar and morphology, and impossible to divide the word into verb and other, as is not hidden from one possessed of fairness. So ponder deeply. The verb.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification regarding the definition of 'compound' (مركب) in logic. The author begins by stating that some compound words are considered composite even though they are not composite by agreement (i.e., their composition is not based on a conventional combination of separate words). The objection is that the form (هيئة) – the pattern or shape of the word – is not a separate audible part and is not ordered sequentially like parts of a sentence. The author then refutes a possible misunderstanding: the imperative form 'idrib' (اضرب) might be thought not compound, but its part 'idrib' is heard in estimation because it is understood as 'idrib anta' (كلمة اضرب مسموع تقديراً), showing it is considered composite in potential. Then an objector says the form is not a part of the verb, because the form is not a word. If it were a part, the verb would not be a word (since a word composed of a non-word is not a word), and then verbs could not be studied in grammar and morphology. The author leaves this to deep reflection. The heading 'الفعل' (the verb) appears to mark the next discussion.

لفظ

When is a Verb Composite? Logicians vs Grammarians on Murakkab and Mufrad
Author analyzes remaining issues with verbs like 'yaf'alu' and 'fa'il' regarding composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given Arabic word is logically composite (murakkab) or simple (mufrad) by checking if its parts each indicate a part of the overall meaning.

Core Idea
For logicians, composition is about meaning, not orthography.

In logic, a word is called 'simple' (mufrad) only if no part of it conveys a part of the meaning. This lesson examines how logicians analyze verbs like 'yaf'alu' and participles like 'fa'il' to determine if they are compound. You will learn to apply the logical criterion independently of grammatical conventions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word in question
Examine 'yaf'alu' and 'fa'il' from the Arabic. Note their morphological structure: they have prefixes that are separable letters.
The word 'yaf'alu' has the prefix 'ya-' and the root 'f-'-l'.
2
Isolate the parts that carry meaning
Identify the prefix letters (huruf al-mudari', alif al-mufa'ala) and determine what they indicate: person, number, gender, participation.
For 'yaf'alu', the 'ya-' indicates third person masculine singular; for 'fa'il', the alif indicates dual participation.
3
Determine if each part contributes to the overall meaning
If the prefix independently signifies a grammatical meaning, then the word is composite by the logical definition, because the whole meaning is composed of the root meaning plus the prefix meaning.
The root 'f-'-l' gives the action, and the prefix gives additional specifications.
4
Contrast with the grammatical view
Grammarians may call such words simple because they are inflected with a single declension. However, logicians do not rely on grammatical terminology; they focus on semantic composition.
The passage notes that grammarians' statements are not authoritative for logicians.
5
Apply the test to other examples
Check the definite article 'al-', the feminine 'ta' (ta' al-ta'nith), and the relative 'ya' (ya' al-nisba). According to logicians, these are also compound because they add meaning to the base word.
These are less controversial; the passage says 'no problem' with them being considered compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بقى الاشكال فى نحو يفعل» و «فاعل فان حروف المضارع تدل على الغيبة و التكلم والخطاب و الف المفاعلة تدل على مشاركة الاثنين مع انها مسموعة ومرتبة فى السمع ولا اشكال في مثل المعرف باللام و اللفظ المعروض لتاء التأنيث او ياء النسبة فانها مركبة عند المنطقيين اذ لم يوجد في كلامهم ما يدل على انها مفردة وما يدل عليه عبارة بعض شراح الكافية من ان الامور المذكورة مفردات، فلعله مبنى على التسامح فانها لما كانت معربة بأعراب واحد فكانها مفردات على ان عبارة النحاة لا تكون سنداً للمنطقيين لامكان المخالفة بينهما. (عبدالرحيم)
Then the problem remains concerning words like 'yaf'alu' and 'fa'il', for the letters of the present tense indicate absence, speaking, and address, and the alif of the participle indicates the sharing of two, while they are heard and ordered in hearing. And there is no problem in the definite article 'al-' and the word subjected to the feminine 'ta' or the relative 'ya'?', for they are compound according to the logicians, since there is not found in their speech what indicates that they are simple. And what is indicated by the statement of some commentators of al-Kafiya that the mentioned matters are simple, perhaps it is based on leniency, because since they are inflected with a single declension, they are as if simple. Moreover, the statement of the grammarians is not a proof for the logicians due to the possibility of disagreement between them. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definitional discussion about compound words. The author identifies a remaining problem with verbs like 'yaf'alu' (يفعل, he does) and participles like 'fa'il' (فاعل, doer). In 'yaf'alu', the prefix letters (حروف المضارع) indicate grammatical meanings: the ya indicates third person masculine singular, the ta indicates feminine or second person, the alif indicates dual, etc. Similarly, in 'fa'il', the alif of the participle (الف المفاعلة) indicates mutual participation. These letters are audible and ordered sequentially, so they seem to be parts of a compound word. However, there is no problem with the definite article 'al-', the feminine 'ta' (تاء التأنيث), or the relative 'ya' (ياء النسبة) being considered compound, because logicians treat them as compound, even though some grammarians call them simple. The author notes that the statement of some commentators of al-Kafiya (شراح الكافية) that these are simple may be based on leniency, as they appear simple because they are inflected with a single declension. However, the author emphasizes that grammarians' statements are not authoritative for logicians, because the two disciplines can disagree. This shows the independence of logical analysis from grammatical conventions.

Key Terms
حروف المضارع
مركب
مفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because grammarians say it is simple.

Key Takeaway
Logical composition is determined by whether parts of a word independently convey meaning, not by grammatical inflection.

The Refinement of Compound: Excluding Proper Names
Refining the definition of compound by adding intent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a compound word from a simple word when the word has meaningful parts but is used as a proper name, by applying the condition of intent (qasd).

Core Idea
A word with meaningful parts is compound only if the parts are intended to indicate parts of the meaning.

The basic definition of a compound word requires that part of the word indicates part of its meaning. But what about proper names like 'Abdullah'? They clearly have meaningful parts, yet they are not considered compound in logic. This passage explains why logicians added the condition of intent (qasd) to the definition, and how the distinction between actual meaning (mutabaqa) and inclusive meaning (tadammun) resolves a subtle objection that could otherwise undermine the definition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the original definition of 'compound'
A compound (مركب) was initially defined as a word whose part indicates part of its meaning. This is the third condition mentioned in the source.
This definition is from Aristotle's Organon.
2
Identify the counterexample
Proper names like 'Abdullah' and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' (when used as proper names) satisfy the original definition: their parts ('abd' and 'Allah') do indicate parts of the meaning of the phrase. Yet these are not considered compound words in logic.
Grammarians might treat them as compound, but logicians follow meanings, not just word forms.
3
Understand the added condition: intent (qasd)
To exclude proper names, the condition of intent was added: the speaker must intend the parts to indicate parts of the meaning. In a proper name, the word as a whole denotes a specific individual; the parts are not intended to convey their separate meanings.
This is why 'Abdullah' as a proper name is simple, not compound.
4
Analyze the further objection and its resolution
An objection arises: if a word is simple in one respect (tadammun, inclusive meaning) and compound in another (mutabaqa, actual meaning), it could satisfy both definitions. The resolution is that the definition of compound must be tied to the actual meaning (mutabaqa), not the inclusive meaning. This ensures that words like 'Abdullah' are only compound when the compositional meaning is intended.
Al-Katibi took the condition of correspondence (mutabaqa) in the definition, which resolves the issue.

(عبد الرحيم) (قال الشيخ محمد علی): قوله والثالث ان يدل جزء لفظه الخ» آوردهنا ان اللفظ اذا كان بسيطاً باعتبار معناه التضمني مثلاً مركباً باعتبار معناه المطابق، يصدق عليه كل واحد من تعريف المركب و المفرد بالاعتبارين المذكورين فيلزم عدم الاطراد في واحد منهما كما لا يخفى. والجواب على تقدير اخذ قيد المطابقة في التعريف كما اخذه الكاتبي وغيره ظاهر، ضرورة انه لا يصدق عليه حالا تعريف المركب و هكذا ان لم يؤخذ قيد المطابقة، اذ لابدع فى ان يكون اللفظ مركباً باعتبار و مفرداً باعتبار آخر كما في نحو «عبدالله» و «الحيوان الناطق» باعتبار الوضع العلمي والوضع التركيبي. والحاصل: ان صحة الارادة فى تعريف المركب وعدمه في تعريف المفرد، انما يلاحظان بالنسبة الى امر واحد فان كان ذلك المعنى المطابق فهو مركب لاغير ، او التضمني ففرد لاغير فلا تغفل عن ذلك.
His statement: 'And the third is that part of its word indicates part of its meaning.' Its meaning: Know that what occurred in the first teaching which Aristotle made in this craft initially was sufficiency in this amount in the definition of 'compound'. And when some scholars objected to him with counterexamples of simple words whose part indicates a meaning, like 'Abdullah' and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' as proper names, they added the condition of intent (qasd) to exclude those words. And they did not make them compound, as the statement of the grammarians runs, because their view of words follows meanings, so their simplicity and composition are based on the unity and multiplicity of meanings. (Abd al-Rahim) (Shaykh Muhammad Ali said): His statement 'And the third is that part of its word indicates part of its meaning etc.' is brought here. [The objection:] If a word is simple in respect of its inclusive meaning (tadammuni) and compound in respect of its actual meaning (mutabiq), each definition of compound and simple applies to it in the two respects mentioned, so lack of consistency (ittarad) in each follows, as is not hidden. The answer: on the supposition of taking the condition of correspondence (mutabaqa) in the definition, as al-Katibi and others took it, it is clear, because necessarily the definition of compound does not apply to it at that time. And similarly if the condition of correspondence is not taken, because there is no objection to a word being compound in one respect and simple in another, like 'Abdullah' and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' in respect of the technical (علمي) vs. compositional (تركيبي) convention. The conclusion: that the validity of intention in the definition of 'compound' and its absence in the definition of 'simple' are considered only with respect to one thing. So if that meaning is the actual (mutabiq), then it is compound only; or the inclusive (tadammuni), then it is simple only; so do not neglect that.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of 'compound' (مركب). It begins by explaining the third condition: a compound word must have a part that indicates part of its meaning. Aristotle's original definition only required this, but some scholars objected with examples like 'Abdullah' (عبدالله) and 'al-Hayawan al-Natiq' (الحيوان الناطق) when used as proper names. In those cases, the parts ('abd' and 'Allah', 'hayawan' and 'natiq') do indicate parts of the meaning of the original phrases, but as proper names, the entire word indicates a single individual without compositional meaning. Therefore, the condition of intent (قصد) was added: the composition must be intended, excluding proper names. The author notes that logicians do not follow grammarians in this, because logicians view words as following meanings, so composition is based on whether the meaning is unitary or multiple. Then Shaykh Muhammad Ali presents a further objection: if a word is simple in terms of inclusive meaning (the meaning that includes the parts implicitly) but compound in terms of actual meaning (the explicit composite meaning), it could satisfy both definitions, leading to inconsistency. The answer: if the definition of compound requires that the parts actually correspond to the meaning (قيد المطابقة), then there is no problem, because the word does not satisfy that when considered as a proper name. If not, a word can be compound in one respect and simple in another, like 'Abdullah' when considered as a proper name (technical convention) vs. as a compositional phrase (تركيبي). The conclusion is that the condition of intention in the definition of compound, and its absence in the definition of simple, must be considered relative to a single respect: either the actual meaning (مطابق) makes it compound, or the inclusive meaning (تضمني) makes it simple.

The definition of compound requires that the parts be intended to indicate parts of the meaning; proper names lack this intent and thus remain simple, even if their parts have independent meanings.

Refining 'Intent' in the Definition of Compound
Author resolves an objection about 'intent' in the definition of compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the refined definition of 'intent' (qasd) to distinguish compound from simple expressions in Arabic logic.

Core Idea
Intent in definition of compound is functional suitability, not speaker's mental state.

The definition of compound requires that the compound's parts indicate parts of the meaning. But what does 'intent' mean here? An objection raises two possibilities: actual intent or suitability for intent. The author resolves by specifying that it is suitability at the time of usage. This clarification is crucial for correctly classifying expressions like 'al-hayawan al-natiq' in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What are the two possible interpretations of 'intent' in the definition of compound? Locate the two options in the Arabic: actual intent (القصد بالفعل) and suitability for intent (صلاحية القصد).
2
Examine the problems
Why does each interpretation fail? If actual intent, then unused compounds would not be compound; if suitability, then a compound used as a proper name would wrongly be considered compound.
3
Extract the refined definition
The author's answer: what is meant is the suitability of the part's indication when the whole is intended to indicate that meaning. Not the part's suitability in isolation, but conditionally on the speaker's intent for the whole.
4
Apply to the example
Classify 'al-hayawan al-natiq' in two uses: (a) as a description of human nature (compound) and (b) as a proper name for a specific person (simple). Apply the refined condition to each case.
The word 'غير' means 'except', reinforcing the exclusion of the part's indication when the whole is not intended to convey that meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «لكن هذه الدلالة غير مقصودة» : فان قلت: المراد بالقصد في تعريف المركب اما القصد بالفعل، أو صلاحية القصد، فعلى الاول يدخل المركبات قبل استعمالها والقصد إلى الدلالة الى معانيها في تعريف المفرد و على الثاني يخرج الحيوان الناطق علماً عن حد المفرد لأنه بحيث يصلح لان يقصد بجزئه الدلالة على جزء معناه. :قلت المراد صلاحية دلالة الجزء الدال حين قصد بالدال الدلالة على ذلك المعنى والحيوان الناطق حين ما يقصد به الدلالة على مفهومها فهو داخل في المفرد وكلمة «غير» بمعنى الا ولذا انت مقصودة. (عبد الرحيم) (۴۲) فان معناه الماهية الانسانية مع التشخص والماهية الانسانية مجموع مفهومي الحيوان والناطق فالحيوان الذى هو جزء اللفظ دال على جزء المعنى المقصود الذى هو الشخص الانسانى باعتبار الوضع التركيبي كما مر لانه دال على مفهوم الحيوان و مفهوم الحيوان جزء الماهية الانسانية و هي جزء
His statement: 'But this indication is not intended.' [Objection:] For if you say: What is meant by 'intent' (qasd) in the definition of 'compound'? Either actual intent (al-qasd bi al-fi'l) or suitability for intent (salahiyyat al-qasd). On the first, compounds enter before their usage and the intent for indication to their meanings in the definition of 'simple'? And on the second, 'al-hayawan al-natiq' as a proper name exits the limit of 'simple' because it is such that it is suitable for its part to intend indication to a part of its meaning. I say: What is meant is the suitability of the indication of the part that indicates when the whole is intended to indicate that meaning. And 'al-hayawan al-natiq' when it is intended to indicate its concept, then it falls within 'simple'. And the word 'غير' (ghayr) means 'except' (illa), and therefore you are intended. (Abd al-Rahim) (42) For its meaning is 'human nature' (al-mahiyya al-insaniyya) with individuation (tashakhkhush), and 'human nature' is the sum of the two concepts of 'animal' and 'rational'. So 'animal', which is part of the word, indicates part of the intended meaning, which is the human person (al-shakhs al-insani) in respect of the compositional convention as passed, because it indicates the concept of 'animal', and the concept of 'animal' is part of the human nature, and it is part.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a further refinement of the definition of 'compound' and the condition of intent. It addresses an objection: what exactly does 'intent' (قصد) mean in the definition? Two interpretations are considered: actual intent (القصد بالفعل) or suitability for intent (صلاحية القصد). If actual intent is required, then compound words that have not yet been used would not be considered compound, which is problematic. If suitability is required, then 'al-hayawan al-natiq' when used as a proper name would be considered compound because its parts are suitable for indicating parts of the meaning (e.g., 'hayawan' could indicate animal), even though the speaker does not intend that. The correct answer: what is meant is the suitability of the indication of the part that indicates, at the time when the whole is intended to indicate that meaning. In other words, the part's indication must be suitable when the speaker intends the whole to convey that meaning. For 'al-hayawan al-natiq' as a proper name, if the speaker intends it to indicate the concept of 'rational animal', then it is compound; but if the speaker intends it as a proper name for a specific person (e.g., a nickname), then it is simple because the parts are not being used to indicate their usual meanings. The word 'غير' in the definition (that the indication is not intended) is explained to mean 'except' (إلا), reinforcing the exclusion. Then a final note (42) elaborates: the meaning of the compound word 'al-hayawan al-natiq' when used as a description is 'human nature' with individuation; 'human nature' is the sum of 'animal' and 'rational'. The word 'animal' as part of the expression indicates the concept 'animal', which is part of the intended meaning (human nature), and that is a part of the whole. This confirms the logical composition.

Key Takeaway
The condition of 'intent' in the definition of compound is properly understood as the suitability of the part's indication when the whole is intended to indicate the meaning, not as actual mental intent.

The Role of Intent in Defining Compound Words
Author clarifies that 'intent' refers to suitability for intent, not actual intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a compound phrase used as a proper name is classified as simple or compound according to the logicians' refined definition.

Core Idea
A compound word remains compound even as a proper name.

In Islamic logic, the distinction between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) is foundational. But what about phrases that are compound in form but used as names? A famous example is 'hayawan natiq' as a proper name. The author of this Ḥāshiya refines the definition to clarify that 'intent' means suitability for intent, not actual intent. This lesson unpacks that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard definitions
A simple word (mufrad) is that whose part does not indicate part of its meaning; a compound word's part does indicate part of its meaning. This is the baseline.
If unfamiliar, review the previous lesson on mufrad and murakkab.
2
Identify the problem case
Consider 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal) when used as a proper name for a person. Its parts still carry meaning, so it seems compound. But some might argue that as a name, the parts are not intended to indicate.
This tension motivates the refinement.
3
Understand the two possible meanings of 'intent'
The author presents two readings of qaṣd in the definition: actual intent (al-qaṣd bi-l-fiʿl) or suitability for intent (ṣalāḥiyyat al-qaṣd).
This is the crux of the objection.
4
Apply the correct interpretation
The correct reading is suitability. So even if the speaker does not actually intend the parts to indicate, as long as they are suitable to indicate part of the meaning, the expression is compound. Therefore, 'hayawan natiq' as a name is still compound.
The author explicitly says: 'al-murād ṣalāḥiyyat dalālat al-juzʾ al-dāll'.
5
Test with other examples
Try other compound names like 'ʿAbdallāh'. The parts 'ʿabd' and 'allāh' are suitable to indicate parts of the meaning (servant and God), so it is compound. This test confirms the rule.
This step solidifies understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عبد الرحيم) (۴۲) فان معناه الماهية الانسانية مع التشخص والماهية الانسانية مجموع مفهومي الحيوان والناطق فالحيوان الذى هو جزء اللفظ دال على جزء المعنى المقصود الذى هو الشخص الانسانى باعتبار الوضع التركيبي كما مر لانه دال على مفهوم الحيوان و مفهوم الحيوان جزء الماهية الانسانية و هي جزء المعنى المقصود و جزء الجزء جزء فيكون مفهوم الحيوان ايضاً جزء ذلك المعنى المقصود. (عبد الرحيم)
For you say: The intended meaning of 'intent' in the definition of the compound is either actual intent or suitability for intent. According to the first, compounds before their use enter [the definition], and intending indication to their meanings enters into the definition of the simple. According to the second, 'hayawan natiq' as a proper name exits from the limit of the simple, because it is such that by its part one can intend indication of part of its meaning. I say: What is intended is the suitability of the indication of the indicating part when indication of that meaning is intended by the indicator, and 'hayawan natiq' when indication of its concept is intended is included in the simple. The word 'ghayr' means 'except', and therefore you are intended. (42) For its meaning is the human essence with individuation, and the human essence is the sum of the two concepts 'animal' and 'rational'. So 'animal', which is part of the utterance, indicates part of the intended meaning, which is the individual human, by virtue of the compound designation as explained, because it indicates the concept of animal, and the concept of animal is part of the human essence, and it is part of the intended meaning, and part of a part is a part, so the concept of animal is also part of that intended meaning.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of a compound word (al-murakkab). In logic, a simple word (mufrad) is defined as that whose part does not indicate part of its meaning, while a compound word's part does indicate part of its meaning. But what about a compound like 'hayawan natiq' when used as a proper name (like a name for a person)? Its parts still indicate parts of the meaning (animal and rational), so it would seem to be compound. The author addresses an objection: does 'intent' in the definition mean actual intent or suitability for intent? If actual intent, then a compound not yet used would be excluded. If suitability, then 'hayawan natiq' as a proper name would be considered simple because its part could intend part of the meaning even if not actually intended. The answer is that what matters is the suitability of the indication of the part when the whole is intended to indicate the meaning. In the case of 'hayawan natiq' as a proper name, when it is used to indicate the concept of that name, the part 'animal' does indicate part of the meaning (the human essence), so it is still a compound. The particle 'ghayr' in the definition (meaning 'not') is interpreted as 'except' (illa) to avoid confusion. The second part further explains that the meaning of 'hayawan natiq' as a proper name is the human essence with individuation, and the human essence includes animal and rational, so the part 'animal' indicates part of the intended meaning, confirming it is compound.

Key Takeaway

Restrictive vs Non-restrictive Composition
Explains that compositional phrases can be restrictive or non-restrictive depending on whether the second part is taken as a qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish restrictive from non-restrictive composition in Arabic logical expressions and identify the three types of restrictive composition (idafa, wasf, ta'liq) based on the speaker's i'tibar.

Core Idea
Whether a composition is restrictive depends on the speaker's intention, not just grammar.

In logic, not all phrases are structured the same way. Some combinations are meant to qualify the first part, others are not. This lesson unpacks the distinction between restrictive (taqyidi) and non-restrictive (ghayr taqyidi) composition as defined by the logicians. You will learn to recognise when a phrase functions as a qualification based on the speaker's consideration (i'tibar).

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of restrictive composition
Find where the author defines 'tarkib taqyidi' as a composition where the second part is taken as a qualification (qayd) for the first.
2
Identify genus and differentia
The genus is 'compositional phrase' (murakkab); the differentia is 'the second part is taken as a qualification'.
This is a real definition (hadd haqiqi).
3
List the three types of restrictive composition
The author mentions three forms: idafa (annexation), wasf (description), and ta'liq (attachment). Note that some scholars mistakenly limited it to the first two.
Example: 'ghulam Zayd' (idafa), 'rajul 'alim' (wasf), 'qa'im fi al-dar' (ta'liq).
4
Understand the criterion: i'tibar
The classification is based on the speaker's consideration, not on inherent qualification. A phrase can be restrictive if the speaker intends qualification, even if the second part is not inherently a qualification.
The same phrase can be restrictive in one context and non-restrictive in another.
5
Apply the distinction to new examples
Given a phrase like 'fi al-dar' in a specific context, decide whether it is restrictive or non-restrictive based on the intended meaning.
Practice with Islamic examples: 'salat al-fajr' (restrictive idafa), 'kitab Allah' (non-restrictive if referring to the Book of God as a whole).

قوله ان كان الجزء الثانى قيداً للاول اى أخذ قيداً و ان كان هو بنفسه ليس بقيد له. والمركبات التقييدية تارة تكون بنحو الاضافة و أخرى ينحو الوصفية وثالثة بنحو التعلق . (التقريب ص ۲۱) (۴۸) قوله غلام زيد و .... انما أورد ثلاثة ،أمثلة اشارة الى ان التركيب التقييدي كما يحصل في ضمن الاضافة والوصف كذلك يحصل في ضمن القلق ايضاً فلا وجه لما يظهر من بعضهم من أنه منحصر فى الاولين و من هذا ظهر ما فى بعض الحواشى من ان قوله: «قائم فى الدار» عطف على قوله : فاضل». (محمد علی) (۴۹) قوله ان لم يكن الثانى قيداً للاول: أى لم يؤخذ قيداً و ان كان صالحاً لان يكون قيداً، فان اعتباره قيداً أو ليس بقيد راجع الى المعتبر فما اعتبره قيداً قيل له: «تقييدى» و ما لم يعتبره قيل له: «غير تقييدي ولا خصوصية لمثال : «في الدار» و «خمسة عشير» فى ذلك بعد ان كان المناط هو اعتبار
His saying: 'if the second part is a qualification for the first' i.e., taken as a qualification, even if it is not in itself a qualification for it. Restrictive compositions are sometimes by way of annexation, sometimes by way of description, and thirdly by way of attachment. (48) His saying: 'ghulam Zayd' and ... he only gave three examples as an indication that restrictive composition occurs within annexation and description as well as within [verbal] attachment as well. So there is no basis for what appears from some that it is confined to the first two. And from this appears what is in some glosses that his saying 'qa'im fi al-dar' is conjoined to his saying 'fadil'. (49) His saying: 'if the second is not a qualification for the first' i.e., it was not taken as a qualification, even if it is suitable to be a qualification. For its being considered a qualification or not is up to the one who considers. So what he considers a qualification is called 'restrictive', and what he does not consider is called 'non-restrictive'. And there is no specificity for the example 'fi al-dar' and 'khamsat 'ashar' in that after the criterion is consideration.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage refines the understanding of compositional phrases (al-murakkabat) in logic. The author distinguishes between restrictive (taqyidi) and non-restrictive (ghayr taqyidi) composition. The key is whether the second part is taken as a qualification (qayd) for the first. Even if the second part is not inherently a qualification, it can be considered as such. Restrictive compositions can occur in three forms: annexation (idafa) like 'ghulam Zayd' (Zayd's slave), description (wasf) like 'rajul 'alim' (a learned man), and attachment (ta'liq) like 'qa'im fi al-dar' (standing in the house). The author notes that some scholars mistakenly thought restrictive composition is limited to the first two, but the inclusion of 'qa'im fi al-dar' shows it also includes prepositional phrases. Furthermore, whether a phrase is restrictive depends on the intention of the speaker/user. The same phrase can be restrictive if the second part is intended to qualify the first, or non-restrictive if not. Examples like 'fi al-dar' (in the house) and 'khamsat 'ashar' (fifteen) are given as illustrative; the former can be restrictive or not depending on usage, while the latter is typically non-restrictive. The criterion is purely based on consideration (i'tibar).

Key Terms
تركيب تقييدي
إضافة
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Annexation (idafa)
B) Description (wasf)
C) Attachment (ta'liq)
D) Conjunction ('ataf)

Key Takeaway
The classification of a composition as restrictive or non-restrictive depends solely on the speaker's consideration (i'tibar), not on grammatical form alone.

The Famous Objection: Does the Verb Form Alone Indicate Time?
Author refutes an objection against the definition of kalima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test a logical definition for over-inclusiveness and under-inclusiveness using the example of the definition of 'kalima' (verb).

Core Idea
A good definition must cover all intended cases and exclude all unintended ones.

The definition of 'kalima' (the logical verb) as a word indicating time is subject to a famous objection. This objection exposes the risk of making the definition too broad or too narrow. By examining the dilemma, we learn how to refine definitions by adjusting the roles of matter (madda) and form (hay'a).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'kalima' in the passage. Note that in logic it specifically means verb, not any word as in grammar.
This shift in meaning is important to avoid confusion.
2
Understand the two possible roles of form and matter
The objection considers two possibilities: either the form alone indicates time, or both matter and form together indicate time.
These are presented as an exhaustive disjunction.
3
Test the first horn: form alone
If the form alone indicates time, then words like 'hijr' (which have the pattern of 'nasara') would incorrectly indicate time. This shows over-inclusiveness.
'Hijr' is a proper noun, not a verb.
4
Test the second horn: matter also contributes
If the matter also contributes, then time words like 'ams' (yesterday) and 'al-an' (now) would be verbs, since they indicate time. But they are nouns, so the definition would be under-inclusive (missing the role of form).
'Ams' and 'al-an' are nouns, not verbs.
5
Recognize the resolution
The commentator's description that the matter is 'placed and capable of being acted upon' provides the key: the matter must be a verbal root that can accept inflection. This precise condition excludes both extremes.
The matter must be a root that can be inflected for tense, not any word indicating time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغرض من وصف المادة لكونها موضوعة متصرف فيها ، دفع البحث المشهور في هذا المقام في السنة الاقوام من ان الهيئة ان كانت مستقلة فى الدلالة على الزمان بحيث لا يكون للمادة مدخل فيها يلزم ان تكون الهيئة فى نحو ((جسق)) و ((حجر)) مما هي على هيئة نصر دالة على الزمان أيضاً مع انها لا تدل عليه و ان لم تكن مستقلة بل يكون للمادة ايضاً دخل فيها يلزم ان يكون نحو «امس» و «الآن» مما يدل على احد الازمنة داخلاً في تعريف الكلمة اذ الظاهر ان دلالتها عليها انها معاً لا المادة وحدها والالوجب ان يفهم الزمان منها و أن غير ترتيبها أو حركاتها مع انها ليست من افراد المحدود. ووجه الدفع واضح مما قرره المحشى (ره) فلا حاجة إلى البيان (محمد على)
The purpose of describing the matter as being 'placed and capable of being acted upon' is to repel the famous investigation at this point among the nations: if the form were independent in indicating time, such that the matter has no entry in it, then the form in words like 'jasq' and 'hijr'—which are on the pattern of 'nasara'—would be indicative of time as well, while they are not indicative of it. And if it were not independent, but rather the matter also has an entry in it, then words like 'ams' and 'al-an'—which indicate one of the times—would be included in the definition of 'kalima' (word), because it is apparent that their indication of time is from both together, not from the matter alone. Otherwise, time would have to be understood from them, and their letters and vowels are not from the individuals of the defined. And the manner of repelling is clear from what the commentator (may God have mercy on him) established, so there is no need for explanation.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents a famous objection regarding the definition of 'kalima' (verb) as a word that indicates time through its matter and form. The objection has two horns: (1) If the form (hay'a) alone indicates time, then non-verbs like 'jasq' and 'hijr' (proper names) that happen to have the pattern of 'nasara' would incorrectly indicate time. (2) If the matter (madda) also contributes, then time words like 'ams' (yesterday) and 'al-an' (now) would be verbs, since they indicate time; but they are nouns. The author states that the purpose of the commentator's description is to refute this objection, and the refutation is clear from what the commentator wrote earlier, so he does not repeat it. This shows the logical concern with ensuring definitions are precise and not over- or under-inclusive.

Key Terms
مادة (madda)
هيئة (hay'a)
كلمة (kalima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'hijr' (a name) not considered a 'kalima' (verb) even though it has the form of 'nasara'?
A) Because it is a noun and nouns cannot be verbs.
B) Because its form does not indicate time since it is not a verb.
C) Because the form alone is insufficient; the matter must also be capable of being inflected for tense, which 'hijr' lacks.
D) Because it is a proper noun and proper nouns are excluded from the definition.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'kalima' must specify that the matter is a verbal root that can be inflected for tense; otherwise, the form alone or matter alone lead to over- or under-inclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
