Apply the two-horned test to the definition of 'wajib' (obligatory) as 'what is rewarded for doing and punished for leaving'. Check if it is over-inclusive (e.g., recommended acts that are rewarded but not punished) or under-inclusive (e.g., obligatory acts with no explicit reward/punishment stated).

Two Types of Non-Restrictive Composition
Author explains two examples of non-restrictive composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify a non-restrictive compound by determining whether the first component is inherently non-absolute or whether no actual restriction occurs.

Core Idea
A non-restrictive compound arises either from the first part's inherent non-absoluteness or from the absence of actual restriction.

In this passage, the commentator explains two examples of non-restrictive composition given in the base text. He distinguishes two distinct reasons why the second component does not restrict the first: either the first component is not absolute to begin with, or although it is absolute, no restriction actually takes place in the compound. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analysing compound expressions in logic and language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two examples: 'fi al-dar' (in the house) and 'khamsa 'ashar' (fifteen). Notice that both are compound expressions where the second part does not restrict the first.
2
Analyze the first example: 'fi al-dar'
Here, the first part 'fi' is a particle (harf) that is not absolute because it is set for each individual instance of containment (zarfiyya), not for a universal. Thus, there is no absoluteness to be restricted by 'al-dar'. The second part merely specifies which instance of containment.
The first part is inherently non-absolute.
3
Analyze the second example: 'khamsa 'ashar'
The first part 'khamsa' is an absolute noun (ism), but when compounded with 'ashar', no actual restriction occurs. The compound is a fixed number phrase; the meaning of 'khamsa' is not narrowed by 'ashar' — it simply forms 'fifteen'.
The first part is absolute but no restriction happens.
4
Contrast the two types
In the first, the lack of restriction is due to the first part's nature (non-absolute). In the second, it is due to the fact that the compound is fixed and no real restriction takes place. Recognize that both are non-restrictive but for different reasons.
5
Apply to a new compound
Given a compound, check the first part: is it inherently non-absolute (like a particle set for individuals) or is it absolute but the compound is fixed (like numbers)? If either condition holds, the compound is non-restrictive.
This applies to analyzing definitions and descriptions in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فى الدار و خمسة عشر وانما اتى بمثائين، ليعرفك ان عدم كون الجزء الثاني قيداً للاول اما بان لايكون الجزء الاول مطلقا حتى يقيده الجزء الثانى كالمثال الاول فان لفظة «في» كما قرر في محله موضوعة لكل واحد واحد من جزئيات الظرفية لاكلية فليس فيها اطلاق حتى يحصل فيه التقييد بسبب «الدار» و اما بان لا يحصل فيه التقييد بعد وان كان مطلقا كالمثال الاخير فان كلمة «خمسة» و ان كانت مطلقة لكنها لم تتقيد بسبب تركيبها مع «عشر) كما لا يخفى و ان الجزء الثاني قد يكون قرينة معينة للجزء الأول كما فى الاول وقد لا يكون كما فى الثانى و ان الجزء الاول قديكون حرفاً وقد يكون اسماً وانه قد يكون عاملاً فى الثانى وقد لا يكون و فيه ايضاً رد على من زعم ان غير التقييدى مختص بالمركب من اسم واداة او كلمة و اداة كما يظهر من بعض المحققين في شرح الرسالة و هو ظاهر لكن ان يأتى بمثال آخر مركب من كلمة واداة كـ ان قام مثلاً لكنه تركه اعتماداً على الأولى ظهوره (محمد علی)
His statement: 'in the house and fifteen' — he gave two examples to make you know that the second part not being a restrictor of the first is either because the first part is not absolute such that the second part could restrict it, like the first example, for the word 'fi' as established in its place is set for each individual instance of containment (zarfiyya) individually, not for a universal, so it has no absoluteness such that it could be restricted by 'the house'; or because restriction does not actually occur even though it is absolute, like the last example, for the word 'five' even though it is absolute, it is not restricted due to its composition with 'ten', as is not hidden. And that the second part may be a clarifying context (qarina mu'ayyina) for the first, as in the first example, or may not, as in the second. And that the first part may be a particle (harf) or a noun (ism). And it may be an operator ('amil) on the second or not. And in it there is also a refutation of those who claim that non-restrictive (ghayr taqyidi) is specific to compounds of a noun and a particle or a word and a particle, as appears from some verifiers (muhaqqiqin) in the commentary on al-Risala. And it is apparent, but he could have brought another example composed of a word and a particle like 'in qama' for instance, but he left it relying on the first one being apparent. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a point about non-restrictive composition in compound expressions. The author comments on a base text that gave two examples: 'fi al-dar' (in the house) and 'khamsa 'ashar' (fifteen). He explains why each exemplifies a different way in which the second part does not restrict the first. For 'fi al-dar', the particle 'fi' is inherently non-absolute because it is a particle that signifies containment in a distributed manner (each occurrence individually), so it never takes a restriction. For 'khamsa 'ashar', the word 'khamsa' is absolute but when compounded with 'ashar', no actual restriction happens because the compound is a number phrase fixed in form. The author then notes that the second part may or may not serve as a clarifying context (qarina) for the first. He also refutes a claim by some verifiers that non-restrictive composition is limited to compounds of noun+particle or word+particle; the author argues that the examples show broader possibilities, and although the commentator could have added an example like 'in qama' (if he stood) as a word+particle compound, he relied on the clarity of the first example.

قرينة معينة
جزئيات الظرفية

Key Takeaway
Non-restrictive composition occurs when the first element is inherently non-absolute or when no actual restriction takes place despite the first being absolute.

Three Types of Word-Coinage: Specific, General-for-General, General-for-Specific
Author explains three types of lexical coinage based on the coiner's conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given word-meaning relationship into one of the three types of word-coinage and identify which type applies to pronouns and definite articles according to the preferred opinion.

Core Idea
Word-coinage is not always one-to-one; the coiner may assign a general word to a general meaning or to specific individuals.

In this passage, the author lays the epistemological foundation for understanding how words are coined. Before evaluating definitions or arguments, we must grasp the different ways a word relates to its meaning. The passage presents three types based on the coiner's mental conception. We will unpack each type and then see how this classification applies to common Arabic grammatical elements like pronouns and definite articles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the precondition of coinage
The coiner must conceive a meaning before coining a word because coinage is a relation between two things (word and meaning). Without conception, no relation can be established.
2
Identify the three types based on the coiner's conception
The passage describes three scenarios: (1) Specific conception of a particular meaning → specific coinage. (2) General conception of a general meaning → general coinage for general. (3) General conception but assigning words to the specific individuals under that meaning (using summary knowledge) → general coinage for specific.
The coiner's conception determines the type.
3
Distinguish type 2 from type 3
In type 2, the word is assigned to the universal meaning itself (e.g., the word 'human' for the species). In type 3, the word is assigned to each individual under that universal (e.g., a pronoun like 'I' is assigned to each specific speaker, even though the concept 'speaker' is general).
Summary knowledge of individuals is sufficient for type 3.
4
Apply the classification to pronouns, demonstratives, etc.
The author notes a dispute: do pronouns and the like belong to type 2 or 3? The preferred opinion (according to the author) is type 2: the coinage is general for general. This is why the author included them under 'if its meaning is unified' and excluded them by the phrase 'in coinage'.
The phrase 'if its meaning is unified' refers to the condition for type 2.
5
Check your understanding with examples
Given a word like 'هذا' (this), determine which type applies. According to the preferred view, it is general-for-general because the meaning is a general concept of 'near thing' coined for that general meaning, not for each specific thing individually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم انه لابد لتحقيق الحال من تفصيل المقال بايراد مقدمة ذكرها بعض الافاضل في نظير المقام على نحو من الاجمال غير مفض الى الاخلال وهى انه لاريب ان الواضع اذا اراد وضع لفظ لابد له ان يتصور له معنى والا لامتنع منه ذلك ضرورة ان الوضع أنما هو نسبة بين شيئين، فحينئذ فان يتصور معنى جزئياً وعين بازائه لفظاً مخصوصاً او الفاظاً مخصوصة متصورة تفصيلاً او اجمالاً، يكون وضعاً خاصاً: الخصوص التصور المعتبر فيه اعنى: تصور المعنى، والموضوع له ايضاً خاصاً وهو ظاهر و ان تصور معنى عاماً تحته جزئيات اضافية او حقيقية فله ان يعين لفظاً معلوماً او الفاظاً معلومة بالتفصيل او الاجمال بازاء ذلك المعنى العام ، فيكون الوضع عاماً لعموم التصور المعتبر فيه، والموضوع له ايضاً عاماً وله ان يعين لفظاً معلوماً أو الفاظاً معلومة بالتفصيل او الاجمال بازاء خصوصيات الجزئيات المندرجة تحته لانها معلومة اجمالاً إذا توجه العقل بذلك المفهوم العام نحوها والعلم الاجمالى كاف فى الوضع فيكون الوضع عاماً والموضوع له خاصاً. اذا تمهد هذا فنقول : اختلفوا في تحقيق أن وضع الضماير واسماء الاشارة والموصولات و المعرف باللام وغيرها مما سوى العلم هل هو من القسم الثاني او الثالث؟ وذهب الى كل فريق والمختار عند المصنف لما كان هو الأول اشتمل قوله ان اتحد معناه عليها فاخرجها بقوله وضعاً على ما ذكره المحشى.
Know that for the verification of the situation, it is necessary to detail the discourse by presenting a preface mentioned by some of the virtuous in a similar context, in a concise manner not leading to deficiency: that there is no doubt that when the coiner wishes to coin a word, he must conceive a meaning for it, otherwise that would be impossible, because coining is necessarily a relation between two things. Then, if he conceives a particular meaning and specifies for it a particular word or particular words conceived in detail or in summary, then the coinage is specific: the specificness of the conception is considered, i.e., the conception of the meaning, and the thing coined for is also specific, and that is apparent. And if he conceives a general meaning under which are particular (relative or real) individuals, then he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary corresponding to that general meaning; then the coinage is general due to the generality of the conception considered in it, and the thing coined for is also general. And he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary corresponding to the specificness of the individuals subsumed under it, because they are known in summary when the mind is directed toward them by that general concept, and summary knowledge is sufficient for coinage; then the coinage is general and the thing coined for is specific. When this is established, we say: They differed in investigating whether the coinage of pronouns, demonstratives, relative pronouns, definite articles, and others besides proper names belongs to the second type or the third. Each group held an opinion, and the chosen view according the author is the first, so his statement 'if its meaning is unified' included them, and he excluded them by his statement 'in coinage' as mentioned by the commentator.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological introduction to the classification of word-coinage (al-wadʿ). The author first establishes a preface: for any coinage, the coiner must have a mental conception of a meaning. Then he delineates three possibilities: (1) specific coinage: the coiner conceives a particular meaning and assigns a particular word to it; (2) general coinage for a general meaning: the coiner conceives a general meaning and assigns a word to that meaning as a whole; (3) general coinage for specific individuals: the coiner conceives a general meaning but assigns words to the specific individuals under it, using summary knowledge of those individuals. Then the author applies this to pronouns, demonstratives, relative pronouns, and definite articles, noting the scholarly dispute: do these fall under type (2) or type (3)? The author indicates that the preferred opinion (of the original author, al-Sayyid al-Sharif?) is the first (i.e., type 2), which is why he included them under 'if its meaning is unified' and excluded them by his phrase 'in coinage'. All examples are drawn from Arabic grammar and semantics.

Key Terms
الوضع
الواضع
الموضوع له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a coiner conceives a general meaning but assigns words to the specific individuals under that meaning (using summary knowledge), this coinage is called:
A) Specific coinage (wadʿ khāṣṣ)
B) General coinage for general (wadʿ ʿāmm li ʿāmm)
C) General coinage for specific (wadʿ ʿāmm li khāṣṣ)
D) Impossible coinage

Key Takeaway
Word-coinage can be specific or general depending on whether the coiner's conception is of a particular or universal meaning, and the assignment may be to the universal or to its individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Senses of 'Meaning' in the Division of Singular Terms
Author clarifies the referent of 'meaning' in the classification of singular words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two senses of 'meaning' (al-maʿnā) in the division of singular terms: as the object of coinage (al-mawḍūʿ lahu) and as the actual usage (whether original or interpretative).

Core Idea
'Meaning' in word classification is not fixed; it shifts between original coinage and current usage.

When logicians divide singular words into proper names, equivocals, univocals, etc., they rely on the term 'meaning' (al-maʿnā). But what does 'meaning' refer to here? This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya clarifies that it can mean either the thing for which the word was originally coined (al-mawḍūʿ lahu) or what the word is actually used for in speech, whether by original coinage or by interpretative coinage (waḍʿ taʾwīlī). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying words in Arabic logic and rhetoric.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage is commenting on a statement about the division of singular terms. The division includes categories like proper name (ʿalam), equivocal (mushtarak), univocal (mutawāṭiʾ), etc. The key phrase is 'if its meaning is unified or multiple'.
The exact division list appears in the Arabic: العلم والمتواطىء والمشكك والمشترك والمنقول والحقيقة والمجاز.
2
Locate the two referents of 'meaning'
The author says 'meaning' here refers either to (1) the object of coinage (al-mawḍūʿ lahu) – what the word was originally coined for – or (2) what the word is used for (mā ustuʿmila fīhi), regardless of whether that usage is by original coinage (waḍʿ taḥqīqī) or interpretative coinage (waḍʿ taʾwīlī).
Interpretative coinage includes metaphorical extensions.
3
Distinguish between original and interpretative coinage
Original coinage (waḍʿ taḥqīqī) is the first linguistic convention. Interpretative coinage (waḍʿ taʾwīlī) is a secondary usage based on some relation (e.g., similarity). For example, 'lion' originally coined for the animal (original), but used for a brave man (interpretative).
This distinction parallels the Arabic rhetorical categories of haqīqa (literal) and majāz (figurative).
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take the word 'ṣalāh' (prayer). Originally it meant 'supplication'. But in Islamic law it is used for specific ritual acts. In the division of singular terms, the 'meaning' could be considered either the original supplication (mawḍūʿ lahu) or the legal usage. Depending on which one we take, the classification changes.
This shows the practical importance of the clarification.

Commentary
This passage is a short definitional clarification. The author specifies that in the context of dividing the singular word into types like proper name, equivocal, univocal, etc., the term 'meaning' (al-maʿnā) can have two referents: (1) the actual object of coinage (al-mawḍūʿ lahu) in the original linguistic convention, or (2) the thing for which the word is used in speech, which may be through original coinage or through interpretative/figurative usage. This distinction is important because it affects how we classify words: a word may be considered a proper name based on its original coinage, or a metaphor based on usage. The passage is incomplete, ending with 'as in', but the intended point is clear. All examples are drawn from Arabic rhetorical categories (haqīqa, majāz) and logical divisions of singular terms.

Key Takeaway
In the division of singular terms, 'meaning' is not a fixed concept; it may refer to the original coinage or to the current usage, and this choice affects word classification.

Defining 'Ilm in Logic: Excluding Names of Indication, Indefinite Nouns, and Generic Proper Nouns
Defends the definition of 'ilm against three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish 'ilm (proper noun) in logic from names of indication, indefinite nouns, and generic proper nouns by applying the logical definition.

Core Idea
Not every proper noun in grammar is a proper noun in logic.

The logical definition of 'ilm (proper noun) is precise: it is a noun posited for a specific individual. Yet objections arise: what about names of indication like 'this', indefinite nouns like 'a man', and generic proper nouns like 'Usama' for lion? This passage defends the definition by clarifying the logical criteria and showing where other disciplines (like Arabic grammar) diverge. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and initial objection
The passage starts with an objection: some say 'ilm is used for a universal essence, not individuals. The commentator denies exclusivity, allowing metaphorical use for individuals.
This sets up the need for a precise definition.
2
Extract the author's definition
The definition of 'ilm includes 'with its individualized particularity' (مع تشخصها). The phrase 'positing' (وضعاً) is added to exclude names of indication, which refer to specified particulars but are not posited for them.
The commentator clarifies that without 'positing', names of indication would be included.
3
Analyze the indefinite noun objection
An indefinite noun like 'a man' appears to refer to a particular, yet it is not 'ilm. The distinction is that indefinite nouns allow truth on multiplicity by substitution (بدلية), not by inclusion (شمول). True 'ilm must be true of only one individual with one intention.
Example: the phantom seen from afar is a real particular even if intellect considers multiple possibilities.
4
Analyze the generic proper noun objection
Generic proper nouns like 'Usama' (lion) are proper in grammar but not in logic because their meaning is universal. The author's definition applies only to personal proper nouns. Including them would break the classification (univocal/equivocal) or create an intermediary.
An excuse: logicians consider meaning, grammarians consider verbal rulings.
5
Consider the alternative view
Some argue that generic proper nouns are posited for the unified essence under mental unity, so they could be 'ilm even for logicians. The passage cautions that this is a slippery issue.
This shows the depth of the debate.

It is said: The thing used in both is only the unified universal essence existing within individuals, not the multiple individuals. But we say: We do not accept the exclusivity; rather, just as they are used for the essence existing within individuals, they are also used for those individuals in their particulars, even if metaphorically, so understand. (Mirza Muhammad Ali (may Allah have mercy on him) (72) His statement: 'So there is no need in extracting it...' It is not hidden that according to the first, names of indication and their likes are excluded from being proper nouns according to the author's doctrine by his statement: 'then with its individualized particularity' so his statement 'as a positing' would be redundant on this estimation as well, so that specification by the last aspect is not good, as is apparent from his words, may Allah have mercy on him. And it is not hidden that this is an objection against the author according to the commentator's interpretation of his words, even if we take the statement as usage, contrary to what he brought. Yes, when by the meaning is intended first the real referent, and by the pronoun referring to it from his statement 'with its individualized' the thing used is more general, including real and metaphorical, in the manner of usage, it can be said: his statement 'as a positing' is to exclude names of indication and the like because it is apparent that the thing used in them is a specified particular. Here remain two matters: First, that the indefinite noun by which a particular individual is intended in the manner of substitution and ambiguity, its meaning is one specified particular by positing, yet it is definitely not called a proper noun. It is not said: We do not accept that its meaning is a particular by positing because of occurrence of generality in its meaning and the possibility of its being true of many, as in the meaning of 'man' and other universals, hence a group called it universal in their words. But we say: The truth on multiplicity considered in the particular as non-existent and in the universal as existent is truth on multiplicity in the manner of inclusion, not in the manner of substitution; in other words, it is truth on multiplicity with one application and one intention, not absolutely. There is no doubt that the concept of 'a particular individual' in the manner of ambiguity is not true of multiplicity in the first sense, just as the concept of man is truly applied to it. Hence they ruled that the phantom seen from afar is a real particular, even though intellect applies it to many things, because the actual referent is one person with no multiplicity, and the probability only arises in the intellect due to the referent not being determined. And the application of 'universal' to the indefinite noun in their words is metaphorical, due to observing its generality, treating it as having the status of a universal true of many, or by observing it stripped of tanwin. There is no doubt that in this respect it is universal, not particular, because it is posited for the absolute universal nature. So understand. Second, that the proper noun, just as it may be particular personal, it may also be universal. The summary: The proper noun is either personal like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' for example, or generic like 'Usama' and 'Tha'ala' for the fox, and 'Umm 'Urayt' for the scorpion. And what the author mentioned of the definition of proper noun is specific to the first of these two divisions. So either he makes it included in the univocal or the equivocal, or he does not include it in either, as he did not include it in the definition of proper noun. Both are invalid. As for the first: it is against the convention of the people. As for the second: it necessitates an intermediary in the exclusive enumeration. An excuse may be offered that such things are not proper nouns in the terminology of logic, even if they are proper nouns in the terminology of Arabic grammarians. That is because their consideration is only of meanings, and their meanings are universal, unlike the Arabic grammarians, for their consideration is of verbal rulings. So when the rulings of proper nouns applied to them—such as not allowing the definite article to enter upon them, being described as definite, and the indefinite noun after them being in the accusative case as a circumstantial accusative, and other things—they ruled that they are proper nouns. So this is a case of difference in terminologies due to difference in perspectives. This. And it may be said: Generic proper nouns are posited for the unified essence under the condition of mental unity, so they are included in the definition of proper noun from the perspective of logicians as well, so there is no problem. So ponder, for this station is difficult for some people because it is a place where feet slip.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) defending and clarifying the definition of ‘ilm (proper noun) in logic. The discussion begins with an objection: it is said that the term ‘ilm is used both for a universal essence existing in individuals and for the individuals themselves. The commentator responds by denying exclusivity, stating that it can be used for both, even if metaphorically for individuals. Then, in a note from Mirza Muhammad Ali, he addresses a specific point about the author's definition excluding names of indication (asma' al-ishara) and similar terms. He explains that if we take the author's phrase 'with its individualized particularity' as part of the definition, then names of indication would be excluded, but the term 'positing' (wad') would become redundant. He offers a resolution by interpreting the definition as using 'meaning' (ma'na) in a general sense that includes both real and metaphorical usage. Following this, two issues are examined. First, indefinite nouns (nakira) intended for a particular individual (like 'a man' meaning a specific man) appear to have a meaning that is a particular by positing, yet they are not considered proper nouns. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between truth on multiplicity via inclusion (shumul) versus substitution (badaliyya). An indefinite noun like 'a man' is not truly predicated of many individuals simultaneously; it is only ambiguous, so it remains particular in the logical sense. He illustrates this with the example of a phantom seen from afar, which is a real particular even if the intellect considers multiple possibilities. Second, the possibility of a proper noun being universal (like generic proper nouns) is discussed. The author's definition is said to apply only to personal proper nouns (like Zayd). If we try to include generic proper nouns (like 'Usama' for lion) under the definition, we encounter problems: either they would be univocal or equivocal, which contradicts established terminology, or they would be excluded entirely, introducing an intermediary in the exclusive enumeration (i.e., the division of words into particular and universal would have a third category). An excuse is presented: generic proper nouns are not considered proper nouns in the logical tradition, only in Arabic grammar. This is because logicians focus on meaning, which is universal, while grammarians focus on verbal rulings like not accepting the definite article. Thus, there is a discrepancy in terminologies. However, another opinion holds that generic proper nouns are posited for a unified essence under mental unity, so they can be considered proper nouns even by logicians, thus avoiding the problem. The passage concludes by cautioning that this matter is difficult and a slippery slope.

النكرة
اسماء الاشارة
علم الجنس

Univocal and Equivocal: Definitions, Objection, and Answer
Definitions, objection, and answer on univocal/equivocal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between univocal (mutawāṭi') and equivocal (mushakkik) terms using the criterion of nafs al-amr rather than mental assumption, and explain why the objection about hypothetical individuals fails.

Core Idea
Univocal and equivocal are defined by real predication, not by actual existence of individuals.

In Islamic logic, terms are classified as univocal (mutawāṭi') or equivocal (mushakkik) based on how they apply to their individuals. But a key question arises: should this equality or difference be judged by actual reality (nafs al-amr) or by mental assumption? This passage from a hashia on a sharh of logic presents a precise objection and answer that clarifies the meaning of 'real state of affairs' and saves the established classifications of terms like human and existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternatives
The objection presents two possibilities: either the equality/difference is based on the real state of affairs (nafs al-amr) or on mental assumption (fard 'aqli). Under each, a problem arises. Read carefully to see what those problems are.
2
Trace the objection's reasoning
Under the first alternative (nafs al-amr), hypothetical universals would be neither univocal nor equivocal, but they clearly must be one or the other. Under the second alternative (mental assumption), two established rules would be violated: (1) the definitive judgment that human is univocal and existence is equivocal, and (2) the rule that equivocal is not essential. Understand how the objection uses these consequences.
The two rules are from the standard logical tradition.
3
Analyze the answer's resolution
The answer chooses the first alternative and reinterprets it: 'by nafs al-amr' means that if the term had actual individuals, the predication would be equal or different accordingly. This meaning does not depend on actual individuals existing. Thus hypothetical universals are still classifiable.
4
Apply the clarified definitions
Now test with examples: 'human' (univocal, individuals external), 'partner of God' (equivocal, individuals mental), 'necessary existence' (equivocal, individuals mixed). Confirm they are correctly classified under the refined definition.

مختلفاً بالنسبة الى افراده فهو مشكك سواء كان ذلك هو الانسان او الوجود او غيرهما . الثانية أنهم حكموا بان المشكك لا يكون ذاتياً لما تحته من الافراد مستدلين بان الذاتى لا يختلف باختلاف الاشخاص كما بين في موضعه فلو كان التشكيك هو الاختلاف الحاصل بالنسبة الى الافراد بحسب الفرض العقلى لما صبح ذلك الحكم منهم كما هو ظاهر اذلاريب في ان هذا المعنى ربما وجد في العقليات ضرورة ان للعقل ان يفرض الذاتي ايضاً مختلفاً و ان كان هذا الفرض منه غير مطابق للواقع هذا. ويمكن ان يجاب بانا نختار الشق الأول ونقول ان كون الافراد فرضية لاينا في كون التساوى او الاختلاف نفس امرى فان معنى التساوى او الاختلاف بحسب نفس الامر انه لو كانت للشيء افراد محققة كان صدق هذا الشيء لهذه الافراد متساوياً أو مختلفاً بحسب نفس الامر والواقع ولاريب ان هذا المعنى لا يتوقف على تحقق الافراد في نفس الامر والواقع كما هو ظاهر لمن تأمل. وقد ظهر مما تلونا عليك ان الافراد فى المتواطى والمشكك اعم من ان تكون خارجية كالانسان او ذهنية كشريك البارى تعالى او بعضها خارجياً وبعضها ذهنياً كواجب الوجود والشمس فاحفظ هذه الجملة. ثم انما سمى المتواطى متواطياً لان افراده متوافقة فى صدقه عليها والتواطى التوافق والمشكك مشككاً، لان افراده متوافقة فى اصل المعنى متفاوتة باحد الوجوه الاتية آنفاً فمن نظر اليه ان لاحظه من جهة توافق افراده في صدقه عليها تو هم انه متواط و ان لاحظه من جهة اختلافها فيه باحد الوجوه الآتية توهم انه لفظ مشترك بين معان متعددة مختلفة فهو يشكك الناظر هل هو متواط او مشترك ؟
His statement: 'i.e., the truth of this universal meaning to those individuals is equally' — an objection is raised here: if the equality considered in the truth of the univocal and the difference considered in the truth of the equivocal were both considered according to actual reality and the real state of affairs — so that the univocal is what is equal in its individuals in the real state of affairs, and the equivocal is what differs in its individuals in the ways mentioned in the coming note in the real state of affairs — then it would follow that hypothetical universals are neither univocal nor equivocal, because they have no individuals in the real state of affairs for equality or difference to occur. And if they were considered according to mental assumption — so that the univocal is what is equal in its individuals according to mental assumption, and the equivocal is what differs in its individuals according to mental assumption, whether it corresponds to reality or not — then it would entail the disruption of two established rules. The first: they have definitively judged that human (insan) with respect to its individuals is univocal because of equality of truth in them, and existence (wujud) with respect to its individuals is equivocal because of difference in truth in them. So if univocity and equivocity were according to mental assumption, then their definitive judgment that some universals like human are univocal and some like existence are equivocal would not be valid. Rather, they would have to say: 'Whatever the mind assumes to be equal in its individuals is univocal, and whatever the mind assumes to be different in its individuals is equivocal, whether it is human or existence or other.' The second: they have ruled that the equivocal is not essential (dhati) for the individuals under it, arguing that the essential does not differ with differences of individuals, as established in its place. So if equivocity were the difference occurring with respect to individuals according to mental assumption, that ruling would not be valid, as is apparent. There is no doubt that this meaning (difference) may be found in mental matters, for the intellect can assume even the essential to be different, even if this assumption is not in accordance with reality. This. And it can be answered: we choose the first alternative and say: the fact that the individuals are hypothetical does not prevent the equality or difference from being real (nafs amri). For the meaning of equality or difference according to the real state of affairs is that if the thing had actual individuals, the truth of this thing to those individuals would be equal or different according to the real state of affairs and actual reality. There is no doubt that this meaning does not depend on the actual existence of individuals in the real state of affairs and actual reality, as is apparent to one who reflects. And it has become clear from what we have recited to you that the individuals in the univocal and the equivocal are more general — they may be external like human, or mental like the partner of the Exalted, or some external and some mental like the Necessary Existence and the sun. So memorize this statement. Then, it is called univocal (mutawati') because its individuals agree in truth upon it — and 'tawatu' is agreement — and equivocal (mushakkik) because its individuals agree in the core meaning but differ in one of the ways to be mentioned shortly. So one who looks at it, if he observes it from the perspective of agreement of its individuals in truth upon it, he imagines it is univocal; and if he observes it from the perspective of difference in it in one of the coming ways, he imagines that it is a homonym shared among multiple different meanings. So it makes the viewer uncertain whether it is univocal or shared.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) defining the terms univocal (mutawāṭi’) and equivocal (mushakkik), raising an objection, and providing an answer. The discussion begins with a statement from the original text (possibly a sharh) that explains the meaning of 'equally' (ala al-sawiyya) in the definition of univocal. The commentator then raises an objection: if equality (taswiya) and difference (ikhtilaf) in truth of a term to its individuals are considered relative to actual reality (nafs al-amr), then hypothetical universals (kulliyat fardiyya) that have no actual individuals would be neither univocal nor equivocal, which is problematic because they clearly should be one or the other. Conversely, if equality and difference are considered relative to mental assumption (fard 'aqli), then two established rules would be violated. The first rule is that scholars have definitively judged that 'human' (insan) is univocal and 'existence' (wujud) is equivocal. If these judgments were based on mental assumption, they could not be certain; instead, one would have to say that anything the mind assumes to be equal is univocal, regardless of its nature. The second rule is that the equivocal is not essential (dhati) for its individuals because the essential does not vary. If equivocity were based on mental assumption, the intellect could assume difference even for essential attributes, undermining that rule. The answer chooses the first alternative (consideration by real state of affairs) and explains that hypothetical individuals do not prevent the equality or difference from being real in the sense that if actual individuals existed, the predication would be equal or different accordingly. This meaning does not depend on actual existence. The passage concludes that individuals in univocal and equivocal terms can be external, mental, or mixed, and gives examples: 'human' (external), 'partner of God' (mental, since God has no partner), and 'necessary existence' (external) versus 'sun' (external). Finally, it clarifies the naming: 'mutawati’' from tawatu' (agreement) because individuals agree in truth; 'mushakkik' because it causes doubt (shakk) whether it is univocal or shared (mushtarak), due to the combination of agreement in core meaning and difference in some aspect.

Key Terms

مشكك
نفس الامر
الفرض العقلى

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of mushakkik (equivocal)?
A) انسان (human)
B) وجود (existence)
C) حيوان (animal)
D) ناطق (rational)

Key Takeaway
The distinction between univocal and equivocal terms is based on real predication (nafs al-amr), not on mental assumption or actual existence of individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why 'insan' (human) is mutawāṭi' and 'wujud' (existence) is mushakkik, using the refined definition: equality/difference is by nafs al-amr, meaning if there were actual individuals, the predication would be equal or different accordingly. Then provide an example of a hypothetical universal (e.g., 'sharik al-Bari') and classify it using the same criterion.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
7 min
Exemplary Mention: Uliyya and Ulawiyya as Examples
Author defends the exemplary nature of the two categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mention of only two categories (firstness and precedence) does not limit differences, and identify where increase/decrease and intensity/weakness are subsumed.

Core Idea
The two categories are examples that cover all differences, with precedence subsuming quantitative and qualitative variations.

This passage resolves a potential objection: the author of the matn listed only two types of tafawut (difference) – firstness (uliyya) and precedence (ulawiyya). A critic might argue that other differences like increase/decrease and intensity/weakness are missing. The commentator explains that the listing is exemplary, not exhaustive, and those other differences actually fall under precedence. This shows how careful wording avoids logical objections in Islamic scholarly writing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
The matn mentions only two types of difference: firstness and precedence. Someone might object that this is incomplete.
2
Analyze the grammatical qualifier
The commentator points out that the word 'for example' (مثلاً) in the original phrase is a restriction, indicating an example.
The phrase is: 'that they differ in firstness or precedence for example'.
3
Understand the commentary chain
The author of al-Taqrib responds to the commentator's wording, confirming that firstness and precedence are not exhaustive; increase/decrease and intensity/weakness are included under precedence.
Al-Taqrib's author is cited to support the subsumption.
4
Distinguish the two subcategories
The passage then clarifies that firstness applies to quantities (e.g., one vs. two cubits), while precedence applies to qualities (e.g., whiteness in snow vs. ivory).
This shows how the two categories cover both quantitative and qualitative differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخفى ان قوله ان تفاوتت باولية او اولوية ليس مقولاً للقول في قوله من قوله كما يتوهم والالبق المبتداء و هو قوله: «غرضه» بلاخير بل هو خبر المبتداء و قوله : «مثلاً» قيد له و مقول القول المذكور غير مذكور فى اللفظ فالمعنى و غرضه من قوله ان تفاوتت باولية أو أولوية حيث ذكره مطلقا ان تفاوتت بأولية أو أولوية مثلاً اى مراده من هذا المطلق هو هذا المقيد. والحاصل ان ذكره لهما وحدهما ليس من جهة أن وجوه الاختلاف عنده منحصرة فيهما حتى يرد ان الاختلاف كما يكون بهما يكون بالزيادة والنقصان والشدة والضعف ايضاً بل ان ذكره لهما انما هو على سبيل التمثيل فلايرد عليه تشنیع. عندنا قال صاحب التقريب عند ما يشكل على قول المحشي: قوله: «وغرضه بقوله: ان تفاوتت باولية او اولوية (مثلاً بل غرضه التحقيق، فان الاولية والاولوية لايبقيان تفاوتاً من التفاوتات لا يشتملان عليه و ما ذكر من الزيادة والنقصان و الشدة والضعف داخل في قسم الأولوية. مثال الاول كالمقدار بالنسبة الى من ومنين والى ذراع و ذراعين ومثال الثاني كالبياض بالنسبة الى الثلج والعاج وفرق بينها بان الاول انما يستعمل في الكميات والثاني في الكيفيات وهيهنا كلام لا يسعها مقام.
It is not hidden that his saying: 'that they differ in firstness or precedence' is not the quoted part of the saying in his statement from his statement as is imagined, but the beginning is his saying: 'his purpose', rather it is the predicate of the subject, and his saying: 'for example' is a restriction for it, and the quoted part of the mentioned saying is not mentioned in the wording. So the meaning is: his purpose from his saying 'that they differ in firstness or precedence' when he mentioned it absolutely is 'that they differ in firstness or precedence for example', i.e., his intention from this absolute is this restricted. The conclusion is that his mentioning of them alone is not because the aspects of difference in his view are limited to them, such that it could be objected that difference occurs by them and also by increase and decrease and intensity and weakness. Rather, his mentioning of them is only by way of example, so no objection is directed at him. The author of al-Taqrib said when objecting to the commentator's statement: 'His statement: "And his purpose by his saying: 'that they differ in firstness or precedence' for example" but his purpose is verification, for firstness and precedence do not remain as differences among differences that do not include them, and what is mentioned of increase and decrease and intensity and weakness are included in the category of precedence. Example of the first is like magnitude in relation to one and two, and to a cubit and two cubits. Example of the second is like whiteness in relation to snow and ivory. The difference between them is that the first is used in quantities and the second in qualities. Here there is speech that the place does not accommodate.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the author's mention of only two forms of difference—firstness (uliyya) and precedence (ulawiyya)—is not meant as an exhaustive list. The commentator explains the grammatical structure to show that 'for example' (mathalan) qualifies the statement, indicating it is exemplary. The concern that other differences like increase/decrease (ziyada wa nuqsan) and intensity/weakness (shidda wa du'f) are not covered is addressed: they fall under the category of precedence (ulawiyya). The author of al-Taqrib confirms this, stating that those other differences are subsumed under precedence. Then examples are given: the first kind (firstness) applies to quantities (e.g., distances of one versus two cubits), while the second kind (precedence) applies to qualities (e.g., whiteness in snow versus ivory). Thus, the passage defends the author against the objection of incompleteness.

الشدة والضعف

الأولوية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are differences of increase/decrease and intensity/weakness related to the two categories (uliyya and ulawiyya)?
A) They are separate types not covered by either
B) They fall under firstness (uliyya)
C) They fall under precedence (ulawiyya)
D) They are examples of both categories equally

Key Takeaway
When a logician lists only two categories, look for qualifiers like 'for example' and consider whether other differences are subsumed under those categories, as seen here with precedence covering quantitative and qualitative variations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a passage from a Mantiq text (e.g., al-Risalah al-Shamsiyyah) where an author lists only two categories of something, and explain whether the passage uses 'for example' or similar qualifier. If not, how might a commentator defend the listing?
?
Construct a brief defense using the method of this passage: show that apparent omissions are actually subsumed under the listed categories, using an example from fiqh (e.g., the types of hukm taklifi often listed as five, but others like nadb and karaha are included under 'mandub' and 'makruh' respectively).
